cfrz10{_}9{_}*****   Title: Catherine Furze  Author: Mark Rutherford  Release Date: July, 2004  [EBook #6023] [Yes, we are more than one year ahead of schedule] [This file was first posted on October 20, 2002] [Most recently updated: October 20, 2002]  Edition: 10  Language: English  Character set encoding: ASCII  *** START OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK, CATHERINE FURZE ***     Transcribed from the 1913 Hodder and Stoughton edition by David Price, email ccx074@coventry.ac.uk     CATHERINE FURZE     CHAPTER I    It was a bright, hot, August Saturday in the market town of Eastthorpe, in the eastern Midlands, in the year 1840.
cfrz10{_}10{_}Eastthorpe lay about five miles on the western side of the Fens, in a very level country on the banks of a river, broad and deep, but with only just sufficient fall to enable its long-lingering waters to reach the sea.
cfrz10{_}11{_}It was an ancient market town, with a six-arched stone bridge, and with a High Street from which three or four smaller and narrower streets connected by courts and alleys diverged at right angles.
cfrz10{_}12{_}In the middle of the town was the church, an immense building, big enough to hold half Eastthorpe, and celebrated for its beautiful spire and its peal of eight bells.
cfrz10{_}13{_}Round the church lay the churchyard, fringed with huge elms, and in the Abbey Close, as it was called, which was the outer girdle of the churchyard on three sides, the fourth side of the square being the High Street, there lived in 1840 the principal doctor, the lawyer, the parson, and two aged gentlewomen with some property, who were daughters of one of the former partners in the bank, had been born in Eastthorpe, and had scarcely ever quitted it.
cfrz10{_}14{_}Here also were a young ladies' seminary and an ancient grammar school for the education of forty boys, sons of freemen of the town.
cfrz10{_}15{_}The houses in the Close were not of the same class as the rest; they were mostly old red brick, with white sashes, and they all had gardens, long, narrow, and shady, which, on the south side of the Close, ran down to the river.
cfrz10{_}16{_}One of these houses was even older, black-timbered, gabled, plastered, the sole remains, saving the church, of Eastthorpe as it was in the reign of Henry the Eighth.
cfrz10{_}17{_}Just beyond the church, going from the bridge, the High Street was so wide that the houses on either side were separated by a space of over two hundred feet.
cfrz10{_}18{_}This elongated space was the market-place.
cfrz10{_}19{_}In the centre was the Moot Hall, a quaint little building, supported on oak pillars, and in the shelter underneath the farmers assembled on market day.
cfrz10{_}20{_}All round the Moot Hall, and extending far up and down the street, were cattle-pens and sheep-pens, which were never removed.
cfrz10{_}21{_}Most of the shops were still bow-windowed, with small panes of glass, but the first innovation, indicative of the new era at hand, had just been made.
cfrz10{_}22{_}The druggist, as a man of science and advanced ideas, had replaced his bow-window with plate-glass, had put a cornice over it, had stuccoed his bricks, and had erected a kind of balustrade of stucco, so as to hide as much as possible the attic windows, which looked over, meekly protesting.
cfrz10{_}23{_}Nearly opposite the Moot Hall was the Bell Inn, the principal inn in the town.
cfrz10{_}24{_}There were other inns, respectable enough, such as the Bull, a little higher up, patronised by the smaller commercial travellers and farmers, but the entrance passage to the Bull had sand on the floor, and carriers made it a house of call.
cfrz10{_}25{_}To the Bell the two coaches came which went through Eastthorpe, and there they changed horses.
cfrz10{_}26{_}Both the Bull and the Bell had market dinners, but at the Bell the charge was three-and-sixpence; sherry was often drunk, and there the steward to the Honourable Mr. Eaton, the principal landowner, always met the tenants.
cfrz10{_}27{_}The Bell was Tory and the Bull was Whig, but no stranger of respectability, Whig or Tory, visiting Eastthorpe could possibly hesitate about going to the Bell, with its large gilded device projecting over the pathway, with its broad archway at the side always freshly gravelled, and its handsome balcony on the first floor, from which the Tory county candidates, during election times, addressed the free and independent electors and cattle.
cfrz10{_}28{_}Eastthorpe was a malting town, and down by the water were two or three large malthouses.
cfrz10{_}29{_}The view from the bridge was not particularly picturesque, but it was pleasant, especially in summer, when the wind was south-west.
cfrz10{_}30{_}The malthouses and their cowls, the wharves and the gaily painted sailing barges alongside, the fringe of slanting willows turning the silver-gray sides of their foliage towards the breeze, the island in the middle of the river with bigger willows, the large expanse of sky, the soft clouds distinct in form almost to the far distant horizon, and, looking eastwards, the illimitable distance towards the fens and the sea--all this made up a landscape, more suitable perhaps to some persons than rock or waterfall, although no picture had ever been painted of it, and nobody had ever come to see it.
cfrz10{_}31{_}Such was Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}32{_}For hundreds of years had the shadow of St. Mary's swept slowly over the roofs underneath it, and, of all those years, scarcely a line of its history survived, save what was written in the churchyard or in the church registers.
cfrz10{_}33{_}The town had stood for the Parliament in the days of the Civil War, and there had been a skirmish in the place; but who fought in it, who were killed in it, and what the result was, nobody knew.
cfrz10{_}34{_}Half a dozen old skulls of much earlier date and of great size were once found in a gravel pit two miles away, and were the subject of much talk, some taking them for Romans, some for Britons, some for Saxons, and some for Danes.
cfrz10{_}35{_}As it was impossible to be sure if they were Christian, they could not be put in consecrated ground; they were therefore included in an auction of dead and live stock, and were bought by the doctor.
cfrz10{_}36{_}Surnames survived in Eastthorpe with singular pertinacity, for it was remote from the world, but what was the relationship between the scores of Thaxtons, for example, whose deaths were inscribed on the tombstones, some of them all awry and weather-worn, and the Thaxtons of 1840, no living Thaxton could tell, every spiritual trace of them having disappeared more utterly than their bones.
cfrz10{_}37{_}Their bones, indeed, did not disappear, and were a source of much trouble to the sexton, for in digging a new grave they came up to the surface in quantities, and had to be shovelled in and covered up again, so that the bodily remains of successive generations were jumbled together, and Puritan and Georgian Thaxtons were mixed promiscuously with their descendants.
cfrz10{_}38{_}Nevertheless, Eastthorpe had really had a history.
cfrz10{_}39{_}It had known victory and defeat, love, hatred, intrigue, hope, despair, and all the passions, just as Elizabeth, King Charles, Cromwell, and Queen Anne knew them, but they were not recorded.
cfrz10{_}40{_}It was a bright, hot, August Saturday, as we have said, and it was market day.
cfrz10{_}41{_}Furthermore, it was half-past two in the afternoon, and the guests at Mr. Furze's had just finished their dinner.
cfrz10{_}42{_}Mr. Furze was the largest ironmonger in Eastthorpe, and sold not only ironmongery, but ploughs and all kinds of agricultural implements.
cfrz10{_}43{_}At the back of the shop was a small foundry where all the foundry work for miles round Eastthorpe was done.
cfrz10{_}44{_}It was Mr. Furze's practice always to keep a kind of open house on Saturday, and on this particular day, at half-past two, Mr. Bellamy, Mr. Chandler, Mr. Gosford, and Mr. Furze were drinking their whiskey-and-water and smoking their pipes in Mr. Furze's parlour.
cfrz10{_}45{_}The first three were well-to-do farmers, and with them the whiskey-and-water was not a pretence.
cfrz10{_}46{_}Mr. Furze was a tradesman, and of a different build.
cfrz10{_}47{_}Strong tobacco and whiskey at that hour and in that heat were rather too much for him, and he played with his pipe and drank very slowly.
cfrz10{_}48{_}The conversation had subsided for a while under the influence of the beef, Yorkshire pudding, beer, and spirits, when Mr. Bellamy observed -  "Old Bartlett's widow still a-livin' up at the Croft?"
cfrz10{_}49{_}"Yes," said Mr. Gosford, after filling his pipe again and pausing for at least a minute, "Bartlett's dead."
cfrz10{_}50{_}"Bartlett wur a slow-coach," observed Mr. Chandler, after another pause of a minute, "so wur his mare.
cfrz10{_}51{_}I mind me I wur behind his mare about five years ago last Michaelmas, and I wur well-nigh perished.
cfrz10{_}52{_}I wur a-goin' to give her a poke with my stick, and old Bartlett says, 'Doan't hit her, doan't hit her; yer can't alter her.'"
cfrz10{_}53{_}The three worthy farmers roared with laughter, Mr. Furze smiling gently.
cfrz10{_}54{_}"That was a good 'un," said Mr. Bellamy.
cfrz10{_}55{_}"Ah," replied Chandler, "I mind that as well as if it wur yesterday."
cfrz10{_}56{_}Mr. Bellamy at this point had to leave, and Mr. Furze was obliged to attend to his shop.
cfrz10{_}57{_}Gosford and Chandler, however, remained, and Gosford continued the subject of Bartlett's widow.
cfrz10{_}58{_}"What's she a-stayin' on for up there?"
cfrz10{_}59{_}"Old Bartlett's left her a goodish bit."
cfrz10{_}60{_}"She wur younger than he."
cfrz10{_}61{_}A dead silence of some minutes.
cfrz10{_}62{_}"She ain't a-goin' to take the Croft on herself," observed Gosford.
cfrz10{_}63{_}"Them beasts of the squire's," replied Chandler, "fetched a goodish lot.
cfrz10{_}64{_}Scaled just over ninety stone apiece."
cfrz10{_}65{_}"Why doan't you go in for the widow, Chandler?"
cfrz10{_}66{_}Mr. Chandler was a widower.
cfrz10{_}67{_}"Eh!"
cfrz10{_}68{_}(with a nasal tone and a smile)--"bit too much for me."
cfrz10{_}69{_}"Too much?
cfrz10{_}70{_}Why, there ain't above fourteen stone of her.
cfrz10{_}71{_}Keep yer warm o' nights up at your cold place."
cfrz10{_}72{_}Mr. Chandler took the pipe out of his mouth, put it inside the fender, compressed his lips, rubbed his chin, and looked up to the ceiling.
cfrz10{_}73{_}"Well, I must be a-goin'."
cfrz10{_}74{_}"I suppose I must too," and they both went their ways, to meet again at tea-time.
cfrz10{_}75{_}At five punctually all had again assembled, the additions to the party being Mrs. Furze and her daughter Catharine, a young woman of nineteen.
cfrz10{_}76{_}Mrs. Furze was not an Eastthorpe lady; she came from Cambridge, and Mr. Furze had first seen her when she was on a visit in Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}77{_}Her father was a draper in Cambridge, which was not only a much bigger place than Eastthorpe, but had a university, and Mrs. Furze talked about the university familiarly, so that, although her education had been slender, a university flavour clung to her, and the farmers round Eastthorpe would have been quite unable to determine the difference between her and a senior wrangler, if they had known what a senior wrangler was.
cfrz10{_}78{_}"Ha," observed Mr. Gosford, when they were seated, "I wur sayin', Mrs. Furze, to Chandler as he ought to go in for old Bartlett's widow.
cfrz10{_}79{_}Now what do YOU think?
cfrz10{_}80{_}Wouldn't they make a pretty pair?"
cfrz10{_}81{_}and he twisted Chandler's shoulders round a little till he faced Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}82{_}"Don't you be a fool, Gosford," said Chandler in good temper, but as he disengaged himself, he upset his tea on Mrs. Furze's carpet.
cfrz10{_}83{_}"Really, Mr. Gosford," replied Mrs. Furze, with some dignity and asperity, "I am no judge in such matters.
cfrz10{_}84{_}They are best left to the persons concerned."
cfrz10{_}85{_}"No offence, ma'am, no offence."
cfrz10{_}86{_}Mrs. Furze was not quite a favourite with her husband's friends, and he knew it, but he was extremely anxious that their dislike to her should not damage his business relationships with them.
cfrz10{_}87{_}So he endeavoured to act as mediator.
cfrz10{_}88{_}"No doubt, my dear, no doubt, but at the same time there is no reason why Mr. Gosford should not make any suggestion which may be to our friend Chandler's advantage,"  But Mr. Gosford was checked and did not pursue the subject.
cfrz10{_}89{_}Catharine sat next to him.
cfrz10{_}90{_}"Mr. Gosford, when may I come to Moat Farm again?"
cfrz10{_}91{_}"Lord, my dear, whenever you like you know that.
cfrz10{_}92{_}Me and Mrs. G. is always glad to see you.
cfrz10{_}93{_}WHENever you please," and Mr. Gosford instantly recovered the good-humour which Mrs. Furze had suppressed.
cfrz10{_}94{_}"Don't forget us," chimed in Mr. Bellamy.
cfrz10{_}95{_}"We'll turn out your room and store apples in it if you don't use it oftener."
cfrz10{_}96{_}"Now, Mr. Bellamy," said Catharine, holding up her finger at him, "you'll be sick of me at last.
cfrz10{_}97{_}You've forgotten when I had that bad cold at your house, and was in bed there for a week, and what a bother I was to Mrs.
cfrz10{_}98{_}Bellamy."
cfrz10{_}99{_}"Bother!"
cfrz10{_}100{_}cried Bellamy--"bother!
cfrz10{_}101{_}Lord have mercy on us!
cfrz10{_}102{_}why the missus was sayin' when you talked about bother, my missus says, 'I'd sooner have Catharine here, and me have tea up there with her, notwithstanding there must be a fire upstairs and I've had to send Lucy to the infirmary with a whitlow on her thumb--yes, I would, than be at a many tea-parties I know.'"
cfrz10{_}103{_}Mrs. Furze gave elaborate tea-parties, and was uncomfortably uncertain whether or not the shaft was intended for her.
cfrz10{_}104{_}"My dear Catharine, I shall be delighted if you go either to Mr. Gosford's or to Mr. Bellamy's, but you must consider your wardrobe a little.
cfrz10{_}105{_}You will remember that the last time on each occasion a dress was torn in pieces."
cfrz10{_}106{_}"But, mother, are not dresses intended to keep thorns from our legs; or, at any rate, isn't that ONE reason why we wear them?"
cfrz10{_}107{_}"Suppose it to be so, my dear, there is no reason why you should plunge about in thorns."
cfrz10{_}108{_}Catharine had a provoking way of saving "yes" or "no" when she wished to terminate a controversy.
cfrz10{_}109{_}She stated her own opinion, and then, if objection was raised, at least by some people, her father and mother included, she professed agreement by a simple monosyllable, either because she was lazy, or because she saw that there was no chance of further profit in the discussion.
cfrz10{_}110{_}It was irritating, because it was always clear she meant nothing.
cfrz10{_}111{_}At this instant a servant opened the door, and Alice, a curly brown retriever, squeezed herself in, and made straight for Catharine, putting her head on Catharine's lap.
cfrz10{_}112{_}"Catharine, Catharine!"
cfrz10{_}113{_}cried her mother, with a little scream, "she's dripping wet.
cfrz10{_}114{_}Do pray, my child, think of the carpet."
cfrz10{_}115{_}But Catharine put her lips to Alice's face and kissed it deliberately, giving her a piece of cake.
cfrz10{_}116{_}"Mr. Gosford, my poor bitch has puppies--three of them--all as true as their mother, for we know the father."
cfrz10{_}117{_}"Ah!"
cfrz10{_}118{_}replied Gosford, "you're lucky, then, Miss Catharine, for dogs, especially in a town--"  Mrs. Furze at this moment hastily rang the bell, making an unusual clatter with the crockery:  Mr. Furze said the company must excuse him, and the three worthy farmers rose to take their departure.
cfrz10{_}119{_}CHAPTER II    It was Mr. Furze's custom on Sunday to go to sleep for an hour between dinner and tea upstairs in what was called the drawing-room, while Mrs. Furze sat and read, or said she read, a religious book.
cfrz10{_}120{_}On hot summer afternoons Mr. Furze always took off his coat before he had his nap, and sometimes divested himself of his waistcoat.
cfrz10{_}121{_}When the coat and waistcoat were taken off, Mrs. Furze invariably drew down the blinds.
cfrz10{_}122{_}She had often remonstrated with her husband for appearing in his shirt-sleeves, and objected to the neighbours seeing him in this costume.
cfrz10{_}123{_}There was a sofa in the room, but it was horsehair, with high ends both alike, not comfortable, which were covered with curious complications called antimacassars, that slipped off directly they were touched, so that anybody who leaned upon them was engaged continually in warfare with them, picking them up from the floor or spreading them out again.
cfrz10{_}124{_}There was also an easy chair, but it was not easy, for it matched the sofa in horsehair, and was so ingeniously contrived, that directly a person placed himself in it, it gently shot him forwards.
cfrz10{_}125{_}Furthermore, it had special antimacassars, which were a work of art, and Mrs. Furze had warned Mr. Furze off them.
cfrz10{_}126{_}"He would ruin them," she said, "if he put his head upon them."
cfrz10{_}127{_}So a windsor chair with a high back was always carried by Mr. Furze upstairs after dinner, together with a common kitchen chair, and on these he slumbered.
cfrz10{_}128{_}The room was never used, save on Sundays and when Mrs. Furze gave a tea-party.
cfrz10{_}129{_}It overlooked the market-place, and, although on a Sunday afternoon the High Street was almost completely silent, Mrs. Furze liked to sit so near the window that she could peep out at the edge of the blind when she was not dozing.
cfrz10{_}130{_}It is true no master nor mistress ever stirred at that hour, but every now and then a maidservant could be seen, and she was better than nothing for the purpose of criticism.
cfrz10{_}131{_}A round table stood in the middle of the room with a pink vase on it containing artificial flowers, and on the mantelpiece were two other pink vases and two great shells.
cfrz10{_}132{_}Over the mantelpiece was a portrait of His Majesty King George the Fourth in his robes, and exactly opposite was a picture of the Virgin Mary, which was old and valuable.
cfrz10{_}133{_}Mr. Furze bought it at a sale with some other things, and did not quite like it.
cfrz10{_}134{_}It savoured of Popery, which he could not abide; but the parson one day saw it and told Mrs. Furze it was worth something; whereupon she put it in a new maple frame, and had it hung in a place of honour second to that occupied by King George, and so arranged that he and the Virgin were always looking at one another.
cfrz10{_}135{_}On the other side of the room were a likeness of Mr. Eaton in hunting array, with the dogs, and a mezzotint of the Deluge.
cfrz10{_}136{_}Mr. Furze had just awaked on the Sunday afternoon following the day of which the history is partly given in the first chapter.
cfrz10{_}137{_}"My dear," said his wife, "I have been thinking a good deal of Catharine.
cfrz10{_}138{_}She is not quite what I could wish."
cfrz10{_}139{_}"No," replied Mr. Furze, with a yawn.
cfrz10{_}140{_}"To begin with, she uses bad language.
cfrz10{_}141{_}I was really quite shocked yesterday to hear the extremely vulgar word, almost--almost,--I do not know what to call it--profane, I may say, which she applied to her dog when talking of it to Mr. Gosford.
cfrz10{_}142{_}Then she goes in the foundry; and I firmly believe that all the money which has been spent on her music is utterly thrown away."
cfrz10{_}143{_}"The thing is--what is to be done?"
cfrz10{_}144{_}"Now, I have a plan."
cfrz10{_}145{_}In order to make Mrs. Furze's plan fully intelligible, it may be as well to explain that, up to the year 1840, the tradesmen of Eastthorpe had lived at their shops.
cfrz10{_}146{_}But a year or two before that date some houses had been built at the north end of the town and called "The Terrace."
cfrz10{_}147{_}A new doctor had taken one, the brewer another, and a third had been taken by the grocer, a man reputed to be very well off, who not only did a large retail business, but supplied the small shops in the villages round.
cfrz10{_}148{_}"Well, my dear, what is your plan?"
cfrz10{_}149{_}"Your connection is extending, and you want more room.
cfrz10{_}150{_}Now, why should you not move to the Terrace?
cfrz10{_}151{_}If we were to go there, Catharine would be withdrawn from the society in which she at present mixes.
cfrz10{_}152{_}You could not continue to give market dinners, and gradually her acquaintance with the persons whom you now invite would cease.
cfrz10{_}153{_}I believe, too, that if we were in the Terrace Mrs. Colston would call on us.
cfrz10{_}154{_}As the wife of a brewer, she cannot do so now.
cfrz10{_}155{_}Then there is just another thing which has been on my mind for a long time.
cfrz10{_}156{_}It is settled that Mr. Jennings is to leave, for he has accepted an invitation from the cause at Ely.
cfrz10{_}157{_}I do not think we shall like anybody after Mr. Jennings, and it would be a good opportunity for us to exchange the chapel for the church.
cfrz10{_}158{_}We have attended the chapel regularly, but I have always felt a kind of prejudice there against us, or at least against myself, and there is no denying that the people who go to church are vastly more genteel, and so are the service and everything about it--the vespers--the bells--somehow there is a respectability in it."
cfrz10{_}159{_}Mr. Furze was silent.
cfrz10{_}160{_}At last he said, "It is a very serious matter.
cfrz10{_}161{_}I must consider it in all its bearings."
cfrz10{_}162{_}It WAS a serious matter, and he did consider it--but not in all its bearings, for he did nothing but think about it, so that it enveloped him, and he could not put himself at such a distance that he could see its real shape.
cfrz10{_}163{_}He was now well over fifty and was the kind of person with whom habits become firmly fixed.
cfrz10{_}164{_}He was fixed even in his dress.
cfrz10{_}165{_}He always wore a white neckcloth, and his shirt was frilled--fashions which were already beginning to die out in Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}166{_}His manner of life was most regular:  breakfast at eight, dinner at one, tea at five, supper at nine with a pipe afterwards, was his unvarying round.
cfrz10{_}167{_}He never left Eastthorpe for a holiday, and read no books of any kind.
cfrz10{_}168{_}He was a most respectable member of a Dissenting congregation, but he was not a member of the church, and was never seen at the week-night services or the prayer- meetings.
cfrz10{_}169{_}He went through the ceremony of family worship morning and evening, but he did not pray extempore, as did the elect, and contented himself with reading prayers from a book called "Family Devotions."
cfrz10{_}170{_}The days were over for Eastthorpe when a man like Mr. Furze could be denounced, a man who paid his pew-rent regularly, and contributed to the missionary societies.
cfrz10{_}171{_}The days were over when any expostulations could be addressed to him, or any attempts made to bring him within the fold, and Mr. Jennings therefore called on him, and religion was not mentioned.
cfrz10{_}172{_}It may seem extraordinary that, without convictions based on any reasoning process, Mr. Furze's outward existence should have been so correct and so moral.
cfrz10{_}173{_}He had passed through the usually stormy period of youth without censure.
cfrz10{_}174{_}It is true he was married young, but before his marriage nobody had ever heard a syllable against him, and, after marriage, he never drank a drop too much, and never was guilty of a single dishonest action.
cfrz10{_}175{_}Day after day passed by like all preceding days, in unbroken, level succession, without even the excitement of meeting-house emotion.
cfrz10{_}176{_}Naturally, therefore, his wife's proposals made him uneasy, and even alarmed him.
cfrz10{_}177{_}He shrank from them unconsciously, and yet his aversion was perfectly wise; more so, perhaps, than any action for which he could have assigned a definite motive.
cfrz10{_}178{_}With men like Mr. Furze the unconscious reason, which is partly a direction by past and forgotten experiences, and partly instinct, is often more to be trusted than any mental operation, strictly so-called.
cfrz10{_}179{_}An attempt to use the mind actively on subjects which are too large, or with which it has not been accustomed to deal, is pretty nearly sure to mislead.
cfrz10{_}180{_}He knew, or it knew, whatever we like to call it, that to break him from his surroundings meant that he himself was to be broken, for they were a part of him.
cfrz10{_}181{_}His wife attacked him again the next day.
cfrz10{_}182{_}She was bent upon moving, and it is only fair to her to say that she did really wish to go for Catharine's sake.
cfrz10{_}183{_}She loved the child in her own way, but she also wanted to go for many other reasons.
cfrz10{_}184{_}"Well, my dear, what have you to say to my little scheme?"
cfrz10{_}185{_}"How about my dinner and tea?"
cfrz10{_}186{_}"Come home to the Terrace.
cfrz10{_}187{_}How far is it?"
cfrz10{_}188{_}"Ten minutes' walk."
cfrz10{_}189{_}"An hour every day, in all weathers; and then there's the expense."
cfrz10{_}190{_}"As to the expense, I am certain we should save in the long run, because you would not be expected to be continually asking people to meals."
cfrz10{_}191{_}"I am afraid that the business might suffer."
cfrz10{_}192{_}"Nonsense!
cfrz10{_}193{_}In what way, my dear?
cfrz10{_}194{_}Your attention will be more fixed upon it than it can be with the parlour always behind you."
cfrz10{_}195{_}There was something in that, and Mr. Furze was perplexed.
cfrz10{_}196{_}He was not sufficiently well educated to know that something, and a great deal, too, can be said for anything, and he had not arrived at that callousness to argument which is the last result of culture.
cfrz10{_}197{_}"Yes, but I was thinking that perhaps if we leave off chapel and go to church some of our customers may not like it."
cfrz10{_}198{_}"Now, my good man, Furze, why you know you have as many customers who go to church as to chapel."
cfrz10{_}199{_}"Ah!
cfrz10{_}200{_}but those who go to chapel may drop off."
cfrz10{_}201{_}"Why should they?
cfrz10{_}202{_}We have plenty of customers who go to church.
cfrz10{_}203{_}They don't leave us because we are Dissenters, and, as there are five times as many church people as Dissenters, your connection will be extended."
cfrz10{_}204{_}Mrs. Furze was unanswerable, but her poor husband, after all, was right.
cfrz10{_}205{_}The change, when it took place, did not bring more people to the shop, and some left who were in the habit of coming.
cfrz10{_}206{_}His dumb, dull presentiment was a prophecy, and his wife's logic was nothing but words.
cfrz10{_}207{_}"Then there are all the rooms here; what shall we do with them?"
cfrz10{_}208{_}"I have told you; you want more space.
cfrz10{_}209{_}Besides, you do not make half enough show.
cfrz10{_}210{_}You ought to go with the times.
cfrz10{_}211{_}Why, at Cross's at Cambridge their upstairs windows are hung full of spades and hoes and such things, and you can see it is business up to the garret.
cfrz10{_}212{_}I should turn the parlour into a counting-house.
cfrz10{_}213{_}It isn't the proper thing for you to be standing always at that pokey little desk at the end of the counter with a pen behind your ear.
cfrz10{_}214{_}Turn the parlour, I say, into a counting-house, and come out when Tom finds it necessary to call you.
cfrz10{_}215{_}That makes a much better impression.
cfrz10{_}216{_}The rooms above the drawing-room might be used for lighter goods, so as not to weight the floors too much."
cfrz10{_}217{_}Mr. Furze was not sentimental, but he shuddered.
cfrz10{_}218{_}In the big front bedroom his father and he had been born.
cfrz10{_}219{_}The first thing he could remember was having measles there, and watching day by day, when he was a little better, what went on in the street below.
cfrz10{_}220{_}His brothers and sisters were also born there.
cfrz10{_}221{_}He remembered how his mother was shut up there, and he was not allowed to enter; how, when he tried the door, Nurse Judkins came and said he must be a good boy and go away, and how he heard a little cry, and was told he had a new sister, and he wondered how she got in.
cfrz10{_}222{_}In that room his father had died.
cfrz10{_}223{_}He was very ill for a long time, and again Nurse Judkins came.
cfrz10{_}224{_}He sat up with his father there night after night, and heard the church clock sound all the hours as the sick man lay waiting for his last.
cfrz10{_}225{_}He rallied towards the end, and, being very pious, he made his son sit down by the bedside and read to him the ninety- first Psalm.
cfrz10{_}226{_}He then blessed his boy in that very room, and five minutes afterwards he had rushed from it, choked with sobbing when the last breath was drawn.
cfrz10{_}227{_}He did not relish the thought of taking down the old four-post bedstead and putting rakes and shovels in its place, but all he could say was -  "I don't quite fall in with it."
cfrz10{_}228{_}"WHY not?
cfrz10{_}229{_}Now, my dear, I will make a bargain with you.
cfrz10{_}230{_}If you can assign a good reason, I will give it up; but, if you cannot, then, of course, we ought to go, because _I_ have plenty of reasons for going.
cfrz10{_}231{_}Nothing can be fairer than that."
cfrz10{_}232{_}Mr. Furze was not quite clear about the "ought," although it was so fair, but he was mute, and, after a pause, went into his shop.
cfrz10{_}233{_}An accident decided the question.
cfrz10{_}234{_}Catharine was the lightest sleeper in the house, notwithstanding her youth.
cfrz10{_}235{_}Two nights after this controversy she awoke suddenly and smelt something burning.
cfrz10{_}236{_}She jumped out of bed, flung her dressing-gown over her, opened her door, and found the landing full of smoke.
cfrz10{_}237{_}Without a moment's hesitation she rushed out and roused her parents.
cfrz10{_}238{_}They were both bewildered, and hesitated, ejaculating all sorts of useless things.
cfrz10{_}239{_}Catharine was impatient.
cfrz10{_}240{_}"Now, then, not a second; upstairs through Jane's bedroom, out into the gutter, and through Hopkins's attic.
cfrz10{_}241{_}You cannot go downstairs."
cfrz10{_}242{_}Still there was trembling and indecision.
cfrz10{_}243{_}"But the tin box," gasped Mr. Furze; "it is in the wardrobe.
cfrz10{_}244{_}I must take it."
cfrz10{_}245{_}Catharine replied by literally driving them before her.
cfrz10{_}246{_}They picked up the maid-servant, crept behind the high parapet, and were soon in safety.
cfrz10{_}247{_}By this time the smoke was pouring up thick and fast, although no flame had appeared.
cfrz10{_}248{_}Suddenly Catharine cried -  "But where is Tom?"
cfrz10{_}249{_}Tom was the assistant, and slept in an offset at the back.
cfrz10{_}250{_}Underneath him was the kitchen, and beyond was the lower offset of the scullery.
cfrz10{_}251{_}Catharine darted towards the window.
cfrz10{_}252{_}"Catharine!"
cfrz10{_}253{_}shrieked her mother, "where are you going?
cfrz10{_}254{_}You cannot; you are not dressed."
cfrz10{_}255{_}But she answered not a word, and had vanished before anybody could arrest her.
cfrz10{_}256{_}The smoke was worse, and almost suffocating, but she wrapped her face and nose in her woollen gown, and reached Tom's door.
cfrz10{_}257{_}He never slept with it fastened, and the amazed youth was awakened by a voice which he knew to be that of Miss Furze.
cfrz10{_}258{_}Escape by the way she had come was hopeless.
cfrz10{_}259{_}The staircase was now opaque.
cfrz10{_}260{_}Fortunately Tom's casement, instead of being in the side wall, was at the end, and the drop to the scullery roof was not above four feet.
cfrz10{_}261{_}Catharine reached it easily, and, Tom coming after her, helped her to scramble down into the yard.
cfrz10{_}262{_}The gate was unbarred, and in another minute they were safe with their neighbours.
cfrz10{_}263{_}The town was now stirring, and a fire-engine came, a machine which attended fires officially, and squirted on them officially, but was never known to do anything more, save to make the road sloppy.
cfrz10{_}264{_}The thick, brick party walls of the houses adjoining saved them, but Mr. Furze's house was gutted from top to bottom.
cfrz10{_}265{_}It was surrounded by a crowd the next day, which stared unceasingly.
cfrz10{_}266{_}The fire-engine still operated on the ashes, and a great steam and smother arose.
cfrz10{_}267{_}A charred oak beam hung where it had always hung, but the roof had disappeared entirely, and the walls of the old bedchamber, which had seen so much of sweetness and of sadness, of the mysteries of love, birth, and death, lay bare to the sky and the street.
cfrz10{_}268{_}CHAPTER III    The stone bridge was deeply recessed, and in each recess was a stone seat.
cfrz10{_}269{_}In the last recess but one, at the north end, and on the east side, there sat daily, some few years before 1840, a blind man, Michael Catchpole by name, selling shoelaces.
cfrz10{_}270{_}He originally came out of Suffolk, but he had lived in Eastthorpe ever since he was a boy, and had worked for Mr. Furze's father.
cfrz10{_}271{_}He was blinded by a splash of melted iron, and was suddenly left helpless, a widower with one boy, Tom, fifteen years old.
cfrz10{_}272{_}His employer, the present Mr. Furze, did nothing for him, save sending him two bottles of lotion which he had heard were good for the eyes, and Mike for a time was confounded.
cfrz10{_}273{_}His club helped him so long as he was actually suffering and confined to his house, but their pay did not last above six weeks.
cfrz10{_}274{_}In these six weeks Mike learned much.
cfrz10{_}275{_}He was brought face to face with a blank wall with the pursuer behind him-- an experience which teaches more than most books, and he was on the point of doing what some of us have been compelled to do--that is to say, to recognise that the worst is inevitable, throw up the arms and bravely yield.
cfrz10{_}276{_}But Mike also learned that this is not always necessary to a man with courage, and that very often escape lies in the last moment, the very last, when endurance seems no longer possible.
cfrz10{_}277{_}His deliverance did not burst upon him in rainbow colours out of the sky complete.
cfrz10{_}278{_}It was a very slow affair.
cfrz10{_}279{_}He heard that an old woman had died who lived in Parker's Alley and sold old clothes, old iron, bottles, and such like trash.
cfrz10{_}280{_}Parker's Alley was not very easy to find.
cfrz10{_}281{_}Going up High Street from the bridge, you first turned to the right through Cross Street, and then to the right again down Lock Lane, and out of Lock Lane ran the alley, a little narrow gutter of a place, dark and squalid, paved with round stones, through which slops of all kinds perpetually percolated, and gave forth on the cleanest days a faint and sickening odour.
cfrz10{_}282{_}Mike thought he could buy the stock for five shillings; the rent was only half a crown a week, and with the help of Tom, a remarkably sharp boy, who could tell him in what condition the goods were which were offered him for purchase, he hoped he could manage to make way.
cfrz10{_}283{_}It was a dreadful trial.
cfrz10{_}284{_}The old woman had lived amongst all her property.
cfrz10{_}285{_}She had eaten and drunk and slept amidst the dirty rags of Eastthorpe, but Mike could not.
cfrz10{_}286{_}Fortunately the cottage was at the end of the alley.
cfrz10{_}287{_}One window looked out on it, but the door was in a kind of indentation in it round the corner.
cfrz10{_}288{_}On the right-hand side of the door was the room looking into the alley, and this Mike made his shop; on the left was a little cupboard of a living-room.
cfrz10{_}289{_}He kept the shop window open, so that no customer came through the doorway, and he begged some scarlet geranium cuttings, which, in due time, bloomed into brilliant colour on his sitting-room window-sill, proclaiming that from their possessor hope and delight in life had not departed.
cfrz10{_}290{_}Alas!
cfrz10{_}291{_}the enterprise was a failure.
cfrz10{_}292{_}Mike was no hand at driving hard bargains, and frequently, when the Jew from Cambridge came round to sweep up what Mike had been unable to sell in the town, he found himself the worse for his purchases.
cfrz10{_}293{_}The unscalable wall was again in front of him, and his foe at his heels, closer than before, and raging for his blood.
cfrz10{_}294{_}He had gone out one morning, Tom leading him, and was passing the bank, when the cashier ran out.
cfrz10{_}295{_}Miss Foster, one of the maiden ladies who, it will be remembered, lived in the Abbey Close, had left a sovereign on the counter, and the cashier was exceedingly anxious to show his zeal by promptly returning it, for Miss Foster, it will also be remembered, was a daughter of a former partner in the bank, and still, as it was supposed, retained some interest in it.
cfrz10{_}296{_}She had gone too far, however, and the cashier could not venture to leave his post and follow her.
cfrz10{_}297{_}Knowing Mike and Tom perfectly well, he asked Mike to take the sovereign at once to the lady.
cfrz10{_}298{_}He promptly obeyed, and was in time to restore it to its owner before it was missed.
cfrz10{_}299{_}She was not particularly sensitive, but the sight of Mike and Tom standing at the hall entrance rather touched her, and she rewarded them with a shilling.
cfrz10{_}300{_}She was also pleased to inquire how Mike was getting on, and he briefly told her he did not get on in any way, save as the most unsuccessful happily get on, and so at last terminate their perplexities.
cfrz10{_}301{_}Miss Foster, although well-to-do, kept neither footman nor page, and a thought struck her.
cfrz10{_}302{_}She abhorred male servants, but it was very often inconvenient to send her maids on errands.
cfrz10{_}303{_}She therefore suggested to Mike that, if he and Tom could station themselves within call, they would not only be useful, but earn something of a livelihood.
cfrz10{_}304{_}The bank wanted an odd man occasionally, and she was sure that other people in the town would employ him.
cfrz10{_}305{_}Accordingly Mike and Tom one morning established themselves in the recess of the bridge, after having given notice to everybody who would be likely to assist them, and Mike set up a stock of boot-laces and shoe-laces of all kinds.
cfrz10{_}306{_}He thus managed to pick up a trifle.
cfrz10{_}307{_}He wrapped sacking round his legs to keep off the cold as he sat, and had for a footstool a box with straw in it.
cfrz10{_}308{_}He also rigged up a little awning on some sticks to keep off the sun and a shower, but of course when a storm came he was obliged to retreat.
cfrz10{_}309{_}He was then allowed a shelter in the bank.
cfrz10{_}310{_}The dust was a nuisance, for it was difficult to predict its capricious eddies, but he learnt its laws at last, and how to choose his station so as to diminish annoyance.
cfrz10{_}311{_}At first he was depressed at the thought of sitting still for so many hours with nothing to do, but he was not left to himself so much as he anticipated.
cfrz10{_}312{_}Two hours on the average were spent on errands; then there was his dinner:  Tom talked to him; people went by and said a word or two, and thus he discovered that a foreseen trouble may look impenetrable, but when we near it, or become immersed in it, it is often at least semi-transparent, and even sometimes admits a ray of sunshine.
cfrz10{_}313{_}Gradually his employment became sweet to him; he was a part of the town; he heard all its news; it was gentle within him; even the rough boys never molested him:  he tamed a black kitten to stay with him, and a red ribbon and a bell were provided for her by a friend.
cfrz10{_}314{_}When the kitten grew to be a cat she gravely watched under Mike's awning during his short absences with Tom, and not a soul ever touched the property she guarded.
cfrz10{_}315{_}Country folk who came to market on Saturday invariably saluted Mike with their kind country friendliness, and brought him all sorts of little gifts in the shape of fruit, and even of something more substantial when a pig was killed.
cfrz10{_}316{_}Thus with Mike time and the hour wore out the roughest day.
cfrz10{_}317{_}Two years had now passed since his accident, and Tom was about seventeen, when Miss Catharine crossed the bridge one fine Monday morning in June with the servant, and, as was her wont, stopped to have a word or two with her friend Mike.
cfrz10{_}318{_}Mike was always at his best on Monday morning.
cfrz10{_}319{_}Sunday was a day of rest, but he preferred Monday.
cfrz10{_}320{_}It was a delight to him to hear again the carts and the noise of feet, and to feel that the world was alive once more.
cfrz10{_}321{_}Sunday with its enforced quietude and inactivity was a burden to him.
cfrz10{_}322{_}"Well, Miss Catharine, how are you to-day?"
cfrz10{_}323{_}"How did you know I was Miss Catharine?
cfrz10{_}324{_}I hadn't spoken."
cfrz10{_}325{_}"Lord, Miss, I could tell.
cfrz10{_}326{_}Though it's only about two years since I lost my eyes, I could tell.
cfrz10{_}327{_}I can make out people's footsteps.
cfrz10{_}328{_}What a lovely morning!
cfrz10{_}329{_}What's going on now down below?"
cfrz10{_}330{_}Mike always took much interest in the wharves by the side of the river.
cfrz10{_}331{_}"Why, Barnes's big lighter is loading malt."
cfrz10{_}332{_}"Ah!
cfrz10{_}333{_}what, the new one with the yellow band round it!
cfrz10{_}334{_}that's a beautiful lighter, that is."
cfrz10{_}335{_}Mike had never seen it.
cfrz10{_}336{_}"What days do you dislike the most?
cfrz10{_}337{_}Foggy, damp, dull, dark days?"
cfrz10{_}338{_}These foggy, damp, dull, dark days were particularly distasteful to Catharine.
cfrz10{_}339{_}"No, Miss, I can't say I do, for, you know, I don't see them."
cfrz10{_}340{_}"Cold, bitter days?"
cfrz10{_}341{_}"They are a bit bad; but somehow I earn more money on cold days than on any other; how it is I don't know."
cfrz10{_}342{_}"I hate the dust."
cfrz10{_}343{_}"Ah now!
cfrz10{_}344{_}that IS unpleasant, but there again, Miss, I dodge it, and it's my belief that it wouldn't worry people half so much if they wouldn't look at it."
cfrz10{_}345{_}"How much have you earned this morning?"
cfrz10{_}346{_}"Not a penny yet, Miss, but it will come."
cfrz10{_}347{_}"I want two pairs of shoe-laces," and Miss Catharine, selecting two pairs, put down a fourpenny-piece, part of her pocket-money, twice the market value of the laces, and tripped over the bridge.
cfrz10{_}348{_}When she was at dinner with her father and mother that day she suddenly said -  "Father, didn't Mike Catchpole lose his sight in our foundry?"
cfrz10{_}349{_}"Yes."
cfrz10{_}350{_}"Have you been talking with him again?"
cfrz10{_}351{_}interposed Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}352{_}"I wish you would not stop on the bridge as you do.
cfrz10{_}353{_}It does not look nice for a girl like you to stay and gossip with Mike."
cfrz10{_}354{_}Catharine took no notice.
cfrz10{_}355{_}"Did you ever do anything for him?"
cfrz10{_}356{_}"What an odd question!"
cfrz10{_}357{_}again interposed Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}358{_}"What should we do?
cfrz10{_}359{_}There was his club besides, we sent him the lotion."
cfrz10{_}360{_}"Why cannot you take Tom as an apprentice?"
cfrz10{_}361{_}"Because," said her father, "there is nobody to pay the premium; you know what that means.
cfrz10{_}362{_}When a boy is bound apprentice the master has a sum of money for teaching him the business."
cfrz10{_}363{_}Catharine did not quite comprehend, inasmuch as there were two boys in the back shop who were paid wages, and who were learning their trade.
cfrz10{_}364{_}She was quiet for a few minutes, but presently returned to the charge.
cfrz10{_}365{_}"You MUST take Tom.
cfrz10{_}366{_}Why shouldn't you give him what you give the other boys?"
cfrz10{_}367{_}"Really, Catharine," said her mother, "why MUST?"
cfrz10{_}368{_}"Must!"
cfrz10{_}369{_}cried the little miss--"yes, I say MUST, because Mike lost his eyes for you, and you've done nothing for him; it's a shame."
cfrz10{_}370{_}"Catharine, Catharine!"
cfrz10{_}371{_}said her father, but in accordance with his usual habit he said nothing more, and the mother, also in accordance with her usual habit, collapsed.
cfrz10{_}372{_}Miss Catharine generally, even at that early age, carried all before her, much to her own detriment.
cfrz10{_}373{_}Her parents unfortunately were perpetually making a brief show of resistance and afterwards yielding.
cfrz10{_}374{_}Frequently they had no pretext for resistance, for Catharine was right and they were wrong.
cfrz10{_}375{_}Consequently the child grew up accustomed to see everything bend to her own will, and accustomed to believe that what she willed was in accordance with the will of the universe--not a healthy education, for the time is sure to come when a destiny which will not bend stands in the path before us, and we are convinced by the roughest processes that what we purpose is to a very small extent the purpose of Nature.
cfrz10{_}376{_}The shock then is serious, especially if the collision be postponed till mature years.
cfrz10{_}377{_}The parental opposition, such as it was, was worse than none, because it enabled her to feel her strength.
cfrz10{_}378{_}She continued to press her point, and not only was victorious, but was empowered to tell Mike that his son would be taken into the foundry and paid five shillings and sixpence a week--"a most special case," as Mr. Furze told Mike, in order to stimulate his gratitude.
cfrz10{_}379{_}Mike was now able to find his way about by himself, but before the date of the first chapter in this history he had left the bridge, and Tom supported him.
cfrz10{_}380{_}The morning after the fire beheld the Furze family at breakfast with the hospitable Hopkins.
cfrz10{_}381{_}They had saved scarcely any clothes, but Tom and his master were equipped from a ready-made shop.
cfrz10{_}382{_}The women had to remain indoors in borrowed garments till they could be made presentable by the dressmaker.
cfrz10{_}383{_}Mr. Furze was so unfitted to deal with events which did not follow in anticipated, regular order, that he was bewildered.
cfrz10{_}384{_}He and Tom went out to look at the ruins, and everything which had to be done seemed to crowd in upon him at once, one thing tumbling incessantly over the other, and nothing staying long enough before him to be settled.
cfrz10{_}385{_}Although his business had been fairly large, he had nothing of the faculty of the captain or the manager, who can let details alone and occupy himself with principles.
cfrz10{_}386{_}He had a stock of copper bolt-stave in the front shop, and he poked about and pestered the men to know if any of it could be found melted.
cfrz10{_}387{_}Then it occurred to him the next instant, and before the inquiry about the bolt-stave could be answered, that he had lost his account-books, and he began to try to recollect what one of his principal customers owed him.
cfrz10{_}388{_}Before his memory was fairly exercised on the subject it struck him that the men in the foundry--which was untouched--would not know what to do, and he hurried in, but came out again without leaving any directions.
cfrz10{_}389{_}At last he became so confused that he would have broken down if Tom had not come to the rescue, and gently laid hold of his arm.
cfrz10{_}390{_}"Let us go into the Bell"; and into the Bell they went, into the large, empty coffee-room, very quiet at that time of the morning.
cfrz10{_}391{_}"We are better here," said Tom, "if we want to know what we ought to do.
cfrz10{_}392{_}The first thing is to write to the insurance company."
cfrz10{_}393{_}"Of course, of course!"
cfrz10{_}394{_}"We will do that at once; I will write the letter, and you sign it."
cfrz10{_}395{_}In less than ten minutes this stage of the business was passed.
cfrz10{_}396{_}"The next thing is to find a shop while they are rebuilding."
cfrz10{_}397{_}That was not quite so easy a matter.
cfrz10{_}398{_}There was not one in the High Street to be let.
cfrz10{_}399{_}At last an idea struck Tom.
cfrz10{_}400{_}"There is the Moot Hall--underneath it, I mean.
cfrz10{_}401{_}We shall have to buy fittings, but I will have them so arranged that they will do for the new building.
cfrz10{_}402{_}All that is necessary is to obtain leave; but we shall be sure to get it:  only half of it is wanted on market days, and that's the part that isn't shut off.
cfrz10{_}403{_}We'll then write to Birmingham and Sheffield about the stock.
cfrz10{_}404{_}We'd better have a few posters stuck about at once, saying that business will be carried on in the Hall for the present."
cfrz10{_}405{_}Mr. Furze saw the complexity unravel itself, and the knot in his head began to loosen, but he did not quite like to reflect that he owed his relief to Tom, and that Tom had seen his agitation.
cfrz10{_}406{_}Accordingly, when a proof of the poster was brought, he was the master, most particularly the master, and observed with much dignity and authority that it ought not to have been set up without the benefit of his revision; that it would not do by any means as it stood, and that it had better be left with him.
cfrz10{_}407{_}Mr. and Mrs. Hopkins insisted upon continuing their hospitality until a new home could be found, and Mrs. Furze urged her project of the Terrace with such eagerness, that at last her husband consented.
cfrz10{_}408{_}"I think," said Mrs. Furze, when the debate was concluded, "that Catharine had better go away for a short time until we are settled in the Terrace and the shop is rebuilt.
cfrz10{_}409{_}She would not be of much use in the new house, and would only knock herself up."
cfrz10{_}410{_}That was not Mrs. Furze's reason.
cfrz10{_}411{_}She had said nothing to Catharine, but she instinctively dreaded her hostility to the scheme.
cfrz10{_}412{_}Mr. Furze knew that was not Mrs. Furze's reason, but he accepted it.
cfrz10{_}413{_}Mrs. Furze knew it was not her own reason, but she also accepted it, and believed it to be the true reason.
cfrz10{_}414{_}Such contradictions are quite possible in that mystery of mysteries the human soul.
cfrz10{_}415{_}"My dear Catharine," quoth her mother that evening, "you look worried and done up.
cfrz10{_}416{_}No wonder, considering what we have gone through.
cfrz10{_}417{_}A change would do you good, and you had better go and stay with your aunt at Ely till we have a roof of our own over our heads once more.
cfrz10{_}418{_}She will be delighted to see you."
cfrz10{_}419{_}Catharine particularly objected to her aunt at Ely.
cfrz10{_}420{_}She was a maiden lady and elder sister to Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}421{_}She had a small annuity, had turned herself into a most faithful churchwoman, and went to live at Ely because it was cheap and a cathedral city.
cfrz10{_}422{_}Every day, morning and afternoon, was Aunt Matilda to be seen at the cathedral services, and frequently she was the only attendant, save the choir and officials.
cfrz10{_}423{_}"Why do you want me out of the way?"
cfrz10{_}424{_}said Catharine, dismissing without the least notice the alleged pretext.
cfrz10{_}425{_}"I have told you, my dear."
cfrz10{_}426{_}"I cannot go to Ely.
cfrz10{_}427{_}If you wish me to go anywhere, I will go to Mrs.
cfrz10{_}428{_}Bellamy's."
cfrz10{_}429{_}"My dear, that is not a sufficient change for you.
cfrz10{_}430{_}Ely is a different climate, and I cannot consent to quartering you on a stranger for so long."
cfrz10{_}431{_}"Mrs. Bellamy will not object.
cfrz10{_}432{_}Will the new house be like the old one?"
cfrz10{_}433{_}"Well, really, may dear, nothing at present is quite determined; no doubt your father will take the opportunity of making a few improvements."
cfrz10{_}434{_}"My bedroom, I hope, will be what it was before, and in the same place."
cfrz10{_}435{_}"Oh, I--I trust there will be no serious alteration, except what-- what will be agreeable to us all, but your father is so much bothered now; perhaps you will have a room which is a little larger, but I really do not know.
cfrz10{_}436{_}I cannot say anything:  how can you EXPECT me to say anything just at present, my dear child?"
cfrz10{_}437{_}Again there was the same contradiction.
cfrz10{_}438{_}Mrs. Furze knew this was wrong, but she believed it was right.
cfrz10{_}439{_}There was, however, a slight balance in favour of what she knew against what she believed, and she hastened to appease her conscience by a mental promise that, as soon as possible, she would tell Catharine that, upon full consideration, they had determined, &c., &c.  That would put everything straight morally.
cfrz10{_}440{_}Had Catharine put her question yesterday--so Mrs. Furze argued--the answer now given would have been perfectly right.
cfrz10{_}441{_}She was doing nothing more than giving a reply which was a trifle in arrear of the facts, and, if she rectified it at the earliest date, the impropriety would be nothing.
cfrz10{_}442{_}It is sometimes thought that it is those who habitually speak the truth who are most easily deceived.
cfrz10{_}443{_}It is not quite so.
cfrz10{_}444{_}If the deceivers are not entirely deceived, they profess acquiescence, and perpetual acquiescence induces half-deception.
cfrz10{_}445{_}It is, perhaps, more correct to say that the word deception has no particular meaning for them, and implies a standard which is altogether inapplicable.
cfrz10{_}446{_}There is a tacit agreement through all society to say things which nobody believes, and that being the constitution under which we live, it is absurd to talk of truth or falsity in the strict sense of the terms.
cfrz10{_}447{_}A thing is true when it is in accordance with the system and on a level with it, and false when it is below it.
cfrz10{_}448{_}Every now and then at rarest intervals a creature is introduced to us who speaks the veritable reality and wakes in us the slumbering conviction of universal imposture.
cfrz10{_}449{_}We know that he is not as other men are; we look into his eyes and see that they penetrate us through and through, but we cannot help ourselves, and we jabber to him as we jabber to the rest of the world.
cfrz10{_}450{_}It was ridiculous that her mother should talk as she did to Catharine.
cfrz10{_}451{_}Mrs. Furze was perfectly aware that she was not deluding her daughter; but she assumed that the delusion was complete.
cfrz10{_}452{_}"Well, mother, I say I cannot go to Ely."
cfrz10{_}453{_}Catharine again had her own way.
cfrz10{_}454{_}She went to Mrs. Bellamy's, and Mrs. Furze, after having told Mrs. Bellamy what was going to happen, begged her not to say anything to Catharine about it.
cfrz10{_}455{_}CHAPTER IV    Mr. Bellamy's farm of Westchapel--Chapel Farm it was usually called- -lay about half a mile from Lampson's Ford, and about five miles from Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}456{_}The road from Eastthorpe running westerly and parallel with the river at a distance of about a mile from it sends out at the fourth milestone a byroad to the south, which crosses the river by a stone bridge, and there is no doubt that before the bridge existed there was a ford, and that there was also a chapel hard by where people probably commended their souls to God before taking the water.
cfrz10{_}457{_}In the angle formed by the main road, the lane, and the river, lay Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}458{_}The house stood on a gentle slope, just enough to lift it above the range of the worst of winter floods, and faced the south.
cfrz10{_}459{_}It was not in the lane, but on a kind of private road or cart-track which issued from it; went through a gate and under a hedge; expanded itself in an open space of carefully weeded gravel just opposite the front door, and became a more insignificant and much rougher track on the other side, passing by the stacks into the field, and finally disappearing altogether.
cfrz10{_}460{_}From the hand-post on the main road to the gate was half a mile, and from the gate to the farm nearly another half-mile.
cfrz10{_}461{_}In driving from Chapel Farm you feel, when you reach the gate, you are in the busy world again, and when you reach the hand-post and turn to Eastthorpe you are in the full tide of life, although not a soul is to be seen.
cfrz10{_}462{_}Opposite the house were the farm-buildings and the farmyard.
cfrz10{_}463{_}The gate to the right of the farm-buildings led into the meadow, and thus anybody sitting in the front rooms could see the barges slowly and silently towed from the sea to the uplands and back again, the rising ground beyond, and so on to Thingleby, whose little spire just emerged above the horizon.
cfrz10{_}464{_}The river, deep and sluggish for the most part, was fringed with willows on the side opposite the towing-path.
cfrz10{_}465{_}At the bridge, just where the ford used to be, it was broken into shallows, over which the stream slipped faster, and here and there there were not above two or three feet of water, so that sometimes the barges were almost aground.
cfrz10{_}466{_}The farmhouse was not quite ideal.
cfrz10{_}467{_}It was plain red brick, now grey and lichen-covered, about a hundred years old; the windows were white-painted, with heavy frames, and the only attempt at ornament was a kind of porch over the front door, supported by brackets, but with no sides to it.
cfrz10{_}468{_}Nevertheless, it had its charms.
cfrz10{_}469{_}Save on the northern side, where it was backed by the huge elms in the home-field, it lay bare to the winds, breezy, airy, full of light.
cfrz10{_}470{_}In summer the front door was always open, and even when it was shut in cold weather no knocker was ever used.
cfrz10{_}471{_}If a visitor came by daylight he was always seen, and if after dark he was heard.
cfrz10{_}472{_}The garden, which lay on the west side of the house and at the back, was rather warm in hot weather, but was delicious.
cfrz10{_}473{_}Under the wall on the north side the apricot and Orleans plum ripened well, and round to the right was the dairy, always cool, sweet, and clean, with the big elder trees before the barred window.
cfrz10{_}474{_}The mistress of the house, Mrs. Bellamy, was not a very robust woman.
cfrz10{_}475{_}She was generally ailing, but never very seriously ill.  She had had two children, but they had both died.
cfrz10{_}476{_}Mrs. Bellamy's mind, unoccupied with parental cares, with politics, or with literature, let itself loose upon her house, her dairy, and her fowls.
cfrz10{_}477{_}She established a series of precautions to prevent dirt, and the precautions themselves became objects to be protected.
cfrz10{_}478{_}There was a rough scraper intervening on behalf of the black-leaded scraper; there was a large mat to preserve the mat beyond it:  and although a drugget coveted the stair carpet, Mrs. Bellamy would have been sorely vexed if she had found a footmark upon it.
cfrz10{_}479{_}If a friend was expected she put some straw outside the garden gate, and she asked him in gentle tones when he dismounted if he would kindly "just take the worst off" there.
cfrz10{_}480{_}The kitchen was scoured and scrubbed till it was fleckless.
cfrz10{_}481{_}It was theoretically the living-room, and a defence for the parlour, but it also was defended in its turn like the scraper, and the back kitchen, which had a fireplace, was used for cooking, the fire in the state kitchen not being lighted in summer time.
cfrz10{_}482{_}Partly Mrs. Bellamy's excessive neatness was due to the need of an occupation.
cfrz10{_}483{_}She brooded much, and the moment she had nothing to do she became low-spirited and unwell.
cfrz10{_}484{_}Partly also it was due to a touch of poetry.
cfrz10{_}485{_}She polished her verses in beeswax and turpentine, and sought on her floors and tables for that which the poet seeks in Eden or Atlantis.
cfrz10{_}486{_}It must not be imagined that because she was so particular she was stingy.
cfrz10{_}487{_}She was one of the most open-handed creatures that ever breathed.
cfrz10{_}488{_}She loved plenty.
cfrz10{_}489{_}The jug was always full to overflowing with beer, and the dishes were always heaped up with good things, so that nobody was ever afraid of robbing his neighbour.
cfrz10{_}490{_}Catharine was never weary of Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}491{_}She was busy from morning to night, and the living creatures on it were her especial delight.
cfrz10{_}492{_}Naturally, as is the case with all country girls, the circumference of her knowledge embraced a region which a town matron would have veiled from her daughters with the heaviest curtains.
cfrz10{_}493{_}"How's the foal going on?"
cfrz10{_}494{_}said Mrs. Bellamy to her husband one evening when he came in to supper.
cfrz10{_}495{_}"Oh, the foal's all right; he'll be just like his father--just the same broad hind-quarters.
cfrz10{_}496{_}Lord!
cfrz10{_}497{_}we shall hardly get him into the shafts.
cfrz10{_}498{_}You remember, Miss Catharine, as I showed you what extrornary quarters King Tom had when he came here?
cfrz10{_}499{_}It is a curious thing, there ain't one of his foals that hasn't got that mark of him.
cfrz10{_}500{_}I allus likes a horse, I do, that leaves his mark strong.
cfrz10{_}501{_}If you pay pretty heavy you ought to have something for your money.
cfrz10{_}502{_}The mother, though, is in a bad way:  my belief is she'll have milk- fever."
cfrz10{_}503{_}"That mare never seemed healthy to me," said Catharine.
cfrz10{_}504{_}"No; she was brought up anyhow.
cfrz10{_}505{_}When she was about a fortnight old her mother died.
cfrz10{_}506{_}They didn't know how to manage her, and half starved her."
cfrz10{_}507{_}"I don't believe in starvin' creatures when they are young," said Mrs. Bellamy, who was herself a very small eater.
cfrz10{_}508{_}"Nor I, either, and yet that mare, although, as you say, Miss Catharine, she was never healthy, has the most wonderful pluck, as you know.
cfrz10{_}509{_}I remember once I had two ton o' muck in the waggon, and we were stuck.
cfrz10{_}510{_}Jack and Blossom couldn't stir it, and, after a bit, chucked up.
cfrz10{_}511{_}I put in Maggie--you should have seen her!
cfrz10{_}512{_}She moved it, a'most all herself, aye, as far as from here to the gate, and then of course the others took it up.
cfrz10{_}513{_}That's blood!
cfrz10{_}514{_}What a thing blood is!--you may load it, but you can't break it.
cfrz10{_}515{_}Never a touch of the whip would she stand, and yet it's quite true she isn't right, and never was.
cfrz10{_}516{_}Maybe the foal will be like her; the shape goes after the father mostly, but the sperrit and temper after the mother."
cfrz10{_}517{_}The next morning Maggie was worse.
cfrz10{_}518{_}Catharine was in the stable as soon as anybody was stirring, and the poor creature was trembling violently.
cfrz10{_}519{_}She was watched with the most tender care, and when she became too weak to stand to eat or drink she was slung with soft bands and pads.
cfrz10{_}520{_}Her groans were dreadful.
cfrz10{_}521{_}After about a week of cruel misery she died.
cfrz10{_}522{_}It was evening, and Catharine sat down and looked at what was left of her friend.
cfrz10{_}523{_}She had never before even partly realised what death meant.
cfrz10{_}524{_}She was too young to feel its full force.
cfrz10{_}525{_}The time was yet to come when death would mean despair- -when the insolubility of the problem would induce carelessness to all other problems and their solution.
cfrz10{_}526{_}Furthermore, this was only a horse.
cfrz10{_}527{_}Still, the contrast struck her between the corpse before her and Maggie with her bright eyes and vivid force.
cfrz10{_}528{_}What had become of all that strength; what had become of HER?--and the girl mused, as countless generations had mused before her.
cfrz10{_}529{_}Then there was the pathos of it.
cfrz10{_}530{_}She thought of the brave animal which she had so often seen, apparently for the mere love of difficulty, struggling as if its sinews would crack.
cfrz10{_}531{_}She thought of its glad recognition when she came into the stable, and of its evident affection, half human, or perhaps wholly human, and imprisoned in a form which did not permit full expression.
cfrz10{_}532{_}She looked at its body as it lay there extended, quiet, pleading as it were against the doom of man and of beast, and tears came to her eyes as she noted the appeal--tears not altogether of sorrow, but partly of revolt.
cfrz10{_}533{_}Mr. Bellamy came in.
cfrz10{_}534{_}"Ah, Miss Catharine, I don't wonder at it.
cfrz10{_}535{_}There's many a human as I should less have missed than Maggie.
cfrz10{_}536{_}I can't make out at times why we should love the beasts so as perish."
cfrz10{_}537{_}"Perhaps they don't."
cfrz10{_}538{_}"Really, Miss, of course they do.
cfrz10{_}539{_}What's the Lord to do with all the dead horses and cows?"
cfrz10{_}540{_}Catharine thought, "Or with the dead men and women," but she said nothing.
cfrz10{_}541{_}The subject was new to her.
cfrz10{_}542{_}She took her scissors and cut off a wisp of Maggie's beautiful mane, twisted it up, put it carefully in a piece of paper, and placed it in a little pocket-book which she always carried.
cfrz10{_}543{_}The next morning as soon as it was daylight a man came over from Eastthorpe; Maggie was hoisted into a cart, her legs dangling down outside, and was driven away to be converted into food for dogs.
cfrz10{_}544{_}One of Catharine's favourite haunts was a meadow by the bridge.
cfrz10{_}545{_}She was not given to reading, but she liked a stroll and, as there were plenty of rats, the dog enjoyed the stroll too.
cfrz10{_}546{_}Not a week after Maggie's death she had wandered to this point without her usual companion.
cfrz10{_}547{_}A barge had gone down just before she arrived, and for some reason or other had made fast to the bank about a quarter of a mile below her on the side opposite to the towing-path.
cfrz10{_}548{_}She sat down under a willow with her face to the water and back to the sun, for it was very hot, and in a few minutes she was half dozing.
cfrz10{_}549{_}Suddenly she started, and one of the bargemen stood close by her.
cfrz10{_}550{_}"Hullo, my beauty!
cfrz10{_}551{_}Why, you was asleep!
cfrz10{_}552{_}Wot's the time?"
cfrz10{_}553{_}"I haven't a watch."
cfrz10{_}554{_}"Haven't a watch!
cfrz10{_}555{_}Now that's a shame; if you was mine, my love, you should 'ave one o' gold."
cfrz10{_}556{_}"It is time I was at home," said Catharine, rising with as much presence of mind as she could muster; "and I should think it must be your dinner-hour."
cfrz10{_}557{_}"Damn my dinner-hour, when I've got the chance of sittin' alongside a gal with sich eyes as yourn, my beauty.
cfrz10{_}558{_}Why, you make me all of a tremble.
cfrz10{_}559{_}Sit down for a bit."
cfrz10{_}560{_}Catharine moved away, but the bargee caught her round the waist.
cfrz10{_}561{_}"Sit down, I tell yer, jist for a minute.
cfrz10{_}562{_}Who's a-goin' to hurt yer?"
cfrz10{_}563{_}It was of no use to resist, and she did not scream.
cfrz10{_}564{_}She sat down, and his arm relaxed its hold to pick up his pipe which had fallen on the other side.
cfrz10{_}565{_}Instantly, without a second's hesitation she leaped up, and, before his heavy bulk could lift itself, she had turned and rushed along the bank.
cfrz10{_}566{_}Had she made for the bridge, he would have overtaken her in the lane, but she went the other way.
cfrz10{_}567{_}About fifty yards down the stream, and in the direction of Chapel Farm, was a deep hole in the river bed, about five feet wide.
cfrz10{_}568{_}On the other side of it there were not more than eighteen inches of water at any point.
cfrz10{_}569{_}Catharine knew that hole well, as the haunt of the jack and the perch.
cfrz10{_}570{_}She reached it, cleared it at a bound, and alighted on the bit of shingle just beyond it.
cfrz10{_}571{_}Her pursuer came up and stared at her silently, with his mouth half open.
cfrz10{_}572{_}Just at that moment the instant sound of wheels was heard, and he slowly sauntered back to his barge.
cfrz10{_}573{_}Catharine boldly waded over the intervening shallows, and was across just as the cart reached the top of the bridge, but her shoes remained behind her in the mud.
cfrz10{_}574{_}It proved to be her father's cart, and to contain Tom, who had been over to Thingleby that morning to see what chance there was of getting any money out of a blacksmith who was largely in Mr. Furze's debt.
cfrz10{_}575{_}He saw there was something wrong, and dismounted.
cfrz10{_}576{_}"Why, Miss Catharine, you are all wet!
cfrz10{_}577{_}What is the matter?"
cfrz10{_}578{_}"I slipped down."
cfrz10{_}579{_}She could not tell the truth, although usually so straightforward.
cfrz10{_}580{_}Tom looked at her inquiringly as if he was not quite sure, but there was something in her face which forbade further investigation.
cfrz10{_}581{_}"You've lost your shoes; you cannot walk home; will you let me give you a lift to Chapel Farm?"
cfrz10{_}582{_}"They do not matter a straw:  it is grass nearly the whole way."
cfrz10{_}583{_}"I'll fish them out, if you will show me where they are."
cfrz10{_}584{_}"Carried down by this time ever so far."
cfrz10{_}585{_}"But you will hurt your feet; it isn't all grass; you had better get in."
cfrz10{_}586{_}She thought suddenly of the bargee again, and reflected that the barge might still be moored where it was an hour ago.
cfrz10{_}587{_}"Very well, then, I will go."
cfrz10{_}588{_}She essayed to put her foot upon the step, but the mud on her stocking was greasy, and she fell backwards.
cfrz10{_}589{_}Tom caught her in his arms, and a strange thrill passed through him when he felt that the whole weight of her body rested on him.
cfrz10{_}590{_}Many a man there is who can call to mind, across forty years, a silly passage like this in his life.
cfrz10{_}591{_}His hair has whitened; all passion ought long ago to have died out of him; thousands of events of infinitely greater consequence have happened; he has read much in philosophy and religion, and has forgotten it all, and a slip on the ice when skating together, or a stumble on the stair, or the pressure of a hand prolonged just for a second in parting, is felt with its original intensity, and the thought of it drives warm blood once more through the arteries.
cfrz10{_}592{_}"Let me get in first," said Tom, putting some straw on the step.
cfrz10{_}593{_}He got into the cart, and he gently pulled her up, relinquishing her very carefully, and, in fact, not until after his assistance was no longer needed.
cfrz10{_}594{_}"How DID you manage it?"
cfrz10{_}595{_}"You know how these things happen:  it was all-over in a minute: how are father and mother?"
cfrz10{_}596{_}"They are very well."
cfrz10{_}597{_}There was a pause for a minute or two.
cfrz10{_}598{_}"Well, how are things going on at Eastthorpe?"
cfrz10{_}599{_}"Oh, pretty well; the building is three parts done.
cfrz10{_}600{_}I don't think, Miss Catharine, you'll ever go back to the old spot again."
cfrz10{_}601{_}"What do you mean?"
cfrz10{_}602{_}"I don't think your father and mother will leave the Terrace."
cfrz10{_}603{_}"Very likely," she replied, decisively.
cfrz10{_}604{_}"It will be better, perhaps, that they should not.
cfrz10{_}605{_}I am sure that whatever they do will be quite right."
cfrz10{_}606{_}"Of course, Miss Catharine, but _I_ shall be sorry.
cfrz10{_}607{_}I wish my bedroom could have been built up again between the old walls.
cfrz10{_}608{_}In that bedroom you saved my life."
cfrz10{_}609{_}"Rubbish!
cfrz10{_}610{_}Even suppose _I_ had done it, as you say, I should have done just the same for my silkworms, and then, somehow when I do a thing on a sudden like that, I always feel as if _I_ had not done it.
cfrz10{_}611{_}I am sure I didn't do it."
cfrz10{_}612{_}The last few words were spoken in a strangely different tone, much softer and sweeter.
cfrz10{_}613{_}"I don't quite understand."
cfrz10{_}614{_}"I mean," said Catharine, speaking slowly, as if half surprised at what had occurred to her, and half lost in looking at it--"I mean that I do not a bit reflect at such times upon what I do.
cfrz10{_}615{_}It is as if something or somebody took hold of me, and, before I know where I am, the thing is done, and yet there is no something nor somebody-- at least, so far as I can see.
cfrz10{_}616{_}It is wonderful, for after all it is I who do it."
cfrz10{_}617{_}Tom looked intently at her.
cfrz10{_}618{_}She seemed to be taking no notice of him and to be talking to herself.
cfrz10{_}619{_}He had never seen her in that mood before, although he had often seen her abstracted and heedless of what was passing.
cfrz10{_}620{_}In a few moments she recovered herself, and the usual everyday accent returned with an added hardness.
cfrz10{_}621{_}"Here we are at Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}622{_}Mind you say nothing to father or mother; it will only frighten them."
cfrz10{_}623{_}Mrs. Bellamy came to the gate.
cfrz10{_}624{_}"Lor' bless the child!
cfrz10{_}625{_}wherever have you been!"
cfrz10{_}626{_}"Slipped into the water and left my shoes behind me, that's all"; and she ran indoors, jumping from mat to mat, and without even so much as bidding Tom goodbye, who rode home, not thinking much about his business, but lost in a muddle of most contradictory presentations, a constant glimmer of Catharine's ankles, wonderment at her accident--was it all true?--the strange look when she disclaimed the honour of his rescue and expounded her philosophy, and the fall between his shoulders.
cfrz10{_}627{_}When he slept, his sleep was usually dreamless, but that night he dreamed as he hardly ever dreamed before.
cfrz10{_}628{_}He perpetually saw the foot on the step, and she was slipping into his arms continually, until he awoke with the sun.
cfrz10{_}629{_}CHAPTER V    Catherine went home, or rather to the Terrace, soon afterwards, and found that there was no intention of removing to the High Street, although, notwithstanding their three months' probation in the realms of respectability, Mrs. Colston had not called, and Mrs. Furze was beginning to despair.
cfrz10{_}630{_}The separation from the chapel was nearly complete.
cfrz10{_}631{_}It had been done by degrees.
cfrz10{_}632{_}On wet days Mrs. Furze went to church because it was a little nearer, and Mr. Furze went to chapel; then Mrs. Furze went on fine days, and, after a little interval, Mr. Furze went on a fine day.
cfrz10{_}633{_}A fund had been set going to "restore" the church:  the heavy roof was to be removed, and a much lighter and handsomer roof covered with slate was to be substituted; the stonework of many of the windows, which the rector declared had begun to show "signs of incipient decay," was to be cut out and replaced with new, so as to make, to use the builder's words, "a good job of it," and a memorial window was to be put in near the great west window with its stained glass, the Honourable Mr. Eaton having determined upon this mode of commemorating the services of his nephew, Lieutenant Eaton, who had died of dysentery in India, brought on by inattention to tropical rules of eating and drinking, particularly the latter.
cfrz10{_}634{_}Oliver Cromwell, it was said, had stabled his horses in the church.
cfrz10{_}635{_}This, however, is doubtful, for the quantity of stable accommodation he must have required throughout the country, to judge from vergers and guidebooks, must have been much larger than his armies would have needed, if they had been entirely composed of cavalry; and the evidence is not strong that his horses were so ubiquitous.
cfrz10{_}636{_}It was further affirmed that, during the Cromwellian occupation, the west window was mutilated; but there was also a tradition that, in the days of George the Third, there were complaints of dinginess and want of light, and that part of the stained glass was removed and sold.
cfrz10{_}637{_}Anyhow, there was stained glass in the Honourable Mr. Eaton's mansion wonderfully like that at Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}638{_}It was now proposed to put new stained glass in the defective lights.
cfrz10{_}639{_}Some of the more advanced of the parishioners, including the parson and the builder, thought the old glass had better all come out, "the only way to make a good job of it"; but at an archidiaconal visitation the archdeacon protested, and he was allowed to have his own way.
cfrz10{_}640{_}Then there was the warming, and this was a great difficulty, because no natural exit for the pipe could be found.
cfrz10{_}641{_}At last it was settled to have three stoves, one at the west end of the nave, and one in each transept.
cfrz10{_}642{_}With regard to the one in the nave there was no help for it but to bore a hole through the wall.
cfrz10{_}643{_}The builder undertook "to give the pipe outside a touch of the Gothic, so that it wouldn't look bad," and as for the other stoves, there were two windows just handy.
cfrz10{_}644{_}By cutting out the head of Matthew in one and that of Mark in another, the thing was done, and, as Mrs. Colston observed, "the general confused effect remained the same."
cfrz10{_}645{_}There were one or two other improvements, such as pointing all over outside, also strongly recommended by the builder, and the shifting some of the tombs, and repairing the tracery, so that altogether the sum to be raised was considerable.
cfrz10{_}646{_}Mrs. Colston was one of the collectors, and Mrs. Furze called on her after two months' residence in the Terrace, and intimated her wish to subscribe.
cfrz10{_}647{_}Mrs. Colston took the money very affably, but still she did not return the visit.
cfrz10{_}648{_}Meanwhile Mrs. Furze was doing everything she could to make herself genteel.
cfrz10{_}649{_}The Terrace contained about a dozen houses; the two in the centre were higher than the rest, and above them, flanked by a large scroll at either end, were the words "THE TERRACE," moulded out of the stucco; up to each door was a flight of stone steps; before each front window on the dining-room floor and the floor above was a balcony protected by cast-iron filigree work, and between each house and the road was a little piece of garden surrounded by dwarf wall and arrow-head railings.
cfrz10{_}650{_}Mrs. Furze's old furniture had, nearly all, been discarded or sold, and two new carpets had been bought.
cfrz10{_}651{_}The one in the dining-room was yellow and chocolate, and the one upstairs in the drawing-room was a lovely rose-pattern, with large full-blown roses nine inches in diameter in blue vases.
cfrz10{_}652{_}The heavy chairs had disappeared, and nice light elegant chairs were bought, insufficient, however, for heavy weights, for one of Mr. Furze's affluent customers being brought to the Terrace as a special mark of respect, and sitting down with a flop, as was his wont, smashed the work of art like card-board and went down on the door with a curse, vowing inwardly never again to set foot in Furze's Folly, as he called it.
cfrz10{_}653{_}The pictures, too, were all renewed.
cfrz10{_}654{_}The "Virgin Mary" and "George the Fourth" went upstairs to the spare bedroom, and some new oleographs, "a rising art," Mrs. Furze was assured, took their places.
cfrz10{_}655{_}They had very large margins, gilt frames, and professed to represent sunsets, sunrises, and full moons, at Tintern, Como, and other places not named, which Mrs. Furze, in answer to inquiries, always called "the Continent."
cfrz10{_}656{_}Mr. Furze had had a longish walk one morning, and was rather tired.
