cfrz10{_}1720{_}Then she leaned upon the wall and found some relief in a great fit of sobbing.
cfrz10{_}1721{_}Consolation she had none; not even the poor reward of conscience and duty.
cfrz10{_}1722{_}She had lost him, and she felt that, if she had been left to herself, she would have kept him.
cfrz10{_}1723{_}She went out again late in the evening.
cfrz10{_}1724{_}The clouds had passed away to the south and east, but the lightning still fired the distant horizon far beyond Eastthorpe and towards Abchurch.
cfrz10{_}1725{_}The sky was clearing in the west, and suddenly in a rift Arcturus, about to set, broke through and looked at her, and in a moment was again eclipsed.
cfrz10{_}1726{_}What strange confusion!
cfrz10{_}1727{_}What inexplicable contrasts!
cfrz10{_}1728{_}Terror and divinest beauty; the calm of the infinite interstellar space and her own anguish; each an undoubted fact, but each to be taken by itself as it stood:  the star was there, the dark blue depth was there, but they were no answer to the storm or her sorrow.
cfrz10{_}1729{_}She returned to Eastthorpe on the following day and immediately told her mother she should not go back to the Misses Ponsonby.
cfrz10{_}1730{_}CHAPTER X    The reader has, doubtless, by this time judged with much severity not only Catharine, but Mr. Cardew.
cfrz10{_}1731{_}It is admitted to the full that they are both most unsatisfactory and most improbable.
cfrz10{_}1732{_}Is it likely that in a sleepy Midland town, such as Eastthorpe, knowing nothing but the common respectabilities of the middle of this century, the daughter of an ironmonger would fall in love with a married clergyman?
cfrz10{_}1733{_}Perhaps to their present biographer it seems more remarkable than to his readers.
cfrz10{_}1734{_}He remembers what the Eastern Midlands were like fifty years ago and they do not.
cfrz10{_}1735{_}They are thinking of Eastthorpe of the present day, of its schoolgirls who are examined in Keats and Shelley, of the Sunday morning walks there, and of the, so to speak, smelling acquaintance with sceptical books and theories which half the population now boasts.
cfrz10{_}1736{_}But Eastthorpe, when Mr. Cardew was at Abchurch, was totally different.
cfrz10{_}1737{_}It knew what it was for parsons to go wrong.
cfrz10{_}1738{_}It had not forgotten a former rector and the young woman at the Bell.
cfrz10{_}1739{_}What talk there was about that affair!
cfrz10{_}1740{_}Happily his friends were well connected:  they exerted themselves, and he obtained a larger sphere of usefulness two hundred miles away.
cfrz10{_}1741{_}Mr. Cardew, however, was not that rector, and Catharine was not the pretty waitress, and it is time now to tell the promised early history of Mr. Cardew.
cfrz10{_}1742{_}He was the son of a well-to-do London merchant, who lived in Stockwell, in a large, white house, with a garden of a couple of acres, shaded by a noble cedar in its midst.
cfrz10{_}1743{_}There were four children, but he was the only boy.
cfrz10{_}1744{_}His mother belonged to an old and very religious family, and inherited all its traditions of Calvinistic piety and decorum.
cfrz10{_}1745{_}Her love for this boy was boundless, and she had a double ambition for him, which was that he might become a minister of God's Word, and in due time might marry Jane Berdoe, the only daughter of the Reverend Charles Berdoe, M.A., and Euphemia, her dearest friend.
cfrz10{_}1746{_}Mrs. Cardew had heard so much of the contamination of boys' schools that Theophilus was educated at home and sent straight from home to Cambridge.
cfrz10{_}1747{_}At the University he became a member of the ultra-evangelical sect of young men there, and devoted himself entirely to theology.
cfrz10{_}1748{_}He thus passed through youth and early manhood without any intercourse with the world so called, and he lacked that wholesome influence which is exercised by healthy companionship with those who differ from us and are not afraid to oppose us.
cfrz10{_}1749{_}Of course he married Jane Berdoe.
cfrz10{_}1750{_}His mother was always contriving that Jane should be present when he was at home; he was young; he had never known what it was to go astray with women, and he was unable to stand at a distance from her and ask himself if he really cared for her.
cfrz10{_}1751{_}He fell in love with himself, married himself, and soon after discovered that he did not know who his wife was.
cfrz10{_}1752{_}After his marriage he became wholly unjust to her, and allowed her defects to veil the whole of her character.
cfrz10{_}1753{_}The ultra-evangelical school in the Church preserved at that time the religious life of England, although in a very strange form.
cfrz10{_}1754{_}They believed and felt certain vital truths, although they did not know what was vital and what has not.
cfrz10{_}1755{_}They had real experience, and their roots lay, not upon the surface, but went deep down to the perennial springs, and the articles of their creed became a vehicle for the expression of the most real emotions.
cfrz10{_}1756{_}Evangelicalism, however, to Mr. Cardew was dangerous.
cfrz10{_}1757{_}He was always prone to self- absorption, and the tendency was much increased by his religion.
cfrz10{_}1758{_}He lived an entirely interior life, and his joys and sorrows were not those of Abchurch, but of another sphere.
cfrz10{_}1759{_}Abchurch feared wet weather, drought, ague, rheumatism, loss of money, and, on Sundays, feared hell, but Mr. Cardew's fears were spiritual or even spectral.
cfrz10{_}1760{_}His self-communion produced one strange and perilous result, a habit of prolonged evolution from particular ideas uncorrected by reference to what was around him.
cfrz10{_}1761{_}If anything struck him it remained with him, deduction followed deduction in practice unfortunately as well as in thought, and he was ultimately landed in absurdity or something worse.
cfrz10{_}1762{_}The wholesome influence of ordinary men and women never permits us to link conclusion to conclusion from a single premiss, or at any rate to act upon our conclusions, but Mr. Cardew had no world at Abchurch save himself.
cfrz10{_}1763{_}He saw himself in things, and not as they were.
cfrz10{_}1764{_}A sunset was just what it might happen to symbolise to him at the time, and his judgments upon events and persons were striking, but they were frequently judgments upon creations of his own imagination, and were not in the least apposite to what was actually before him.
cfrz10{_}1765{_}The happy, artistic, Shakespearean temper, mirroring the world like a lake, was altogether foreign to him.
cfrz10{_}1766{_}When he saw Catharine a new love awoke in him instantaneously.
cfrz10{_}1767{_}Was it legitimate or illegitimate?
cfrz10{_}1768{_}In many cases of the same kind the answer would be that the question is one which cannot be put.
cfrz10{_}1769{_}No matter how pure the intellectual bond between man and woman may be, it is certain to carry with it a sentiment which cannot be explained by the attraction of mere mental similarity.
cfrz10{_}1770{_}A man says to a man, "Do you really believe it?"
cfrz10{_}1771{_}and, if the answer is "yes," the two become friends; but if it is a woman who responds to him, something follows which is sweeter than friendship, whether she be bound or free.
cfrz10{_}1772{_}It cannot be helped; there is no reason why we should try to help it, provided only we do no harm to others, and indeed these delicate threads are the very fairest in the tissue of life.
cfrz10{_}1773{_}With Mr. Cardew it was a little different.
cfrz10{_}1774{_}Undoubtedly he was drawn to Catharine because her thoughts were his thoughts.
cfrz10{_}1775{_}St. Paul and Milton in him saluted St. Paul and Milton in her.
cfrz10{_}1776{_}But he did not know where to stop, nor could he look round and realise whither he was being led.
cfrz10{_}1777{_}Any other person in six weeks would have noticed the milestones on the road, and would have determined that it was time to turn, but he gaily walked forward with his head in the clouds.
cfrz10{_}1778{_}If anybody at that particular moment when he left the bridge could have made him comprehend that he was making love to a girl; that what he was doing was an ordinary, commonplace criminal act, or one which would justifiably be interpreted as such, he not only would have been staggered and confounded, but would instantly have drawn back.
cfrz10{_}1779{_}As it was, he was neither staggered nor confounded, and went home to his wife with but one image in his brain, that of Catharine Furze.
cfrz10{_}1780{_}Catharine was one of those creatures whose life is not uniform from sixteen to sixty, a simple progressive accumulation of experiences, the addition of a ring of wood each year.
cfrz10{_}1781{_}There had come a time to her when she had suddenly opened.
cfrz10{_}1782{_}The sun shone with new light, a new lustre lay on river and meadow, the stars became something more than mere luminous points in the sky, she asked herself strange questions, and she loved more than ever her long wanderings at Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}1783{_}This phenomenon of a new birth is more often seen at some epochs than at others.
cfrz10{_}1784{_}When a nation is stirred by any religious movement it is common, but it is also common in a different shape during certain periods of spiritual activity, such as the latter part of the eighteenth century and the first half of the nineteenth in England and Germany.
cfrz10{_}1785{_}Had Catharine been born two hundred years earlier, life would have been easy.
cfrz10{_}1786{_}All that was in her would have found expression in the faith of her ancestors, large enough for any intellect or any heart at that time.
cfrz10{_}1787{_}She would have been happy in the possession of a key which unlocks the mystery of things, and there would have been ample room for emotion.
cfrz10{_}1788{_}How impatient she became of those bars which nowadays restrain people from coming close to one another!
cfrz10{_}1789{_}Often and often she felt that she could have leaped out towards the person talking to her, that she could have cried to him to put away his circumlocutions, his forms and his trivialities, and to let her see and feel what he really was.
cfrz10{_}1790{_}Often she knew what it was to thirst like one in a desert for human intercourse, and she marvelled how those who pretended to care for her could stay away so long:  she could have humiliated herself if only they would have permitted her to love them and be near them.
cfrz10{_}1791{_}Poor Catharine!
cfrz10{_}1792{_}the world as it is now is no place for people so framed!
cfrz10{_}1793{_}When life runs high and takes a common form men can walk together as the disciples walked on the road to Emmaus.
cfrz10{_}1794{_}Christian and Hopeful can pour out their hearts to one another as they travel towards the Celestial City and are knit together in everlasting bonds by the same Christ and the same salvation.
cfrz10{_}1795{_}But when each man is left to shift for himself, to work out the answers to his own problems, the result is isolation.
cfrz10{_}1796{_}People who, if they were believers, would find the richest gift of life in utter confidence and mutual help are now necessarily strangers.
cfrz10{_}1797{_}One turns to metaphysics; another to science; one takes up with Rousseau's theory of existence, and another with Kant's; they meet; they have nothing to say; they are of no use to one another in trouble; one hears that the other is sick; what can be done?
cfrz10{_}1798{_}There is a nurse; he does not go; his old friend dies, and as to the funeral--well, we are liable to catch cold.
cfrz10{_}1799{_}Not so Christian and Hopeful!
cfrz10{_}1800{_}for when Christian was troubled "with apparitions of hobgoblins and evil spirits, even on the borderland of Heaven--oh, Bunyan!
cfrz10{_}1801{_}Hopeful kept his brother's head above water, and called upon him to turn his eyes to the Gate and the men standing by it to receive him."
cfrz10{_}1802{_}My poor reader-friend, how many times have you in this nineteenth century, when the billows have gone over you--how many times have you felt the arm of man or woman under you raising you to see the shining ones and the glory that is inexpressible?
cfrz10{_}1803{_}Had Catharine been born later it would have been better.
cfrz10{_}1804{_}She would perhaps have been able to distract herself with the thousand and one subjects which are now got up for examinations, or she would perhaps.
cfrz10{_}1805{_}have seriously studied some science, which might at least have been effectual as an opiate in suppressing sensibility.
cfrz10{_}1806{_}She was, however, in Eastthorpe before the new education, as it is called, had been invented.
cfrz10{_}1807{_}There was no elaborate system of needle points, Roman and Greek history, plain and spherical trigonometry, political economy, ethics, literature, chemistry, conic sections, music, English history, and mental philosophy, to draw off the electricity within her, nor did she possess the invaluable privilege of being able, after studying a half-crown handbook, to unbosom herself to women of her own age upon the position of Longland as an English poet.
cfrz10{_}1808{_}Shakespeare or Wordsworth might have been of some use to her, but to Shakespeare she was not led, although there was a brown, dusty, one- volume edition at the Terrace; and of Wordsworth nobody whom she knew in Eastthorpe had so much as heard.
cfrz10{_}1809{_}A book would have turned much that was vague in her into definite shape; it would have enabled her to recognise herself; it would have given an orthodox expression to cloud singularity, and she would have seen that she was a part of humanity in her most extravagant and personal emotions.
cfrz10{_}1810{_}As it was, her position was critical because she stood by herself, affiliated to nothing, an individual belonging to no species, so far as she knew.
cfrz10{_}1811{_}She then met Mr. Cardew.
cfrz10{_}1812{_}It was through him the word was spoken to her, and he was the interpreter of the new world to her.
cfrz10{_}1813{_}She was in love with him--but what is love?
cfrz10{_}1814{_}There is no such thing:  there are loves, and they are all different.
cfrz10{_}1815{_}Catharine's was the very life of all that was Catharine, senses, heart, and intellect, a summing-up and projection of her whole selfhood.
cfrz10{_}1816{_}He was more to her than she to him--was any woman ever so much to a man as a man is to a woman?
cfrz10{_}1817{_}She was happy when she was near him.
cfrz10{_}1818{_}When she was in ordinary Eastthorpe society she felt as a pent-up lake might feel if the weight of its waters were used in threading needles, but when Mr. Cardew talked to her, and she to him, she rejoiced in the flow of all her force, and that horrible oppression in her chest vanished.
cfrz10{_}1819{_}Nevertheless, the fear, the shudder, came to her and not to him; the wrench came from her and not from him.
cfrz10{_}1820{_}It was she and not he who watched through the night and found no motive for the day, save a dull, miserable sense that it was her duty to live through it.
cfrz10{_}1821{_}CHAPTER XI    It was a fact, and everybody noticed it, that since the removal to the Terrace, and the alteration in their way of living, Mr. Furze was no longer the man he used to be, and seemed to have lost his grasp over his business.
cfrz10{_}1822{_}To begin with, he was not so much in the shop.
cfrz10{_}1823{_}His absences in the Terrace at meal-times made a great gap in the day, and Tom Catchpole was constantly left in sole charge.
cfrz10{_}1824{_}Mr. Bellamy came home one evening and told his wife that he had called at Furze's to ask the meaning of a letter Furze had signed, explaining the action of a threshing-machine which was out of order.
cfrz10{_}1825{_}To his astonishment Furze, who was in his counting-house, called for Tom, and said, "Here, Tom, this is one of your letters; you had better tell Mr. Bellamy how the thing works."
cfrz10{_}1826{_}"I held my tongue, Mrs. Bellamy, but I had my thoughts all the same, and the next time I go there, IF I go at all, I shall ask for Tom."
cfrz10{_}1827{_}Mr. Furze was aware of Tom's growing importance, and Mrs. Furze was aware of it too.
cfrz10{_}1828{_}The worst of it was that Mr. Furze, at any rate, knew that he could not do without him.
cfrz10{_}1829{_}It is very galling to the master to feel that his power is slipping from him into the hands of a subordinate, and he is apt to assert himself by spasmodic attempts at interference which generally make matters worse and rivet his chains more tightly.
cfrz10{_}1830{_}There was a small factory in Eastthorpe in which a couple of grindstones were used which were turned by water- power at considerable speed.
cfrz10{_}1831{_}One of them had broken at a flaw.
cfrz10{_}1832{_}It had flown to pieces while revolving, and had nearly caused a serious accident.
cfrz10{_}1833{_}The owner called at Mr. Furze's to buy another.
cfrz10{_}1834{_}There were two in stock, one of which he would have taken; but Tom, his master being at the Terrace, strongly recommended his customer not to have that quality, as it was from the same quarry as the one which was faulty, but that another should be ordered.
cfrz10{_}1835{_}To this he assented.
cfrz10{_}1836{_}When Mr. Furze returned Tom told him what had happened.
cfrz10{_}1837{_}He was in an unusually irritable, despotic mood.
cfrz10{_}1838{_}Mrs. Furze had forced him to yield upon a point which he had foolishly made up his mind not to concede, and consequently he was all the more disposed to avenge his individuality elsewhere.
cfrz10{_}1839{_}After meditating for a minute or two he called Tom from the counter.
cfrz10{_}1840{_}"Mr. Catchpole, what do you mean by taking upon yourself to promise you would obtain another grindstone?"
cfrz10{_}1841{_}"Mean, sir!
cfrz10{_}1842{_}I do not quite understand.
cfrz10{_}1843{_}The two out there are of the same sort as the one that broke, and I did not think them safe."
cfrz10{_}1844{_}"Think, sir!
cfrz10{_}1845{_}What business had you to think?
cfrz10{_}1846{_}I tell you what it is, you are much too fond of thinking.
cfrz10{_}1847{_}If you would only leave the thinking to me, and do what you are told, it would be much better for you."
cfrz10{_}1848{_}Tom's first impulse was to make a sharp reply, and to express his willingness to leave, but for certain private reasons he was silent.
cfrz10{_}1849{_}Encouraged by the apparent absence of resistance, Mr. Furze continued -  "I've meant to have a word or two with you several times.
cfrz10{_}1850{_}You seem to have forgotten your position altogether, and that I am master here, and not you.
cfrz10{_}1851{_}You, perhaps, do not remember where you came from, and what you would have been if I had not picked you up.
cfrz10{_}1852{_}Let there be no misunderstanding in future."
cfrz10{_}1853{_}"There shall be none, sir.
cfrz10{_}1854{_}Shall I call at the factory and explain your wishes about the grindstone?
cfrz10{_}1855{_}I will tell them I was mistaken, and that they had better have one of those in stock."
cfrz10{_}1856{_}"No, you cannot do that now; let matters remain as they are; I must lose the sale of the stone and put up with it."
cfrz10{_}1857{_}Tom withdrew.
cfrz10{_}1858{_}That evening, after supper, Mr. Furze, anxious to show his wife that he possessed some power to quell opposition, told her what had happened.
cfrz10{_}1859{_}It met with her entire approval.
cfrz10{_}1860{_}She hated Tom.
cfrz10{_}1861{_}For all hatred, as well as for all love, there is doubtless a reason, but the reasons for the hatreds of a woman of Mrs. Furze's stamp are often obscure, and perhaps more nearly an exception than any other known fact in nature to the rule that every effect must have a cause.
cfrz10{_}1862{_}"I would get rid of him," said she.
cfrz10{_}1863{_}"I think that his not replying to you is ten times more aggravating than if he had gone into a passion."
cfrz10{_}1864{_}"You cannot get rid of him," said Catharine.
cfrz10{_}1865{_}"Cannot!
cfrz10{_}1866{_}What do you mean, Catharine--cannot?
cfrz10{_}1867{_}I like that!
cfrz10{_}1868{_}Do you suppose that I do not understand my own business--I who took him up out of the gutter and taught him?
cfrz10{_}1869{_}Cannot, indeed!"
cfrz10{_}1870{_}"Of course you CAN get rid of him, father; but I would not advise you to try it."
cfrz10{_}1871{_}"Now, do take MY advice," said Mrs. Furze:  "send him about his business, at once, before he does any further mischief, and gets hold of your connection.
cfrz10{_}1872{_}Promise me."
cfrz10{_}1873{_}"I will," said Mr. Furze, "to-morrow morning, the very first thing."
cfrz10{_}1874{_}Morning came, and Mr. Furze was not quite so confident.
cfrz10{_}1875{_}Mrs. Furze had not relented, and as her husband went out at the door she reminded him of his vow.
cfrz10{_}1876{_}"You will, now?
cfrz10{_}1877{_}I shall expect to hear when you come home that he has had notice."
cfrz10{_}1878{_}"Oh, certainly he shall go, but I am doubtful whether I had better not wait till I have somebody in my eye whom I can put in his place."
cfrz10{_}1879{_}"Nonsense!
cfrz10{_}1880{_}you can find somebody easily enough."
cfrz10{_}1881{_}Mr. Furze strode into his shop looking and feeling very important.
cfrz10{_}1882{_}Instead of the usual kindly "Good morning," he nodded almost imperceptibly and marched straight into his counting-house.
cfrz10{_}1883{_}It had been his habit to call Tom in there and open the letters with him, Tom suggesting a course of action and replies.
cfrz10{_}1884{_}To-day he opened his correspondence in silence.
cfrz10{_}1885{_}It happened to be unusually bulky for a small business, and unusually important.
cfrz10{_}1886{_}The Honourable Mr. Eaton was about to make some important alterations in his house and grounds.
cfrz10{_}1887{_}New conservatories were to be built, and an elaborate system of hot-water warming apparatus was to be put up both for house and garden.
cfrz10{_}1888{_}He had invited tenders to specification from three houses--one in London, one in Cambridge, and from Mr. Furze.
cfrz10{_}1889{_}Tom and Mr. Furze had gone over the specification carefully, but Tom had preceded and originated, and Mr. Furze had followed, and, in order not to appear slow of comprehension, had frequently assented when he did not understand--a most dangerous weakness.
cfrz10{_}1890{_}To his surprise he found that his tender of 850 pounds was accepted.
cfrz10{_}1891{_}There was much work to be done which was not in his line, but had been put into his contract in order to save subdivision, and consequently arrangements had to be made with sub-contractors.
cfrz10{_}1892{_}Materials had also to be provided at once, and there was a penalty of so much a day if the job was not completed by a certain time.
cfrz10{_}1893{_}He did not know exactly where to begin; he was stunned, as if somebody had hit him a blow on the head, and, after trying in vain to think, he felt that his brain was in knots.
cfrz10{_}1894{_}He put the thing aside; looked at his other letters, and they were worse.
cfrz10{_}1895{_}One of his creditors, a blacksmith, who owed him 55 pounds for iron, had failed, and he was asked to attend a meeting of creditors.
cfrz10{_}1896{_}A Staffordshire firm, upon whom he had depended for pipes, in case he should obtain Mr. Eaton's order, had sent a circular announcing an advance in iron, and he forgot that in their offer their price held good for another week.
cfrz10{_}1897{_}He was trustee under an old trust, upon which no action had been taken for years; he remembered none of its provisions, and now the solicitors had written to him requesting him to be present at a most important conference in London that day week.
cfrz10{_}1898{_}There was also a notice from the Navigation Commissioners informing him that, in consequence of an accident at one of their locks, it would be fully a fortnight before any barge could pass through, and he knew that his supply of smithery coal would be exhausted before that date, as he had refrained from purchasing in consequence of high prices.
cfrz10{_}1899{_}To crown everything a tap came at the door, and in walked his chief man at the foundry to announce that he would shortly leave, as he had obtained a better berth.
cfrz10{_}1900{_}Mr. Furze by this time was so confused that he said nothing but "Very well," and when the man had gone he leaned his head on his elbows in despair.
cfrz10{_}1901{_}He looked through the glass window of the counting-house and saw Tom quietly weighing some nails.
cfrz10{_}1902{_}He would have given anything if he could have called him in, but he could not.
cfrz10{_}1903{_}As to dismissing him, it was out of the question now, and yet his sense of dependence on him excited a jealousy nearly as intense as his wife's animosity.
cfrz10{_}1904{_}When a man cannot submit to be helped he dislikes the benevolent friend who offers assistance worse than an avowed enemy.
cfrz10{_}1905{_}Mr. Furze felt as if he must at once request Tom's aid, and at the same time do him some grievous bodily harm.
cfrz10{_}1906{_}The morning passed away and nothing was advanced one single step.
cfrz10{_}1907{_}He went home to his dinner excited, and he was dangerous.
cfrz10{_}1908{_}It is very trying, when we are in a coil of difficulty, out of which we see no way of escape, to hear some silly thing suggested by an outsider who perhaps has not spent five minutes in considering the case.
cfrz10{_}1909{_}Mrs. Furze, knowing nothing of Mr. Eaton's contract, of the blacksmith's failure, of the advance in iron, of the trust meeting, of the stoppage of the navigation, and of the departure of the foundryman, asked her husband the moment the servant had brought in the dinner and had left the room -  "Well, my dear, what did Tom say when you told him to go?"
cfrz10{_}1910{_}"I haven't told him."
cfrz10{_}1911{_}"Not told him, my dear!
cfrz10{_}1912{_}how is that?"
cfrz10{_}1913{_}"I wish with all my heart you'd mind your own affairs."
cfrz10{_}1914{_}"Mr. Furze!
cfrz10{_}1915{_}what is the matter?
cfrz10{_}1916{_}You do not seem to know what you are saying."
cfrz10{_}1917{_}"I know perfectly well what I am saying.
cfrz10{_}1918{_}I wish you knew what YOU are saying.
cfrz10{_}1919{_}When we came up here to the Terrace--much good has it done us--I thought I should have no interference with my business.
cfrz10{_}1920{_}You understand nothing whatever about it, and I shall take it as a favour if you will leave it alone."
cfrz10{_}1921{_}Mrs. Furze was aghast.
cfrz10{_}1922{_}Presently she took out her pocket- handkerchief and retreated to her bedroom.
cfrz10{_}1923{_}Mr. Furze did not follow her, but his dinner remained untouched.
cfrz10{_}1924{_}When he rose to leave, Catharine went after him to the door, caught hold of his hand and silently kissed him, but he did not respond.
cfrz10{_}1925{_}During the dinner-hour Tom had looked in the counting-house and saw the letters lying on the table untouched.
cfrz10{_}1926{_}Mr. Eaton's steward came in with congratulations that the tender was accepted, but he could not wait.
cfrz10{_}1927{_}As Mr. Furze passed through the shop Tom told him simply that the steward had called.
cfrz10{_}1928{_}"What did he want?"
cfrz10{_}1929{_}"I do not know, sir."
cfrz10{_}1930{_}Mr. Furze went to his papers again and shut the door.
cfrz10{_}1931{_}He was still more incapable of collecting his thoughts and of determining how to begin.
cfrz10{_}1932{_}First of all came the contract, but before he could settle a single step the navigation presented itself.
cfrz10{_}1933{_}Then, without any progress, came the rise in the price of iron, and so forth.
cfrz10{_}1934{_}In about three hours the post would be going, and nothing was done.
cfrz10{_}1935{_}He cast about for some opportunity of a renewal of intercourse with Tom, and looked anxiously through his window, hoping that Tom might have some question to ask.
cfrz10{_}1936{_}At last he could stand it no longer, and he opened the door and called out -  "Mr. Catchpole"--not the familiar "Tom."
cfrz10{_}1937{_}Mr. Catchpole presented himself.
cfrz10{_}1938{_}"I wish to give you some instructions about these letters.
cfrz10{_}1939{_}I have arranged them in order.
cfrz10{_}1940{_}You will please write what I say, and I will sign in time for the post to-night.
cfrz10{_}1941{_}First of all there is the contract.
cfrz10{_}1942{_}You had better take the necessary action and ask the Staffordshire people what advance they want."
cfrz10{_}1943{_}"Yes, sir, but"--deferentially--"the Staffordshire people cannot claim an advance if you accept at once:  you remember the condition?"
cfrz10{_}1944{_}"Certainly; what I mean is that you can accept their tender.
cfrz10{_}1945{_}Then there is the meeting of creditors."
cfrz10{_}1946{_}"I suppose you wish Mr. Eaton's acceptance acknowledged and the sub- contractors at once informed?"
cfrz10{_}1947{_}"Of course, of course; I said necessary action--that covers everything.
cfrz10{_}1948{_}With regard to the creditors' meeting, my proposal is-- "  A pause.
cfrz10{_}1949{_}"Perhaps it will be as well, sir, if you merely say you will attend."
cfrz10{_}1950{_}"I thought you would take that for granted.
cfrz10{_}1951{_}I was considering what proposal I should make when we meet."
cfrz10{_}1952{_}"Probably, sir, you can make it better after you hear his statement."
cfrz10{_}1953{_}"Well, possibly it may be so; but I am always in favour of being prepared.
cfrz10{_}1954{_}However, we will postpone that for the present.
cfrz10{_}1955{_}Then there is the trustee business.
cfrz10{_}1956{_}That is a private matter of my own, which you will not understand.
cfrz10{_}1957{_}I will give you the papers, however, and you can make an abstract of them.
cfrz10{_}1958{_}I cannot carry every point in my head.
cfrz10{_}1959{_}If you are in any doubt come to me."
cfrz10{_}1960{_}"You wish me to say you will go, sir?"
cfrz10{_}1961{_}"I should have thought there was no need to ask.
cfrz10{_}1962{_}You surely do not suppose that I am to give instructions upon every petty detail!
cfrz10{_}1963{_}Then about the navigation:  I MUST have some coal, and that is the long and the short of it."
cfrz10{_}1964{_}The "how" was probably a petty detail, for Mr. Furze went no further with the subject, and was inclined to proceed with the man at the foundry.
cfrz10{_}1965{_}"It will be too late if we wait till the lock is repaired, sir.
cfrz10{_}1966{_}I understand it will be three weeks really.
cfrz10{_}1967{_}Will you write to Ditchfield and tell them five tons are to come to Millfield Sluice?
cfrz10{_}1968{_}We will then cart it from there.
cfrz10{_}1969{_}That will be the cheapest and the best way."
cfrz10{_}1970{_}"Yes, I do not object; but we MUST have the coal--that is really the important point.
cfrz10{_}1971{_}As to Jack in the foundry, I will get somebody else.
cfrz10{_}1972{_}I suppose we shall have to pay more."
cfrz10{_}1973{_}"How would it be, sir, if you put Sims in Jack's place, and Spurling in Sims' place?
cfrz10{_}1974{_}You would then only want a new labourer, and you would pay no more than you pay now.
cfrz10{_}1975{_}Sims, too, knows the work, and it might be awkward to have a new man at the head just now."
cfrz10{_}1976{_}"Yes, that may do; but what I wish to impress on you is that the vacancy MUST be filled up.
cfrz10{_}1977{_}That is all, I think; you can take the letters."
cfrz10{_}1978{_}Tom took them up and went to his little corner near the window to reperuse them.
cfrz10{_}1979{_}There was much to be done which had not been mentioned, particularly with regard to Mr. Eaton's contract.
cfrz10{_}1980{_}He took out the specification, jotted down on a piece of paper the several items, marked methodically with a cross those which required prompt attention, and began to write.
cfrz10{_}1981{_}Mr. Furze, seeing his desk unencumbered, was very well satisfied with himself.
cfrz10{_}1982{_}He had "managed" the whole thing perfectly.
cfrz10{_}1983{_}His head became clear, the knots were untied, and he hummed a few bars of a hymn.
cfrz10{_}1984{_}He then went to his safe, took out the trust papers without looking at them, handed them over to Tom with a remark that he should like the abstract the next morning, and at once went up to the Terrace.
cfrz10{_}1985{_}He was hungry:  he had left Mrs. Furze unwell, and, in his extreme good-humour, had relented towards her.
cfrz10{_}1986{_}She had recovered, but did not mention again the subject of Tom's discharge.
cfrz10{_}1987{_}He had ham with his tea, but it was over sooner than usual, and he rose to depart.
cfrz10{_}1988{_}"You are going early, father," said Catharine.
cfrz10{_}1989{_}"Yes, my dear; it has been a busy day.
cfrz10{_}1990{_}I have been successful with my tender for Mr. Eaton's improvements; iron has advanced; the navigation has stopped; Castle, the blacksmith, has gone to smash; I have to go to a trustees' meeting under that old Fothergill trust; and Jack in the foundry has given notice to leave."
cfrz10{_}1991{_}"When did you hear all this?"
cfrz10{_}1992{_}"All within an hour after breakfast.
cfrz10{_}1993{_}I have been entirely occupied this afternoon in directing Tom what to do, and I must be off to see that he has carried out my instructions.
cfrz10{_}1994{_}What a coil it is!
cfrz10{_}1995{_}and yet I rather like it."
cfrz10{_}1996{_}Catharine reflected that her father did not seem to like it at dinner-time, and went through the familiar operation of putting two and two together.
cfrz10{_}1997{_}She accompanied him to the front gate, and as he passed out she said -  "You have not given Tom notice?"
cfrz10{_}1998{_}"No, my dear, not yet.
cfrz10{_}1999{_}It would be a little inconvenient at present.
cfrz10{_}2000{_}I COULD do without him easily, even now; but perhaps it will be better to wait.
cfrz10{_}2001{_}Besides, he is a little more teachable after the talking-to I have given him."
cfrz10{_}2002{_}Mr. Furze signed his letters.
cfrz10{_}2003{_}He did not observe that many others, of which he had not thought, remained to be written, and when Tom brought them the next day he made no remark.
cfrz10{_}2004{_}The assumption was that he had noticed the day before what remained to be done, saw that it was not urgent, and consented to the delay.
cfrz10{_}2005{_}The curious thing was that he assumed it to himself.
cfrz10{_}2006{_}It is a tact--not incredible to those who know that nobody, not the most accomplished master in flattery, can humbug us so completely as we can and do humbug ourselves--that Mr. Furze, ten minutes after the letters were posted, was perfectly convinced that he had foreseen the necessity of each one--that he had personally and thoroughly controlled the whole day's operations, and that Tom had performed the duties of a merely menial clerk.
cfrz10{_}2007{_}As he went home he thought over Catharine's attitude with regard to Tom.
cfrz10{_}2008{_}She, in reality, had been anxious to protect her father; but such a motive he could not be expected to suggest to himself.
cfrz10{_}2009{_}A horrid notion came into his head.
cfrz10{_}2010{_}She might be fond of Tom!
cfrz10{_}2011{_}Did she not once save his life?
cfrz10{_}2012{_}Had she not, even when a child, pleaded that something ought to be done for him?
cfrz10{_}2013{_}Had she not affirmed that he was indispensable?
cfrz10{_}2014{_}Had she not inquired again about him that very day?
cfrz10{_}2015{_}Had she not openly expressed her contempt for that most eligible person, Mr. Colston?
cfrz10{_}2016{_}He determined to watch most strictly, and again he resolved to dismiss his assistant.
cfrz10{_}2017{_}A trifling increase in his attention to small matters should enable him to do this within a month or two.
cfrz10{_}2018{_}It would be as well for Mrs. Furze to watch too.
cfrz10{_}2019{_}After supper Catharine went to bed early, and her father hung out the white flag, to which friendly response was given directly the subject of his communications was apparent.
cfrz10{_}2020{_}It became a basis of almost instantaneous reconciliation, and Mrs. Furze, mindful of the repulse of the brewer's son and the ruin of her own scheme thereon built, hated Tom more than ever.
cfrz10{_}2021{_}It was Tom, then, who had prevented admission into Eastthorpe society.
cfrz10{_}2022{_}CHAPTER XII    Mr. Tom Catchpole had never had any schooling.
cfrz10{_}2023{_}What he had learned he had learned by himself, and the books he had read were but few, and chosen rather by chance.
cfrz10{_}2024{_}He had never had the advantage of the common introduction to the world of ideas which is given, in a measure, to all boys who are systematically taught by teachers, and consequently, not knowing the relative value of what came before him, his perspective and proportion were incorrect.
cfrz10{_}2025{_}His mind, too, was essentially plain.
cfrz10{_}2026{_}He was perfect in his loyalty to duty; he was, as we have seen, very good in business matters, had a clear head, and could give shrewd advice upon any solid, matter-of-fact difficulty, but the spiritual world was non-existent for him.
cfrz10{_}2027{_}He attended chapel regularly, for he was a Dissenter, but his reasons for going, so far as he had any, were very simple.
cfrz10{_}2028{_}There was a great God in heaven, against whom he had sinned and was perpetually sinning.
cfrz10{_}2029{_}To save himself from the consequences of his transgressions certain means were provided and he was bound to use them.
cfrz10{_}2030{_}On Monday morning chapel and all thoughts connected with it entirely disappeared, but he said his prayers twice a day with great regularity.
cfrz10{_}2031{_}There are very few, however, of God's creatures to whom the supernatural does not in some way present itself, and no man lives by bread alone.
cfrz10{_}2032{_}To Tom, Catharine was miracle, soul, inspiration, religion, enthusiasm, patriotism, immortality, the fact, essentially identical, whatever we like to call it, which is not bread and yet is life.
cfrz10{_}2033{_}He never dared to say anything to her.
cfrz10{_}2034{_}He felt that she lived in a world beyond him, and he did not know what kind of a world it was.
cfrz10{_}2035{_}He knew that she thought about things which were strange to him, and that she was anxious upon subjects which never troubled him.
cfrz10{_}2036{_}She was often greatly depressed when there was no cause for depression so far as he could see, and he could not comprehend why a person should be ill when there was nothing the matter.
cfrz10{_}2037{_}If he felt unwell--a rare event with him--he always took two antibilous pills before going to bed, and was all right the next morning.
cfrz10{_}2038{_}He wished he himself could be ill without a reason, and then perhaps he would be able to understand Catharine better.
cfrz10{_}2039{_}Her elation and excitement were equally unintelligible.
cfrz10{_}2040{_}He once saw her sitting in her father's counting-house with a book.
cfrz10{_}2041{_}She was not a great reader--nobody in Eastthorpe read books, and there were not many to read--but she was so absorbed in this particular book that she did not lift her eyes from it when he came in, and it was not until her father had spoken twice to her, and had told her that he was expecting somebody, that she moved.
cfrz10{_}2042{_}She then ran upstairs into a storeroom, and was there for half an hour in the cold.
cfrz10{_}2043{_}The book was left open when she went away, and Tom looked at it.
cfrz10{_}2044{_}It was a collection of poems by all kinds of people, and the one over which she had been poring was about a man who had shot an albatross.
cfrz10{_}2045{_}Tom studied it, but could make nothing of it, and yet this was what had so much interested her!
cfrz10{_}2046{_}"O God!"
cfrz10{_}2047{_}he said to himself passionately, "if I could, if I did but know!
cfrz10{_}2048{_}She cares not a pin for me; this is what she cares for."
cfrz10{_}2049{_}Poor Tom!
cfrz10{_}2050{_}he did not pride himself on the absence of a sense in him, but knew and acknowledged to himself that he was defective.
cfrz10{_}2051{_}It is quite possible to be aware of a spiritual insensibility which there is no power to overcome--of the existence of a universe in which other favoured souls are able to live, one which they can report, and yet its doors are closed to us, or, if sitting outside we catch a glimpse of what is within, we have no power to utter a single sufficient word to acquaint anybody with what we have seen:  Catharine respected Tom greatly, for she understood well enough what her father owed to him, but she could not love him.
cfrz10{_}2052{_}One penetrating word from Mr. Cardew thrilled every fibre in her, no matter what the subject might be.
cfrz10{_}2053{_}Tom, in every mood and on every topic, was uninteresting and ordinary.
cfrz10{_}2054{_}To tell the truth, plain, common probity taken by itself was not attractive to her.
cfrz10{_}2055{_}Horses, dogs, cows, the fields were more stimulant than perfect integrity, for she was young and did not know how precious it was; but, after all, the reason of reasons why she did not love Tom was that she did not love him.
cfrz10{_}2056{_}It was announced one day by small handbills in the shop windows that a sermon was to be preached by Mr. Cardew, of Abchurch, in Eastthorpe, on behalf of the County Infirmary, and Catharine went to hear him.
cfrz10{_}2057{_}It was in the evening, and she was purposely late.
cfrz10{_}2058{_}She did no go to her mother's pew, but sat down close to the door.
cfrz10{_}2059{_}To her surprise she saw Tom not far off.
cfrz10{_}2060{_}He was on his way to his chapel when he noticed Catharine alone, walking towards the church, and he had followed her.
cfrz10{_}2061{_}Mr. Cardew took for his text the parable of the prodigal son.
cfrz10{_}2062{_}He began by saying that this parable had been taken to be an exhibition of God's love for man.
cfrz10{_}2063{_}It seemed rather intended to set forth, not the magnificence of the Divine nature, but of human nature--of that nature which God assumed.
cfrz10{_}2064{_}The determination on the part of the younger son to arise, to go to his father, and above everything to say to him simply, "Father, I have sinned," was as great as God is great:  it was God--God moving in us; in a sense it was far more truly God--far greater than the force which binds the planets into a system.
cfrz10{_}2065{_}But the splendour of human nature--do not suppose any heresy here; it is Bible truth, the very gospel--is shown in the father as well as in the son.
cfrz10{_}2066{_}"When he was yet a great way off."
cfrz10{_}2067{_}We are as good as told then, that day after day the father had been watching.
cfrz10{_}2068{_}How small were the probabilities that at any particular hour the son would return, and yet every hour the father's eyes were on that long, dusty road!
cfrz10{_}2069{_}When at last he saw what he was dying to see, what did he say?
cfrz10{_}2070{_}Was there a word of rebuke?
cfrz10{_}2071{_}He stopped his boy's mouth with kisses and cried for the best robe and the ring and the shoes, and proclaimed a feast--the ring, mark you, a sign of honour!
cfrz10{_}2072{_}"Say nothing of pardon; the darkness hath gone: Shall pardon be asked for the night by the sun?
cfrz10{_}2073{_}No word of the past; of the future no fear: 'Tis enough, my beloved, to know thou art here."
cfrz10{_}2074{_}"Oh, my friends," said the preacher, "just consider that it is this upon which Jesus, the Son of God, has put His stamp, not the lecture, not chastisement, not expiation, but an instant unquestioning embrace, no matter what the wrong may have been.
cfrz10{_}2075{_}If you say this is dangerous doctrine, I say it is HERE.
cfrz10{_}2076{_}What other meaning can you give to it?
cfrz10{_}2077{_}At the same time I am astonished to find it here, astonished that priestcraft and the enemy of souls should not have erased it.
cfrz10{_}2078{_}Sacred truth!
cfrz10{_}2079{_}Is it not moving to think of all the millions of men who for eighteen hundred years have read this parable, philosophers and peasants, in every climate, and now are we reading it to-day!
cfrz10{_}2080{_}Is it not moving--nay, awful--to think of all the good it has done, of the sweet stream of tenderness, broad and deep, which has flowed down from it through all history?
cfrz10{_}2081{_}History would all have been different if this parable had never been told."
cfrz10{_}2082{_}Mr. Cardew paused, and after his emotion had a little subsided he concluded by an appeal on behalf of the infirmary.
cfrz10{_}2083{_}He inserted a saving clause on Christ's mediatorial work, but it had no particular connection with the former part of his discourse.
cfrz10{_}2084{_}It was spoken in a different tone, and it satisfied the congregation that they had really heard nothing heterodox.
cfrz10{_}2085{_}Tom watched Catharine closely.
cfrz10{_}2086{_}He noted her eager, rapt attention, and that she did not recover herself till the voluntary was at an end.
cfrz10{_}2087{_}He went out after her; she met Mr. and Mrs. Cardew at the churchyard gates; he saw the excitement of all three, and he saw Catharine leave her friends at the Rectory, for they were evidently going to stay the night there.
cfrz10{_}2088{_}Mrs. Cardew went into the house first, but Catharine turned down Fosbrooke Street, a street which did not lead, save by a very roundabout way, to the Terrace.
cfrz10{_}2089{_}Presently Mr. Cardew came out and walked slowly down Rectory Lane.
cfrz10{_}2090{_}In those days it was hardly a thoroughfare.
cfrz10{_}2091{_}It ended at the river bank, and during daylight a boat was generally there, belonging to an old, superannuated boatman, who carried chance passengers over to the mill meadows and saved them a walk if they wanted to go that side of the town.
cfrz10{_}2092{_}A rough seat had been placed near the boat moorings for the convenience of the ferryman's customers.
cfrz10{_}2093{_}At this time in the evening the place was deserted.
cfrz10{_}2094{_}Tom followed Mr. Cardew, and presently overtook him.
cfrz10{_}2095{_}Mr. Cardew and he knew one another slightly, for there were few persons for miles round who did not know and then visit Mr. Furze's shop.
cfrz10{_}2096{_}"Good evening, Mr.
cfrz10{_}2097{_}Cardew."
cfrz10{_}2098{_}"Ah!
cfrz10{_}2099{_}Mr. Catchpole, is that you?
cfrz10{_}2100{_}What are you doing here?"
cfrz10{_}2101{_}"I have been to hear you preach, sir, and I thought I would have a stroll before I went home."
cfrz10{_}2102{_}"I thought I should like a stroll too."
cfrz10{_}2103{_}The two went on together, and sat down on the seat.
cfrz10{_}2104{_}The moon had just risen, nearly full, sending its rays obliquely across the water, and lighting up the footpath which went right and left along the river's edge.
cfrz10{_}2105{_}Mr. Cardew seemed disinclined to talk, was rather restless, and walked backwards and forwards by the bank.
cfrz10{_}2106{_}Tom reflected that he might be intruding, but there was something on his mind, and he did not leave.
cfrz10{_}2107{_}Mr. Cardew sat down again by his side.
cfrz10{_}2108{_}They both happened to be looking in the same direction eastwards at the same moment.
cfrz10{_}2109{_}"If that lady thinks to cross to-night," said Tom, "she's mistaken.
cfrz10{_}2110{_}I'd take her over myself, though it is Sunday, if the boat were not locked."
cfrz10{_}2111{_}"What lady?"
cfrz10{_}2112{_}asked Mr. Cardew--as if he were frightened, Tom thought.
cfrz10{_}2113{_}"The lady coming down there just against the willow."
cfrz10{_}2114{_}Mr. Cardew was short-sighted, and could not see her.
cfrz10{_}2115{_}He made as if he would go to meet her, but he stopped, returned, and remained standing.
cfrz10{_}2116{_}The figure approached, but before Tom could discern anything more than that it was a woman, it disappeared behind the hedge up the little bypath that cut off the corner into Rectory Lane.
cfrz10{_}2117{_}"She's gone," said Tom.
cfrz10{_}2118{_}"I suppose she was not coming here after all."
cfrz10{_}2119{_}"Which way has she gone?"
cfrz10{_}2120{_}asked Mr. Cardew, looking straight on the ground and scratching it with his stick.
cfrz10{_}2121{_}"Into the town."
cfrz10{_}2122{_}"I must be going, I think, Mr. Catchpole; good-night."
cfrz10{_}2123{_}"I'll walk with you as far as your door, sir.
cfrz10{_}2124{_}There's something I want to say to you."
cfrz10{_}2125{_}Mr. Cardew did not reply, and meditated for a moment.
cfrz10{_}2126{_}"It is a lovely evening.
cfrz10{_}2127{_}We will sit here a little longer.
cfrz10{_}2128{_}What is it?"
cfrz10{_}2129{_}"Mr. Cardew, as I said, I have been to hear you preach, and I thank you with all my heart for your sermon, but I want to ask you something about it.
cfrz10{_}2130{_}What you said about the Mediator was true enough, but somehow, sir, I feel as if I ought to have liked the first part most, but I couldn't, and perhaps the reason is that it was poetry.
cfrz10{_}2131{_}Oh, Mr. Cardew, if you could but tell me how to like poetry!"
cfrz10{_}2132{_}"I am afraid neither I nor anybody else can teach you that; but why are you anxious to like it?
cfrz10{_}2133{_}Why are you dissatisfied with yourself?"
cfrz10{_}2134{_}"I do not think I am stupid.
cfrz10{_}2135{_}When I am in the shop I know that I am more of a match for most persons, and yet, Mr. Cardew, there are some people who seem to me to have something I have not got, and they value it more than anything besides, and they have nothing to say really, REALLY, I mean, to those who have not got it, although they are kind to them."
cfrz10{_}2136{_}"It is not very easy to understand what you mean."
cfrz10{_}2137{_}"Well now to-night, sir, when you talked about God moving in us, and the force which binds the planets together, and all that, I am sure you felt it, and I am sure it is true, and yet I was out of doors, so to speak."
cfrz10{_}2138{_}"Perhaps I may be peculiar, and it is you who are sane and sound."
cfrz10{_}2139{_}"Ah, Mr. Cardew, if you were alone in it, and everybody were like me, that might be true, but it is not so; it is I who am alone."
cfrz10{_}2140{_}"Who cares for it whom you know?
cfrz10{_}2141{_}You are under a delusion."
cfrz10{_}2142{_}"Oh, no, I am not.
cfrz10{_}2143{_}Why there--there."
cfrz10{_}2144{_}Tom stopped.
cfrz10{_}2145{_}"There was what?"
cfrz10{_}2146{_}"There was Miss Furze--she took it in."
cfrz10{_}2147{_}"Indeed!"
cfrz10{_}2148{_}Mr Cardew again looked straight on the ground, and again scratched it with his stick.
cfrz10{_}2149{_}It was a night of nights, dying twilight long lingering in the north-west, the low golden moon, the slow, placid, shining stream, perfect stillness.
cfrz10{_}2150{_}Tom was not very susceptible, but even he was overcome and tempted into confidence.
cfrz10{_}2151{_}"Mr. Cardew, you are a minister, and I may tell you:  I know you will not betray me.
cfrz10{_}2152{_}I love Miss Furze; I cannot help it.
cfrz10{_}2153{_}I have never loved any girl before.
cfrz10{_}2154{_}It is very foolish, for I am only her father's journeyman; but that might be got over.
cfrz10{_}2155{_}She would not let that stand in her way, I am sure.
cfrz10{_}2156{_}But, Mr. Cardew, I am not up to her; she is strange to me.
cfrz10{_}2157{_}If I try to mention her subjects, what I say is not right, and when I drove her home from Chapel Farm, and admired the view I know she admired, she directly began to speak about business, as if she did not wish to talk about better things; perhaps it is because I never was taught.
cfrz10{_}2158{_}I had no schooling; cannot you help me, sir?

cfrz10{_}2208{_}Accident!
cfrz10{_}2209{_}my friend Reuben told me the other day his marriage was an accident.
cfrz10{_}2210{_}The more I think about accidents, the less do I believe in them.

cfrz10{_}2223{_}We have discarded Providence as our forefathers believed in it; but nevertheless there is a providence without the big P, if we choose so to spell it, and yet surely deserving it as much as the Providence of theology, a non-theological Providence which watches over us and leads us.

cfrz10{_}2405{_}I will not say anything about religion, and all that, for I daresay that is nothing to YOU, but you might have had some consideration for your mother, especially in her weak state of health, before you broke her heart, and yet I blame myself, for you always had low tastes--going to Bellamy's, and consorting with people of that kind rather than with your mother's friends.
cfrz10{_}2406{_}Do you suppose Mrs. Colston will come near us again!
cfrz10{_}2407{_}And it all comes of trying to do one's best, for there's Carry Hawkins, only a grocer's daughter, who never had a sixpence spent on her compared with what you have, and she is engaged to Carver, the doctor at Cambridge.
cfrz10{_}2408{_}Oh, it's a serpent's tooth, it is, and if we had never scraped and screwed for you, and denied ourselves, but left you to yourself, you might have been better; oh dear, oh dear!"
cfrz10{_}2409{_}Catharine held her tongue.
cfrz10{_}2410{_}She saw instantly that if she denied any engagement with Tom she would not be believed, and that in any case Tom would have to depart.
cfrz10{_}2411{_}Moreover, one of her defects was a certain hardness to persons for whom she had small respect, and she did not understand that just because Mrs. Furze was her mother, she owed her at least deference, and, if possible, a tenderness due to no other person.
cfrz10{_}2412{_}However weak, foolish, and even criminal parents may be, a child ought to honour them as Moses commanded, for the injunction is, and should be, entirely unconditional.
cfrz10{_}2413{_}"Catharine," said Mr. Furze, "why do you not answer your mother?"
cfrz10{_}2414{_}"I cannot; I had better leave."
cfrz10{_}2415{_}She opened the door and went to her room.
cfrz10{_}2416{_}After she had left further debate arose, and three points were settled:  First, that no opposition should be offered to a visit to Chapel Farm, which had been proposed for the next day, as she would be better at the Farm than at the Terrace; secondly, that Tom and she were in love with one another; and thirdly, that not a word should be said to Tom.
cfrz10{_}2417{_}"Leave that to me," said Mrs. Furze again.
cfrz10{_}2418{_}Although she saw nothing distinctly, a vague, misty hope dawned upon her, the possibility of something she could not yet discern, and, notwithstanding the blow she had received, she was decidedly more herself within an hour after breakfast than she had been during the twelve hours preceding.
cfrz10{_}2419{_}CHAPTER XIV    In Mr. Furze's establishment was a man who went by the name of Orkid Jim, "Orkid" signifying the general contradictoriness and awkwardness of his temper.
cfrz10{_}2420{_}He had a brother who was called Orkid Joe, in the employ of a builder in the town, but it was the general opinion that Orkid Jim was much the orkider of the two.
cfrz10{_}2421{_}He was a person with whom Mr. Furze seldom interfered.
cfrz10{_}2422{_}He was, it is true, a good workman in the general fitting department, in setting grates, and for jobs of that kind, but he was impertinent and disobedient.
cfrz10{_}2423{_}Mr. Furze, however, tolerated his insults, and generally allowed him to have his own way.
cfrz10{_}2424{_}He was not only afraid of Orkid Jim, but he was a victim to that unhappy dread of a quarrel which is the torment and curse of weak minds.
cfrz10{_}2425{_}It is, no doubt, very horrible to see a man trample upon opinions and feelings as easily and carelessly as he would upon the grass, and go on his way undisturbed, but it is more painful to see faltering, trembling incapacity for self- assertion, especially before subordinates.
cfrz10{_}2426{_}Mr. Furze could not have suffered more than two or three days' inconvenience if Orkid Jim had been discharged, but a vague terror haunted him of something which might possibly happen.
cfrz10{_}2427{_}Partly this distressing weakness is due to the absence of a clear conviction that we are right; it is an intellectual difficulty; but frequently it is simple mushiness of character, the same defect which tempts us, when we know a thing is true, to whittle it down if we meet with opposition, and to refrain from presenting it in all its sharpness.
cfrz10{_}2428{_}Cowardice of this kind is not only injustice to ourselves, but to our friends.
cfrz10{_}2429{_}We inflict a grievous wrong by compromise.
cfrz10{_}2430{_}We are responsible for what we see, and the denial or the qualification should be left to take care of itself.
cfrz10{_}2431{_}Our duty is, if possible, to give a distinct outline to what we have in our mind.
cfrz10{_}2432{_}It is easy to say we should not be obstinate, pigheaded, and argue for argument's sake.
cfrz10{_}2433{_}That is true, just as much as every half truth is true, but the other half is also true.
cfrz10{_}2434{_}Mr. Furze, excepting when he was out of temper, never stood up to Orkid Jim.
cfrz10{_}2435{_}He needed the stimulus of passion to do what ought to have been done by reason, and when we cannot do what is right save under the pressure of excitement it is generally misdone.
cfrz10{_}2436{_}Orkid Jim had a great dislike to Tom, which he took no pains to conceal.
cfrz10{_}2437{_}It was difficult to ascertain the cause, but partly it was jealousy.
cfrz10{_}2438{_}Tom had got before him.
cfrz10{_}2439{_}This, however, was not all.
cfrz10{_}2440{_}It was a case of pure antipathy, such as may often be observed amongst animals.
cfrz10{_}2441{_}Some dogs are the objects of special hatred by others, and are immediately attacked by them, before any cause of offence can possibly have been given.
cfrz10{_}2442{_}Jim had called at the Terrace on the morning after the explosion with Catharine.
cfrz10{_}2443{_}He came to replace a cracked kitchen boiler, and Mrs. Furze, for some reason or other, felt inclined to go down to the kitchen and have some talk with him.
cfrz10{_}2444{_}She knew how matters stood between him and Tom.
cfrz10{_}2445{_}"Well, Jim, how are you getting on now?
cfrz10{_}2446{_}I have not seen you lately."
cfrz10{_}2447{_}"No, marm, I ain't one as comes to the front much now."
cfrz10{_}2448{_}"What do you mean?
cfrz10{_}2449{_}I suppose you might if you liked.
cfrz10{_}2450{_}I am sure Mr. Furze values you highly."
cfrz10{_}2451{_}Jim was cautious and cunning; not inclined to commit himself.
cfrz10{_}2452{_}He consequently replied by an "Ah," and knocked with great energy at the brickwork from which he was detaching the range.
cfrz10{_}2453{_}"Anything been the matter then, Jim?"
cfrz10{_}2454{_}"No, marm; nothing's the matter."
cfrz10{_}2455{_}"You have not quarrelled with Mr. Furze, I hope?
cfrz10{_}2456{_}You do not seem quite happy."
cfrz10{_}2457{_}"Me quarrel with Mr. Furze, marm!--no, I never quarrel with HIM.
cfrz10{_}2458{_}He's a gentleman, he is."
cfrz10{_}2459{_}Mrs. Furze was impatient.
cfrz10{_}2460{_}She wanted to come to the point, and could not wait to manoeuvre.
cfrz10{_}2461{_}"I am afraid you and Tom do not get on together."
cfrz10{_}2462{_}"Well, Mrs. Furze, if we don't it ain't my fault."
cfrz10{_}2463{_}"No, I dare say not; in fact, I am sure it is not.
cfrz10{_}2464{_}I dare say Tom is a little overbearing.
cfrz10{_}2465{_}Considering his origin, and the position he now occupies, it is natural he should be."
cfrz10{_}2466{_}"He ain't one as ought to give himself airs, marm.
cfrz10{_}2467{_}Why--"  Jim all at once dropped his chisel and his mask of indifference and flashed into ferocity.
cfrz10{_}2468{_}"Why, my father was a tradesman, he was, and I was in your husband's foundry earning a pound a week when Master Tom was in rags.
cfrz10{_}2469{_}Who taught him I should like to know?"
cfrz10{_}2470{_}"Jim, you must not talk like that; although, to tell you the truth, Tom is no favourite of mine.
cfrz10{_}2471{_}Mr. Furze, however, relies on him."
cfrz10{_}2472{_}"Relies on him, does he?
cfrz10{_}2473{_}Leastways, I know he does; just as if scores of others couldn't do jist as well, only they 'aven't 'ad his chance!
cfrz10{_}2474{_}Relies on him, as yer call it!
cfrz10{_}2475{_}But there, if I wur to speak, wot 'ud be the use?"
cfrz10{_}2476{_}It is always a consolation to incapable people that their lack of success is due to the absence of chances.
cfrz10{_}2477{_}From the time of Korah, Dathan, and Abiram--who accused Moses and Aaron of taking too much upon themselves, because every man in the congregation was as holy as his God-selected leaders--it has been a theory, one may even say a religion, with those who have been passed over, that their sole reason for their super-session is an election as arbitrary as that by the Antinomian deity, who, out of pure wilfulness, gives opportunities to some and denies them to others.
cfrz10{_}2478{_}"What do you mean, Jim?
cfrz10{_}2479{_}What is it that you see?"
cfrz10{_}2480{_}"You'll excuse me, missus, if I says no more.
cfrz10{_}2481{_}I ain't a-goin' to meddle with wot don't concern me, and get myself into trouble for nothing:  wot for, I should like to know?
cfrz10{_}2482{_}Wot good would it do me?"
cfrz10{_}2483{_}"But, Jim, if you are aware of anything wrong it is your duty to report it."
cfrz10{_}2484{_}"Maybe it is, maybe it isn't; but wot thanks should I get?"
cfrz10{_}2485{_}"You would get my thanks and the thanks of Mr. Furze, I am sure.
cfrz10{_}2486{_}Look here, Jim."
cfrz10{_}2487{_}Mrs. Furze rose and shut the kitchen door.
cfrz10{_}2488{_}Phoebe was upstairs, but she thought it necessary to take every precaution.
cfrz10{_}2489{_}"I know you may be trusted, and therefore I do not mind speaking to you.
cfrz10{_}2490{_}Tom's conduct has not been very satisfactory of late.
cfrz10{_}2491{_}I need not go into particulars, but I shall really be glad if you will communicate to me anything you may observe which is amiss.
cfrz10{_}2492{_}You may depend upon it you shall not suffer."
cfrz10{_}2493{_}She put two half-crowns into Jim's hand.
cfrz10{_}2494{_}He turned and looked at her with one eye partly shut, and a curious expression on his face-- half smile, half suspicion.
cfrz10{_}2495{_}He then looked at the money for a few seconds and put it deliberately in his pocket, but without any sign of gratitude.
cfrz10{_}2496{_}"I'll bear wot you say in mind," he replied.
cfrz10{_}2497{_}At this instant the kitchen door opened, and Phoebe entered.
cfrz10{_}2498{_}Mrs. Furze went on with the conversation immediately, but it took a different turn.
cfrz10{_}2499{_}"How do you think the old boiler became cracked?"
cfrz10{_}2500{_}He was taken aback; his muddled brain did not quite comprehend the situation, but at last he managed to stammer out that he did not know, and Mrs. Furze retired.
cfrz10{_}2501{_}Jim was very slow in arranging his thoughts, especially after a sudden surprise.
cfrz10{_}2502{_}A shock, or a quick intellectual movement on the part of anybody in contact with him, paralysed him, and he recovered and extended himself very gradually.
cfrz10{_}2503{_}Presently, however, his wits returned, and he concluded that the pretext of the shop and business mismanagement was but very partially the cause of Mrs. Furze's advances.
cfrz10{_}2504{_}He knew that although Mr. Furze was restive under Tom's superior capacity, there was no doubt whatever of his honesty and ability.
cfrz10{_}2505{_}Besides, if it was business, why did the mistress interfere?
cfrz10{_}2506{_}Why did she thrust herself upon him?--"coming down 'ere a purpose," thought Mr. Orkid Jim.
cfrz10{_}2507{_}"No, no, it ain't business," and, delighted with his discovery so far, and with the conscious exercise of mental power, he smote the bricks with more vigour than ever.
cfrz10{_}2508{_}"Good-bye, Phoebe," said Catharine, looking in at the door.
cfrz10{_}2509{_}"Good-bye, Miss," said Phoebe, running out; "hope you'll enjoy yourself:  I wish I were going with you."
cfrz10{_}2510{_}"Where is she a-goin'?"
cfrz10{_}2511{_}asked Jim, when Phoebe returned.
cfrz10{_}2512{_}"Chapel Farm."
cfrz10{_}2513{_}"Oh, is she?
cfrz10{_}2514{_}Wot, goin' there agin!
cfrz10{_}2515{_}She's oftener there than here.
cfrz10{_}2516{_}Not much love lost 'twixt her and the missus, is there?"
cfrz10{_}2517{_}Phoebe was uncommunicative, and went on with her work.
cfrz10{_}2518{_}"I say, Phoebe, has Catchpole been up here lately?"
cfrz10{_}2519{_}"Why do you want to know?
cfrz10{_}2520{_}What is it to you?"
cfrz10{_}2521{_}"Now, my beauty, wot is it to me?
cfrz10{_}2522{_}Why, in course it's nothin' to me; but you know he's been here."
cfrz10{_}2523{_}"Well, then, he hasn't."
cfrz10{_}2524{_}Phoebe, going to bed, had seen Tom and Catharine outside the gate.
cfrz10{_}2525{_}"Wy, now, I myself see'd 'im out the night afore last, and I'd swear he come this way afore he went home."
cfrz10{_}2526{_}"He did not come in; he only brought Miss Catharine back from church:  she'd gone there alone."
cfrz10{_}2527{_}Jim dropped his chisel.
cfrz10{_}2528{_}The three events presented themselves together--Tom's escort of Catharine, the interview with Mrs. Furze, and the departure to Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}2529{_}He was excited, and his excitement took the form of a sudden passion for Phoebe.
cfrz10{_}2530{_}"You're ten times too 'ansom for that chap," he cried, and turning suddenly, he caught her with one arm round her waist.
cfrz10{_}2531{_}She strove to release herself with great energy, and in the struggle he caught his foot in his tool basket and fell on the floor, cutting his head severely with a brick.
cfrz10{_}2532{_}Phoebe was out of the kitchen in an instant.
cfrz10{_}2533{_}"You damned cat!"
cfrz10{_}2534{_}growled he, "I'll be even with you and your Master Tom!
cfrz10{_}2535{_}I know all about it now."
cfrz10{_}2536{_}CHAPTER XV    As Jim walked home to his dinner he became pensive.
cfrz10{_}2537{_}He was under a kind of pledge to his own hatred and to Mrs. Furze to produce something against Tom, and he had nothing.
cfrz10{_}2538{_}Even he could see that to make up a charge would not be safe.
cfrz10{_}2539{_}It required more skill than he possessed.
cfrz10{_}2540{_}The opportunity, however, very soon came.
cfrz10{_}2541{_}Destiny delights in offering to the wicked chances of damning themselves.
cfrz10{_}2542{_}It was a few days before the end of the quarter.
cfrz10{_}2543{_}The builder--in whose service Jim's brother, Joe, was--sent Joe to pay a small account for ironmongery, which had been due for some weeks.
cfrz10{_}2544{_}When he entered the shop Tom was behind his desk, and Jim was taking some instructions about a job.
cfrz10{_}2545{_}Mr. Furze was out.
cfrz10{_}2546{_}Joe produced his bill, threw it across to Tom, and pulled the money out of his pocket.
cfrz10{_}2547{_}It was also market day; the town was crowded, and just at that moment Mr. Eaton drove by.
cfrz10{_}2548{_}Tom looked out of the window on his left hand and saw the horse shy at something in the cattle pens, pitching Mr. Eaton out.
cfrz10{_}2549{_}Without saying a word he rushed round the counter and out into the street, the two men, who had not seen the accident, thinking he had gone to speak to Mr. Eaton.
cfrz10{_}2550{_}He was absent some minutes.
cfrz10{_}2551{_}"A nice sort of a chap, this," said Jim; "he's signed your bill, and he ain't got the money."
cfrz10{_}2552{_}"S'pose I must wait, then."
cfrz10{_}2553{_}"Look 'ere, Joe:  don't you be a b---y fool!
cfrz10{_}2554{_}You take your account.
cfrz10{_}2555{_}If he writes his name afore he's paid, that's HIS look-out."
cfrz10{_}2556{_}Joe hesitated.
cfrz10{_}2557{_}"Wot are you a-starin' at?
cfrz10{_}2558{_}You've got the receipt, ain't yer?
cfrz10{_}2559{_}Isn't that enough?
cfrz10{_}2560{_}You ain't a-robbin' of him, for you never giv him the money, and I tell yer agin as he's the one as ought to lose if he don't look sharp arter people.
cfrz10{_}2561{_}That's square enough, ain't it?"
cfrz10{_}2562{_}Joe had a remarkably open mind to reasoning of this description, and, without another word, he took up the bill and was off.
cfrz10{_}2563{_}Jim also thought it better to return to the foundry.
cfrz10{_}2564{_}Mr. Eaton, happily, was not injured, for he fell on a truss of straw, but the excitement was great; and, when Tom returned, Joe's visit completely went out of his head, and did not occur to him again, for two or three customers were waiting for him, and, as already observed, it was market day.
cfrz10{_}2565{_}Now, it was Mr. Furze's practice always to make out his accounts himself.
cfrz10{_}2566{_}It was a pure waste of time, for he would have been much better employed in looking after his men, and any boy could have transcribed his ledger.
cfrz10{_}2567{_}But no, it was characteristic of the man that he preferred this occupation--that he took the utmost pains to write his best copybook hand, and to rule red-ink lines with mathematical accuracy.
cfrz10{_}2568{_}Two days after the quarter a bill went to the builder, beginning, "To account delivered."
cfrz10{_}2569{_}The builder was astonished, and instantly posted down to the shop, receipt in hand, signed, "For J. Furze, T. C."  Mr. Furze looked at his ledger again, called for the day-book, found no entry, and then sent for Tom.
cfrz10{_}2570{_}The history of that afternoon flashed across him in an instant.
cfrz10{_}2571{_}"That's your signature, Mr. Catchpole," said Mr. Furze.
cfrz10{_}2572{_}"Yes, sir."
cfrz10{_}2573{_}"But here's no entry in the day-book, and, what's more, there weren't thirty shillings that night in the till."
cfrz10{_}2574{_}"I cannot account for it, unless I signed the receipt before I had the money.
cfrz10{_}2575{_}It was just when Mr. Eaton's accident happened, and I ran out of the shop while Joe was waiting.
cfrz10{_}2576{_}When I came back he had gone."
cfrz10{_}2577{_}"Which is as much as to say," said the builder, "that Joe's a thief.
cfrz10{_}2578{_}You'd better be careful, young man."
cfrz10{_}2579{_}"Well, Mr. Humphries," said Mr. Furze, loftily, "we will not detain you:  there is clearly a mistake somewhere; we will credit you at once with the amount due for the previous quarter, and if you will give me your account I will correct it now."
cfrz10{_}2580{_}Mr. Furze took it, and ruled through the first line, altering the total.
cfrz10{_}2581{_}"This is very unpleasant, Mr. Catchpole," observed Mr. Furze, after the builder had departed.
cfrz10{_}2582{_}"Was there anybody in the shop besides yourself and Joe?"
cfrz10{_}2583{_}"Jim was there."
cfrz10{_}2584{_}Mr. Furze rang a bell, and Jim presently appeared.
cfrz10{_}2585{_}"Jim, were you in the shop when your brother came to pay Mr. Humphries' bill about a week ago?"
cfrz10{_}2586{_}"I wor."
cfrz10{_}2587{_}"Did he pay it?
cfrz10{_}2588{_}did you see him hand over the money?"
cfrz10{_}2589{_}"I did, and Mr. Catchpole took it and put it in the till.
cfrz10{_}2590{_}I see'd it go in with my own eyes."
cfrz10{_}2591{_}"Well, what happened then?"
cfrz10{_}2592{_}"He locked the till all in a hurry, put the key in his waistcoat pocket; let me see, it wor in his left-hand pocket--no, wot am I a- sayin'?--it wor in his right-hand pocket--I want to be particklar, Mr. Furze--and then he run out of the shop.
cfrz10{_}2593{_}Joe, he took up his receipt, and he says, says he, 'He might a given me the odd penny,' and says I, 'He ain't Mr. Furze, he can't give away none of the guvnor's money.
cfrz10{_}2594{_}If it wor the guvnor himself he'd a done it,' and with that we went out of the shop together."
cfrz10{_}2595{_}"That will do, Jim; you can go."
cfrz10{_}2596{_}"Mr. Catchpole, this assumes a very--I may say--painful aspect."
cfrz10{_}2597{_}"I can only repeat, sir, that I have not had the money.
cfrz10{_}2598{_}It is inexplicable.
cfrz10{_}2599{_}I may have been robbed."
cfrz10{_}2600{_}"But there is no entry in the day-book."
cfrz10{_}2601{_}It did not occur to Tom at the moment to plead that if he was dishonest he would have contrived not to be so in such a singularly silly fashion:  that he might have taken cash paid for goods bought, and that the possibility of discovery would have been much smaller.
cfrz10{_}2602{_}He was stunned.
cfrz10{_}2603{_}"It is so painful," continued Mr. Furze, "that I must have time to reflect.
cfrz10{_}2604{_}I will talk to you again about it to-morrow."
cfrz10{_}2605{_}The truth was that Mr. Furze wished to consult his wife.
cfrz10{_}2606{_}When he went home his first news was what had happened, but he forgot to mention the corroboration by Jim.
cfrz10{_}2607{_}"But," said Mrs. Furze, "Joe may have been mistaken; perhaps, after all, he did not pay the money."
cfrz10{_}2608{_}"Ah!
cfrz10{_}2609{_}but Jim was in the shop at the time.
cfrz10{_}2610{_}I had Jim in, and he swears that he saw Joe give it to Tom, and that Tom put it in the till."
cfrz10{_}2611{_}Mrs. Furze seemed a little uncomfortable, but she soon recovered.
cfrz10{_}2612{_}"We ought to have proof beyond all doubt of Tom's dishonesty.
cfrz10{_}2613{_}I do not see that this is proof.
cfrz10{_}2614{_}At any rate, it would not satisfy Catharine.
cfrz10{_}2615{_}I should wait a month.
cfrz10{_}2616{_}It is of no use making two faces about this business; we must take one line or the other.
cfrz10{_}2617{_}I should tell him that, on reconsideration, you cannot bring yourself to suspect him; that you have perfect confidence in him, and that there must be some mistake somewhere, though you cannot at present see how.
cfrz10{_}2618{_}That will throw him off his guard."
cfrz10{_}2619{_}Mr. Furze acknowledged the superiority of his wife's intellect and obeyed.
cfrz10{_}2620{_}Tom came to work on the following morning in a state of great excitement, and with an offer of restitution, but was appeased, and Orkid Jim, appearing in the shop, was astonished and dismayed to find Tom and his master on the same footing as before.
cfrz10{_}2621{_}He went up to the Terrace, the excuse being that he called to see how the new boiler was going on.
cfrz10{_}2622{_}Phoebe came to the door, but he wanted to see the mistress.
cfrz10{_}2623{_}"What do you want her for?
cfrz10{_}2624{_}She knows nothing about the boiler.
cfrz10{_}2625{_}It is all right, I tell you."
cfrz10{_}2626{_}"Never you mind.
cfrz10{_}2627{_}It wor she as give me the directions, worn't it, when I was 'ere afore?"
cfrz10{_}2628{_}Accordingly the mistress appeared, and Phoebe, remaining in the kitchen, was sent upstairs upon some important business, much cogitating upon the unusual interest Mrs. Furze took in the kitchen range, and the evident desire on her part that her instructions to Jim should be private.
cfrz10{_}2629{_}"Well, Jim, the boiler is all right."
cfrz10{_}2630{_}"That's more nor some things are."
cfrz10{_}2631{_}"Why, what has happened?"
cfrz10{_}2632{_}"I s'pose you know.
cfrz10{_}2633{_}Joe paid Humphries' bill, and Mr. Catchpole swears he never had the money, but Joe's got his receipt."
cfrz10{_}2634{_}"You were in the shop and saw it paid?"
cfrz10{_}2635{_}"Of course I was.
cfrz10{_}2636{_}I s'pose you heerd that too?"
cfrz10{_}2637{_}"Yes.
cfrz10{_}2638{_}We do not think, however, that the case is clear, and we shall do nothing this time."
cfrz10{_}2639{_}"I don't know wot you'd 'ave, Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}2640{_}If this ere ain't worth the five shillin' yer gave me, nothin' is--that's all I've got to say."
cfrz10{_}2641{_}"But, Jim, you must see we cannot do anything unless the proof is complete.
cfrz10{_}2642{_}Now, if there should happen to be a second instance, that would be a different thing altogether."
cfrz10{_}2643{_}"It ain't very comfortable for ME."
cfrz10{_}2644{_}"What do you mean?
cfrz10{_}2645{_}Mr. Furze sent for you, and you told him what you saw with your own eyes."
cfrz10{_}2646{_}"Ah!
cfrz10{_}2647{_}you'd better mind wot you're sayin', Mrs. Furze, and you needn't put it in that way.
cfrz10{_}2648{_}Jist you look 'ere:  I ain't very particklar myself, I ain't, but it may come to takin' my oath, and, to tell yer the truth, five shillin' don't pay me."
cfrz10{_}2649{_}"But we are not going to prosecute."
cfrz10{_}2650{_}"No, not now, but you may, and I shall have to stick to it, and maybe have to be brought up.
