cfrz10{_}2652{_}As I say, five shillin' don't pay me."
cfrz10{_}2653{_}"Well, we shall not let the matter drop.
cfrz10{_}2654{_}We shall keep our eyes open:  you may be sure of that, Jim.
cfrz10{_}2655{_}I dare say you have been worried over the business.
cfrz10{_}2656{_}Here's another five shillings for you."
cfrz10{_}2657{_}Again Jim refrained from thanking her, but slowly put on his cap and left the house.
cfrz10{_}2658{_}CHAPTER XVI    Mr. Furze tried several experiments during the next two or three weeks.
cfrz10{_}2659{_}It was his custom to look after his shop when Tom went to his meals, and on those rare occasions when he had to go out during Tom's absence, Orkid Jim acted as a substitute.
cfrz10{_}2660{_}Whenever Mr. Furze found a sovereign in the till he quietly marked it with his knife or a filet but it was invariably handed over to him in the evening.
cfrz10{_}2661{_}On a certain Wednesday afternoon, Tom being at his dinner, Mr. Furze was summoned to the Bell by a message from Mr. Eaton, and Jim was ordered to come immediately.
cfrz10{_}2662{_}He usually went round to the front door.
cfrz10{_}2663{_}He preferred to walk down the lane from the foundry, and when the back rooms were living rooms, passage through them was of course forbidden.
cfrz10{_}2664{_}As the summons, however, was urgent, he came the shortest way, and, looking in through the window which let in some borrowed light from the back of the shop to the warehouse behind, he saw Mr. Furze, penknife in hand, at the till.
cfrz10{_}2665{_}Wondering what he could be doing, Jim watched him for a moment.
cfrz10{_}2666{_}As soon as Mr. Furze's back was turned he went to the till, took out a sovereign which was in it, closely examined it, discovered a distinct though faint cross at the back of his Majesty George the Third's head, pondered a moment, and then put the coin back again.
cfrz10{_}2667{_}He looked very abstruse, rubbed his chin, and finally smiled after his fashion.
cfrz10{_}2668{_}Tom's shop coat and waistcoat were hung up just inside the counting-house.
cfrz10{_}2669{_}Jim went to them and turned the waistcoat pockets inside out.
cfrz10{_}2670{_}To put the sovereign in an empty pocket would be dangerous.
cfrz10{_}2671{_}Tom would discover it as soon as he returned, and would probably inform Mr. Furze at once.
cfrz10{_}2672{_}A similar test for the future would then be impossible.
cfrz10{_}2673{_}Jim thought of a better plan, and it was strange that so slow a brain was so quick to conceive it.
cfrz10{_}2674{_}Along one particular line, however, that brain, otherwise so dull, was even rapid in its movements.
cfrz10{_}2675{_}It was Mr. Furze's practice to pay wages at half-past five on Saturday afternoon, and he paid them himself.
cfrz10{_}2676{_}He generally went to his tea at six on that day, Tom waiting till he returned.
cfrz10{_}2677{_}On the following Saturday at half-past six Jim came into the shop.
cfrz10{_}2678{_}"I met Eaton's man a minute ago as I wur goin' 'ome.
cfrz10{_}2679{_}He wanted to see the guvnor particklar, he said."
cfrz10{_}2680{_}This was partly true, but the "particklar" was not true.
cfrz10{_}2681{_}"I told him the guvnor warn't in, but you was there.
cfrz10{_}2682{_}He said he was goin' to the Bell, but he'd call again if he had time.
cfrz10{_}2683{_}You'd better go and see wot it is."
cfrz10{_}2684{_}Tom took off his black apron and his shop coat and waistcoat, put them up in the usual place, and went out, leaving Jim in charge.
cfrz10{_}2685{_}Jim instantly went to the till.
cfrz10{_}2686{_}There were several sovereigns in it, for it had been a busy day.
cfrz10{_}2687{_}He turned them over, and again recognised the indubitable cross.
cfrz10{_}2688{_}With a swift promptitude utterly beyond his ordinary self, he again went to Tom's waistcoat--Tom always put gold in his waistcoat pocket--took out a sovereign of the thirty shillings there, put it in his own pocket, and replaced it by the marked sovereign.
cfrz10{_}2689{_}Just before the shop closed, the cash was taken to Mr. Furze.
cfrz10{_}2690{_}He tied it carefully in a bag, carried it home, turned it over, and the sovereign was absent.
cfrz10{_}2691{_}Meanwhile Orkid Jim had begun to reflect that the chain of evidence was not complete.
cfrz10{_}2692{_}He knew Tom's habits perfectly, and one of them was to buy his Sunday's dinner on Saturday night.
cfrz10{_}2693{_}He generally went to a small butcher near his own house.
cfrz10{_}2694{_}Jim followed him, having previously exchanged his own sovereign for twenty shillings in silver.
cfrz10{_}2695{_}As soon as Tom had left the butcher's shop Jim walked in.
cfrz10{_}2696{_}He was well known.
cfrz10{_}2697{_}"Mr. Butterfield, you 'aven't got a sovereign, 'ave you, as you could give me for twenty shillings in silver?"

cfrz10{_}2699{_}Besides, we don't take many sovereigns here--we ain't like people in the High Street."

cfrz10{_}2701{_}I don't get much enjoyment, Mr. Butterfield, but I mean to 'ave it then."

cfrz10{_}2730{_}Mr. Furze lost no time in communicating his discovery to his wife.

cfrz10{_}2735{_}Mr. Furze was crestfallen, and made no reply.
cfrz10{_}2736{_}The next morning at church he was picturing to himself incessantly the dreadful moment when he would have to do something so totally unlike anything he had ever done before.

cfrz10{_}2738{_}"What can the man want?

cfrz10{_}2774{_}"That will be much better."
cfrz10{_}2775{_}It was now tea-time, and immediately afterwards Mr. and Mrs. Furze went to church.
cfrz10{_}2776{_}Soon after nine on the following morning, and before Mr. Furze had left, Jim appeared with another request "to see the missus."

cfrz10{_}2779{_}There was nobody but Jim in the kitchen.
cfrz10{_}2780{_}"Well, Jim?"

cfrz10{_}2791{_}Yes, of course you are.
cfrz10{_}2792{_}Wot are yer goin' to do with him?"

cfrz10{_}2794{_}"No, marm, you take my advice, don't yer do that; it wouldn't do nobody no good."
cfrz10{_}2795{_}"We shall discharge him at once."
cfrz10{_}2796{_}"Yes, that's all right; but don't you prosecute 'im on no account, mind that.

cfrz10{_}2801{_}Jim was unusually excited.

cfrz10{_}2804{_}In one short minute she lived a lifetime, and the decision was taken which determined her destiny.
cfrz10{_}2805{_}She resolved that she would NOT tread one single step in one particular direction, nor even look that way.
cfrz10{_}2806{_}She did not resolve to tell a lie, or, in fact, to do anything which was not strictly defensible and virtuous.
cfrz10{_}2807{_}She simply refused to reflect on the possibility of perjury on Jim's part.
cfrz10{_}2808{_}Refusing to reflect on it, she naturally had no proof of it; and, having no proof of it, she had no ground for believing that she was not perfectly innocent and upright--a very pretty process, much commoner than perhaps might be suspected.
cfrz10{_}2809{_}After the lapse of two or three hours there was in fact no test by which to distinguish the validity of this belief from that of her other beliefs, nor indeed, it may be said, from that of the beliefs in which many people live, and for the sake of which they die.

cfrz10{_}2814{_}The reward was brilliant, and he saw what course he ought to take.
cfrz10{_}2815{_}"Thank yer, marm; it was very lucky; now I may speak freely I may say as I've 'ad my eyes on Mr. Catchpole ever so long.
cfrz10{_}2816{_}I told yer as much afore, and this ain't the fust time as he's robbed yer, but I couldn't prove it, and it worn't no good my sayin' wot I worn't sure of."
cfrz10{_}2817{_}This, then, is the way in which Destiny rewards those who refuse to listen to the Divine Voice.
cfrz10{_}2818{_}Destiny supplies them with reasons for discrediting it.
cfrz10{_}2819{_}Mrs. Furze was more than ever thankful to Jim; not so much because of these additional revelations, but because she was still further released from the obligation to turn her eyes.
cfrz10{_}2820{_}Had not Jim said it once, twice, and now thrice?
cfrz10{_}2821{_}Who could condemn her?
cfrz10{_}2822{_}She boldly faced herself, and asked herself what authority this other self possessed which, just for a moment, whispered something in her ear.

cfrz10{_}2826{_}The business of this other self is suggestion only, and, if it be resisted, it is either dumb or will reply just as it is bidden.
cfrz10{_}2827{_}"You can tell Mr. Catchpole his master wishes to see him here."

cfrz10{_}2838{_}I could not tell who the--delinquent--might be, and, under advice, under advice, I resorted to the usual means of detection, and the result is that a marked coin placed in the till on Saturday was changed by you on Saturday night."

cfrz10{_}2849{_}"I will ask you both"--he spoke with deliberation and emphasis--"do you, both of you, believe I am a thief?"

cfrz10{_}2852{_}You seem rather absurd, Mr.

cfrz10{_}2856{_}"Then I tell you, Mrs. Furze, and I tell you, Mr. Furze, before the all-knowing God, who is in this room at this moment, that I am utterly innocent, and that somebody has wickedly lied."

cfrz10{_}2883{_}Before noon her box was packed, and she too had departed.
cfrz10{_}2884{_}CHAPTER XVII    Tom began to understand, as soon as he left the Terrace, that a consciousness of his own innocence was not all that was necessary for his peace of mind.
cfrz10{_}2885{_}What would other people say?
cfrz10{_}2886{_}There was a damning chain of evidence, and what was he to do for a living with no character?
cfrz10{_}2887{_}He did not return home nor to the shop.
cfrz10{_}2888{_}He took the road to Chapel Farm.
cfrz10{_}2889{_}He did not go to the house direct, but went round it, and walked about, and at last found himself on the bridge.
cfrz10{_}2890{_}It was there that he met Catharine after her jump into the water; it was there, although he knew nothing about it, that she parted from Mr. Cardew.
cfrz10{_}2891{_}It was no thundery, summer day now, but cold and dark.
cfrz10{_}2892{_}The wind was north-east, persistent with unvarying force; the sky was covered with an almost uniform sheet of heavy grey clouds, with no form or beauty in them; there was nothing in the heavens or earth which seemed to have any relationship with man or to show any interest in him.
cfrz10{_}2893{_}Tom was not a philosopher, but some of his misery was due to a sense of carelessness and injustice somewhere in the government of the world.
cfrz10{_}2894{_}He was religious after his fashion, but the time had passed when a man could believe, as his forefathers believed, that the earth is a school of trial, and that after death is the judgment.
cfrz10{_}2895{_}What had he done to be visited thus?
cfrz10{_}2896{_}How was his integrity to be discovered?
cfrz10{_}2897{_}He had often thought that it was possible that a man should be convicted of some dreadful crime; that he should be execrated, not only by the whole countryside, but by his own wife and children; that his descendants for ages might curse him as the solitary ancestor who had brought disgrace into the family, and that he might be innocent.
cfrz10{_}2898{_}There might be hundreds of such; doubtless there have been.
cfrz10{_}2899{_}Perhaps, even worse, there have been men who have been misinterpreted, traduced, forsaken, because they have been compelled for a reason sacredly secret to take a certain course which seemed disreputable, and the word which would have explained everything they have loyally sworn, for the sake of a friend, never to speak, and it has remained unspoken for ever.
cfrz10{_}2900{_}As he stood leaning over the parapet he saw Catharine coming along the path.
cfrz10{_}2901{_}She did not attempt to avoid him, for she wandered what he could be doing.
cfrz10{_}2902{_}He told her the whole story.
cfrz10{_}2903{_}"Miss Catharine, there is just one thing I want to know:  do you believe I am guilty?"
cfrz10{_}2904{_}"I know you are not."
cfrz10{_}2905{_}"Thank God for that."
cfrz10{_}2906{_}Both remained silent for a minute or two.
cfrz10{_}2907{_}At last Tom spoke.
cfrz10{_}2908{_}"Oh, Miss Catharine, this makes it harder to bear.
cfrz10{_}2909{_}You are the one person, perhaps, in the world now who has any faith in me; there is, perhaps, no human being at this moment, excepting yourself, who, after having heard what you have heard, would at once put it all aside.
cfrz10{_}2910{_}What do you suppose I think of you now?
cfrz10{_}2911{_}If I loved you before, what must my love now be?
cfrz10{_}2912{_}Miss Catharine, I could tear out my heart for you, and if you can trust me so much, why can you not love me too?
cfrz10{_}2913{_}What is it that prevents your love?
cfrz10{_}2914{_}Why cannot I alter it?
cfrz10{_}2915{_}And yet, what am I saying?
cfrz10{_}2916{_}You may think me honest, but how can I expect you to take a discharged felon?"
cfrz10{_}2917{_}Catharine knew what Tom did not know.
cfrz10{_}2918{_}She was perfectly sure that the accusation against him was the result of the supposed discovery of their love for one another.
cfrz10{_}2919{_}If she had denied it promptly nothing perhaps would have happened.
cfrz10{_}2920{_}It was all due to her, then.
cfrz10{_}2921{_}She gazed up the stream; the leaden clouds drove on; the leaden water lay rippled; the willows and the rushes, vexed with the bitter blast, bent themselves continually.
cfrz10{_}2922{_}She turned and took her ring off her finger.
cfrz10{_}2923{_}"It can never be," she slowly said; "here is my ring; you may keep it, but while I am alive you must never wear it."
cfrz10{_}2924{_}Tom took it mechanically, bent his head over the parapet, and his anguish broke out in sobs and tears.
cfrz10{_}2925{_}Catharine took his hand in hers, leaned over him, and whispered:  "Tom, listen--I shall never be any man's wife."
cfrz10{_}2926{_}Before he could say another word she had gone, and he felt that he should never see her again.
cfrz10{_}2927{_}What makes the peculiar pang of parting?
cfrz10{_}2928{_}The coach comes up; the friend mounts; there is the wave of a handkerchief.
cfrz10{_}2929{_}I follow him to the crest of the hill; he disappears, and I am left to walk down the dusty lane alone.
cfrz10{_}2930{_}Am I melancholy simply because I shall not see him for a month or a year?
cfrz10{_}2931{_}She whom I have loved for half a life lies dying.
cfrz10{_}2932{_}I kiss her and bid her good-bye.
cfrz10{_}2933{_}Is the bare loss the sole cause of my misery, my despair, breeding that mad longing that I myself might die?
cfrz10{_}2934{_}In all parting there is something infinite.
cfrz10{_}2935{_}We see in it a symbol of the order of the universe, and it is because that death-bed farewell stands for so much that we break down.
cfrz10{_}2936{_}"If it pleases God," says Swift to Pope, "to restore me to my health, I shall readily make a third journey; if not, we must part AS ALL HUMAN CREATURES HAVE PARTED."
cfrz10{_}2937{_}As all human creatures have parted!
cfrz10{_}2938{_}Swift did not say that by way of consolation.
cfrz10{_}2939{_}Tom turned homewards.
cfrz10{_}2940{_}Catharine's last words were incessantly in his mind.
cfrz10{_}2941{_}What they meant he knew not and could not imagine, but in the midst of his trouble rose up something not worth calling joy, a little thread of water in the waste:  it was a little relief that nobody was preferred before him, and that nobody would possess what to him was denied.
cfrz10{_}2942{_}He told his father, and found his faith unshakable.
cfrz10{_}2943{_}There was a letter for him in a handwriting he thought he knew, but he was not quite sure.
cfrz10{_}2944{_}It was as follows:-   "DEAR MR. CATCHPOLE,--I hope you will excuse the liberty I have taken in writing to you.
cfrz10{_}2945{_}I have left my place at the Terrace.
cfrz10{_}2946{_}I cannot help sending these few lines to say that Orkid Jim has been causing mischief here, and if he's had anything to do with your going he's a liar.
cfrz10{_}2947{_}It was all because I wouldn't go to the door and let him in, and gave missus a bit of my mind about him that I had notice.
cfrz10{_}2948{_}I wasn't sorry, however, for my cough is bad, and I couldn't stand running up and down those Terrace stairs.
cfrz10{_}2949{_}It was different at the shop.
cfrz10{_}2950{_}I thought I should just like to let you know that whatever missus and master may say, I'M sure you have done nothing but what is quite straight.
cfrz10{_}2951{_}"Yours truly, "PHOEBE CROWHURST."
cfrz10{_}2952{_}Tom was grateful to Phoebe, and he put her letter in his pocket:  it remained there for some time:  it then came out with one or two other papers, was accidentally burnt with them, and was never answered.
cfrz10{_}2953{_}Day after day poor Phoebe watched the postman, but nothing came.
cfrz10{_}2954{_}She wondered if she had made any mistake in the address, but she had not the courage to write again.
cfrz10{_}2955{_}"He may be very much taken up," thought she, "but he might have sent me just a line;" and then she felt ashamed, and wished she had not written, and would have given the world to have her letter back again.
cfrz10{_}2956{_}She had been betrayed into a little tenderness which met with no response.
cfrz10{_}2957{_}She was only a housemaid, and yet when she said to herself that maybe she had been too forward, the blood came to her cheeks; beautifully, too beautifully white they were.
cfrz10{_}2958{_}Poor Phoebe!
cfrz10{_}2959{_}Tom met Mr. Cardew in Eastthorpe the evening after the interview with Catharine, and told him his story.
cfrz10{_}2960{_}"I am ruined," he said:  "I have no character."
cfrz10{_}2961{_}"Wait a minute; come with me into the Bell where my horse is."
cfrz10{_}2962{_}They went into the coffee-room, and Mr. Cardew took a sheet of note- paper and wrote:-   "MY DEAR ROBERT,--The bearer of this note, Mr. Thomas Catchpole, is well known to me as a perfectly honest man, and he thoroughly understands his business.
cfrz10{_}2963{_}He is coming to London, and I hope you will consider it your duty to obtain remunerative employment for him.
cfrz10{_}2964{_}He has been wickedly accused of a crime of which he is as innocent as I am, and this is an additional reason why you should exert yourself on his behalf.
cfrz10{_}2965{_}"Your affectionate cousin,  "THEOPHILUS CARDEW.
cfrz10{_}2966{_}"TO ROBERT BERDOE, Esq., "Clapham Common."
cfrz10{_}2967{_}Mr. Cardew married a Berdoe, it will be remembered, and this Robert Berdoe was a wealthy wholesale ironmonger, who carried on business in Southwark.
cfrz10{_}2968{_}"You had better leave Eastthorpe, Mr. Catchpole, and take your father with you.
cfrz10{_}2969{_}Are you in want of any money?"
cfrz10{_}2970{_}"No, sir, thank you; I have saved a little.
cfrz10{_}2971{_}I cannot speak very well, Mr. Cardew; you know I cannot; I cannot say to you what I ought."
cfrz10{_}2972{_}"I want no thanks, my dear friend.
cfrz10{_}2973{_}What I do is a simple duty.
cfrz10{_}2974{_}I am a minister of God's Word, and I know no obligation more pressing which He has laid upon me than that of bearing witness to the truth."
cfrz10{_}2975{_}Mr. Cardew went off as usual away from what was before him.
cfrz10{_}2976{_}"The duty of Christ's minister is, generally speaking, TO TAKE THE OTHER SIDE--that is to say, to resist the verdicts passed by the world upon men and things.
cfrz10{_}2977{_}Preaching mere abstractions, too, is not by itself of much use.
cfrz10{_}2978{_}What we are bound to do is not only to preserve the eternal standard, but to measure actual human beings and human deeds by it.
cfrz10{_}2979{_}I sometimes think, too, it is of more importance to say THIS IS RIGHT than to say THIS IS WRONG, to save that which is true than to assist into perdition that which is false.
cfrz10{_}2980{_}Especially ought we to defend character unjustly assailed.
cfrz10{_}2981{_}A character is something alive, a soul; to rescue it is the salvation of a soul!"
cfrz10{_}2982{_}He stopped and seemed to wake up suddenly.
cfrz10{_}2983{_}"Good-bye!
cfrz10{_}2984{_}God's blessing on you."
cfrz10{_}2985{_}He shook Tom's hand and was going out of the yard.
cfrz10{_}2986{_}"There is just one thing more, sir:  I do not want to leave Eastthorpe with such a character behind me--to leave in the dark, one may say, and not defend myself.
cfrz10{_}2987{_}It looks as if it were an admission I was wrong.
cfrz10{_}2988{_}I should, above everything, like to get to the bottom of it, and see who is the liar or what the mistake is."
cfrz10{_}2989{_}"Nobody would listen to you, and if you were to make a noise Mr. Furze might prosecute, and with the evidence he has we do not know what the end might be; I will do my part, as I am bound to do, to set you right.
cfrz10{_}2990{_}But, above everything, Mr. Catchpole, endeavour to put yourself where the condemnation of the world and even crucifixion by it are of no consequence."
cfrz10{_}2991{_}Mr. Cardew gave Tom one more shake of the hand, mounted his horse, and rode off.
cfrz10{_}2992{_}He had asked Tom for no proofs:  he had merely heard the tale and had given his certificate.
cfrz10{_}2993{_}Mr Furze distinctly enjoined Orkid Jim to hold his tongue.
cfrz10{_}2994{_}Neither Mr. nor Mrs. Furze wished to appear in court, and they were uncertain what Catharine might do if they went any further.
cfrz10{_}2995{_}Mr. Orkid Jim had the best of reasons for silence, but Mr. Humphries, the builder, of course repeated what he himself knew, and so it went about that Tom was wrong in his accounts, and all Eastthorpe affirmed him to be little better than a rascal.
cfrz10{_}2996{_}Mr. Cardew, with every tittle of much stronger and apparently irresistible testimony before him, never for a moment considered it as a feather's weight in the balance.
cfrz10{_}2997{_}"But the facts, my good sir, the facts; the facts--there they are: the receipt to the bill; Jim's declaration; his brother's declaration; the marked coin; the absolute proof that Catchpole gave it to Butterfield, and he could not, as some may think, have changed silver of his own for it, for Mr. Furze paid him in gold, and there was not twenty shillings worth of silver in the till; what HAVE you got to say?
cfrz10{_}2998{_}Do you tell me all this may be accident and coincidence?
cfrz10{_}2999{_}If you do, we may just as well give up reasoning and the whole of our criminal procedure."
cfrz10{_}3000{_}Mr. Cardew did know the facts, THE facts, and relying on them he delivered his judgment.
cfrz10{_}3001{_}Catharine, Phoebe, and Tom's father agreed with him--four jurors out of one thousand of full age; but the four were right and the nine hundred odd were wrong.
cfrz10{_}3002{_}In the four dwelt what aforetime would have been called faith, nothing magical, nothing superstitious, but really the noblest form of reason, for it is the ability to rest upon the one reality which is of value, neglecting all delusive appearances which may apparently contradict it.
cfrz10{_}3003{_}Tom left Eastthorpe the next morning, and on that day Catharine received the following letter from her mother:   "MY DEAR CATHARINE,--I write to tell you that we have made an awful discovery.
cfrz10{_}3004{_}Catchpole has appropriated money belonging to your father, and the evidence against him is complete.
cfrz10{_}3005{_}(Mrs. Furze then told the story.)
cfrz10{_}3006{_}You will now, my dear Catharine, be able, I hope, to do justice to your father and mother, and to understand their anxiety that you should form no connection with a man like this.
cfrz10{_}3007{_}It is true that on the morning when we spoke to you we did not know the extent of his guilt, but we had suspected him for some time.
cfrz10{_}3008{_}It is quite providential that the disclosure comes--at the present moment, and I hope it will detach you from him for ever.
cfrz10{_}3009{_}Your father and I send our love, and please assure Mr. and Mrs. Bellamy of our regard.
cfrz10{_}3010{_}"Your affectionate mother, "AMELIA FURZE."
cfrz10{_}3011{_}On the same morning Mr. Furze received the following note from Mr. Cardew:-   "DEAR SIR,--I regret to hear that a false charge has been preferred against my friend Mr. Catchpole.
cfrz10{_}3012{_}By my advice he has left Eastthorpe without any attempt to defend himself, but I consider it my duty to tell you he is innocent; that you have lost a faithful servant, and, what is worse, you have done him harm, not only in body, but in soul, for there are not many men who can be wrongfully accused and remain calm and resigned.
cfrz10{_}3013{_}You ask me on what evidence I acquit him.
cfrz10{_}3014{_}I know the whole story, but I also know him, and I know that he cannot lie.
cfrz10{_}3015{_}I beg you to consider what you do in branding as foul that which God has made good.
cfrz10{_}3016{_}I offer no apology for thus addressing you, for I am a minister of God's Word, and I have to do all that He bids.
cfrz10{_}3017{_}I should consider I was but a poor servant of the Most High if I did not protest against wrong-doing face to face with the doer of it.
cfrz10{_}3018{_}"Faithfully yours, "THEOPHILUS CARDEW."
cfrz10{_}3019{_}Both Mr. and Mrs. Furze Were greatly incensed, and Mr. Cardew received the following reply, due rather to Mrs. than to Mr. Furze -   "SIR,--I am greatly surprised at the receipt of your letter.
cfrz10{_}3020{_}You have taken up the cause of a servant against his master, and a dishonest servant, too:  you have taken it up with only an imperfect acquaintance with the case, and knowing nothing of it except from his representation.
cfrz10{_}3021{_}If you were the clergyman of this parish I might, perhaps, recognise your right to address me, although I am inclined to believe that the clergy do far more harm than good by meddling with matters outside their own sphere.
cfrz10{_}3022{_}How can we listen with respect to a minister who is occupied with worldly affairs rather than with those matters which befit his calling and concern our salvation?
cfrz10{_}3023{_}Sir, I must decline any discussion with you as to Mr. Catchpole's innocence or guilt, and respectfully deny your right to interfere.
cfrz10{_}3024{_}"I am, sir, "Your obedient servant, "J.
cfrz10{_}3025{_}FURZE."
cfrz10{_}3026{_}Catharine's first impulse was to go home instantly and vindicate Tom, but she did not move, and the letter remained unanswered.
cfrz10{_}3027{_}What could she say to her own parents which would meet the case or would be worthy of such a conspiracy?
cfrz10{_}3028{_}She would not be believed, and no good would be done.
cfrz10{_}3029{_}A stronger reason for not speaking was a certain pride and a determination to retaliate by silence, but the strongest of all reasons was a kind of collapse after she arrived at Chapel Farm, and the disappearance of all desire to fight.
cfrz10{_}3030{_}Her old cheerfulness began to depart, and a cloud to creep over her like the shadow of an eclipse.
cfrz10{_}3031{_}Young as she was, strange thoughts possessed her.
cfrz10{_}3032{_}The interval between the present moment and death appeared annihilated; life was a mere span; a day would go by and then a week, and in a few months, which could easily be counted, would come the end; nay, it was already out there, visible, approaching, and when she came to think what death really meant, the difference between right and wrong was worth nothing.
cfrz10{_}3033{_}Terrors, vague and misty possessed her, all the worse because they were not substantial.
cfrz10{_}3034{_}She could not put into words what ailed her, and she wrestled with shapeless clinging forms which she could hardly discern, and could not distangle from her, much less overthrow.
cfrz10{_}3035{_}They wound themselves about her, and although they were but shadows, they made her shriek, and at times she fainted under their grasp, and thought she could not survive.
cfrz10{_}3036{_}She had no peace.
cfrz10{_}3037{_}If soldiers lie dead upon a battle- field there is an end of them; new armies may be raised, but the enemy is at any rate weaker by those who are killed.
cfrz10{_}3038{_}It is not quite the same with our ghostly foes, for they rise into life after we think they are buried, and often with greater strength than ever.
cfrz10{_}3039{_}There is something awful in the obstinacy of the assaults upon us.
cfrz10{_}3040{_}Day after day, night after night, and perhaps year after year, the wretched citadel is environed, and the pressure of the attack is unremitting, while the force which resists has to be summoned by a direct effort of the will, and the moment that effort relaxes the force fails, and the besiegers swarm upon the fortifications.
cfrz10{_}3041{_}That which makes for our destruction, everything that is horrible, seems spontaneously active, and the opposition is an everlasting struggle.
cfrz10{_}3042{_}At last the effect upon Catharine's health was so obvious that Mrs. Bellamy was alarmed, and went over to Eastthorpe to see Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}3043{_}Mrs. Furze in her own mind instantly concluded that Tom was the cause of her daughter's trouble, but she did not mean to admit it to her.
cfrz10{_}3044{_}In a sense Tom was the cause; not that she loved him, but because her refusal of him brought it vividly before her that her life would be spent without love, or, at least, without a love which could be acknowledged.
cfrz10{_}3045{_}It was a crisis, for the pattern of her existence was henceforth settled, and she was to live not only without that which is sweetest for woman, but with no definite object before her.
cfrz10{_}3046{_}The force in woman is so great that something with which it can grapple, on which it can expend itself, is a necessity, and Catharine felt that her strength would have to occupy itself in twisting straws.
cfrz10{_}3047{_}It is really this which is the root of many a poor girl's suffering.
cfrz10{_}3048{_}As the world is arranged at present, there is too much power for the mills which have to be turned by it.
cfrz10{_}3049{_}Mrs. Furze requested Mrs. Bellamy to send back Catharine at once in order that a doctor might be consulted.
cfrz10{_}3050{_}She returned; she did not really much care where she was; and to the doctor she went.
cfrz10{_}3051{_}Dr. Turnbull was the gentleman selected.
cfrz10{_}3052{_}CHAPTER XVIII    Dr. Turnbull was the doctor who, it will be remembered, lived in the square near the church.
cfrz10{_}3053{_}There was another doctor in Eastthorpe, Mr. Butcher, of whom we have heard, but Dr. Turnbull's reputation as a doctor was far higher than Mr. Butcher's.
cfrz10{_}3054{_}What Eastthorpe thought of Dr. Turnbull as a man is another matter.
cfrz10{_}3055{_}Mr. Butcher was married, church-going, polite, smiling to everybody, and when he called he always said, "Well, and how are WE?"
cfrz10{_}3056{_}in such a nice way, identifying himself with his patient.
cfrz10{_}3057{_}But even Eastthorpe had not much faith in him, and in very serious cases always preferred Dr. Turnbull.
cfrz10{_}3058{_}Eastthorpe had remarked that Mr. Butcher's medicines had a curious similarity.
cfrz10{_}3059{_}He believed in two classes of diseases-- sthenic and asthenic.
cfrz10{_}3060{_}For the former he prescribed bleeding and purgatives; for the latter he "threw in" bark and iron, and ordered port wine.
cfrz10{_}3061{_}Eastthorpe thought him very fair for colds, measles, chicken-pox, and for rashes of all sorts, and so did all the country round.
cfrz10{_}3062{_}He generally attended everybody for such complaints, but as Mr. Gosford said after his recovery from a dangerous attack, "when it come to a stoppage, I thought I'd better have Turnbull," and Mr. Gosford sent for him promptly.
cfrz10{_}3063{_}Dr. Turnbull was born three or four years before the outbreak of the French Revolution.
cfrz10{_}3064{_}He was consequently a little older than the great Dr. Elliotson, whose memory some of us still piously cherish, and Dr. Elliotson and he were devoted friends.
cfrz10{_}3065{_}Dr. Turnbull was tall, thin, upright, with undimmed grey eyes and dark hair, which had hardly yet begun to turn in colour, but was a little worn off his forehead.
cfrz10{_}3066{_}He had a curiously piercing look in his face, so that it was impossible if you told him an untruth not to feel that you were detected.
cfrz10{_}3067{_}He never joked or laughed in the sickroom or in his consulting-room, and his words were few.
cfrz10{_}3068{_}But what was most striking in him was his mute power of command, so that everybody in contact with him did his bidding without any effort on his part.
cfrz10{_}3069{_}He kept three servants--two women and a man.
cfrz10{_}3070{_}They were very good servants, but all three had been pronounced utterly intractable before they went to him.
cfrz10{_}3071{_}Master and mistress dared not speak to them; but with Dr. Turnbull they were suppressed as completely as if he had been Napoleon and they had been privates.
cfrz10{_}3072{_}He was kind to them, it is true, but at times very severe, and they could neither reply to him nor leave him.
cfrz10{_}3073{_}He did not affect the dress nor the manners of the doctors who preceded him.
cfrz10{_}3074{_}He wore a simple, black necktie, a shirt with no frill, and a black frock-coat.
cfrz10{_}3075{_}The poor worshipped him, as well they might, for his generosity to them was unexampled, and he took as much pains with them and was as kind to them as if they were the first people in Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}3076{_}He was perhaps even gentler with the poor than with the rich.
cfrz10{_}3077{_}He was very apt to be contemptuous, and to snarl when called to a rich man suffering from some trifling disorder who thought that his wealth justified a second opinion, but he watched the whole night through with the tenderness of a woman by the bedside of poor Phoebe Crowhurst when she had congestion of the lungs before she lived with Mrs. Furze.
cfrz10{_}3078{_}He saved that girl and would not take a sixpence, and when the mother, overcome with gratitude, actually fell on her knees before him and clung to him and sobbed and could not speak, he lifted her up with a "Nonsense, my good woman!"
cfrz10{_}3079{_}and quickly departed.
cfrz10{_}3080{_}He was a materialist, and described himself as one:  he disbelieved in what he called the soap-bubble theory, that somewhere in us there is something like a bubble, which controls everything, and is everything, and escapes invisible and gaseous to some other place after death.
cfrz10{_}3081{_}Consequently he never went to church.
cfrz10{_}3082{_}He was not openly combative, but Eastthorpe knew his heresies, and was taught to shudder at them.
cfrz10{_}3083{_}His professionally religious neighbours of course put him in hell in the future, but the common people did not go so far as that, although they could not believe him saved.
cfrz10{_}3084{_}They somehow confounded his denial of immortality with his own mortality, and imagined he would be at an end when he was put into the grave.
cfrz10{_}3085{_}As time wore on the attitude, even of the clergy, towards the doctor was gradually changed.
cfrz10{_}3086{_}They hastened to recognise him on week-days as he walked in his rapid, stately manner through the streets, although if they saw him on Sundays they considered it more becoming to avoid him.
cfrz10{_}3087{_}He was, as we have seen, a materialist, but yet he was the most spiritual person in the whole district.
cfrz10{_}3088{_}He took the keenest interest in science; he was generous, and a believer in a spiritualism infinitely beyond that of most of his neighbours, for they had not a single spiritual interest.
cfrz10{_}3089{_}He was spiritual in his treatment of disease.
cfrz10{_}3090{_}He was before his age by half a century, and instead of "throwing in" drugs after the fashion of Butcher, he prescribed fresh air, rest, and change, and, above everything, administered his own powerful individuality.
cfrz10{_}3091{_}He did not follow his friend Elliotson into mesmerism, but he had a mesmerism of his own, subduing all terror and sanative like light.
cfrz10{_}3092{_}Mr. Gosford was not capable of great expression, but he was always as expressive as he could be when he told the story of that dreadful illness.
cfrz10{_}3093{_}"He come into the room and ordered all the physic away, and then he sat down beside me, and it was just afore hay-harvest, and I was in mortal fright, and I said to him, 'Oh, doctor, I shall die.'
cfrz10{_}3094{_}Never shall I forget what I had gone through that night, for I'd done nothing but see the grave afore me, and I was lying in it a-rotting.
cfrz10{_}3095{_}Well, he took my hand, and he said, 'Why, for that matter, my friend, I must die too; but there's nothing in it; you won't complain when you find out what death is.
cfrz10{_}3096{_}You won't die yet, though, and you'll get this lot of hay in at any rate; what a heavy crop it is!'
cfrz10{_}3097{_}and he opened the winder and looked out.
cfrz10{_}3098{_}The way he spoke was wonderful, and what it was which come into me when he said, 'I MUST DIE TOO,' I don't know, but all my terrors went away, and I lay as calm as a child.
cfrz10{_}3099{_}'Fore God I did, as calm as a child, and I felt the wind upon me across the meadow while he stood looking at it, and I could almost have got up that minute.
cfrz10{_}3100{_}I warn't out of bed for a fortnight, but I did go out into the hayfield, as he said."
cfrz10{_}3101{_}Why did Dr. Turnbull come to Eastthorpe?
cfrz10{_}3102{_}Nobody ever knew while he lived.
cfrz10{_}3103{_}The question had been put at least some thousands of times, and all kinds of inquiries made, but with no result.
cfrz10{_}3104{_}The real reason, discovered afterwards, was simply that he had bad health, and that he had fled from temptation in the shape of a woman whom he loved, but whom duty, as he interpreted it, forbade him to marry, because he considered it wicked to run the risk of bringing diseased children into the world.
cfrz10{_}3105{_}This was the man to whom Catharine went.
cfrz10{_}3106{_}Mrs. Furze went with her.
cfrz10{_}3107{_}He was perfectly acquainted with Mrs. Furze, and had seen Catharine, but had never spoken to her.
cfrz10{_}3108{_}Mrs. Furze told her story, which was that Catharine had no appetite, and was wasting from no assignable cause.
cfrz10{_}3109{_}The doctor sounded her carefully, and then sat down without speaking.
cfrz10{_}3110{_}There was undoubtedly a weakness in one lung, but he was not satisfied.
cfrz10{_}3111{_}He knew how difficult it is to get people to tell the real truth to a physician, and that if a third person is present, it is impossible.
cfrz10{_}3112{_}He therefore asked Mrs. Furze if she would step into the next room.
cfrz10{_}3113{_}"A girl," he said, "will not say all she has to say even to her mother."
cfrz10{_}3114{_}Mrs. Furze did not quite like it, but obeyed.
cfrz10{_}3115{_}"Miss Furze," said the doctor, "I imagine you are a person who would not like to be deceived:  you have a slight tenderness in the chest; there is no reasonable cause for alarm, but you will have to be careful."
cfrz10{_}3116{_}Catharine's face lighted up a little when the last sentence was half finished, and the careful observer noticed it instantly.
cfrz10{_}3117{_}"That, however, is not the cause of your troubles:  there is something on your mind.
cfrz10{_}3118{_}I never make any inquiries in such cases, because I know if I did I should be met with evasions."
cfrz10{_}3119{_}Catharine's eyes were on the floor.
cfrz10{_}3120{_}After a long pause she said -  "I am wretched:  I have no pleasure in life; that is all I can say."
cfrz10{_}3121{_}"If there is no definite cause for it--mind, I say that --I may do something to relieve your distress.
cfrz10{_}3122{_}When people have no pleasure in living, and there is no concrete reason for it, they are out of health, and argument is of no avail.
cfrz10{_}3123{_}If a man does not find that food and light and the air are pleasant, it is of no use to debate with himself.
cfrz10{_}3124{_}Have you any friends at a distance?"
cfrz10{_}3125{_}"None."
cfrz10{_}3126{_}"What occupation have you?"
cfrz10{_}3127{_}"None."
cfrz10{_}3128{_}"It is not often that people are so miserable that they are unable to make others less miserable.
cfrz10{_}3129{_}If instead of thinking about yourself you were to think a little about those who are worse, if you would just consider that you have duties and attempt to do them, the effort might be a mere dead lift at first, but it would do you good, and you would find a little comfort in knowing at the end of the day that, although it had brought no delight to you, it had through you been made more tolerable to somebody.
cfrz10{_}3130{_}Disorders of the type with which you are afflicted are terribly selfish.
cfrz10{_}3131{_}Mind, I repeat it, I presuppose nothing but general depression.
cfrz10{_}3132{_}If it is more than that I can be of no use."
cfrz10{_}3133{_}Catharine was dumb, and Dr. Turnbull's singular power of winning confidence was of no avail to extract anything more from her.
cfrz10{_}3134{_}"I am sorry you cannot leave home.
cfrz10{_}3135{_}I shall give you no medicine.
cfrz10{_}3136{_}With regard to the chest, the single definite point, you know what precautions to take; as to the nervous trouble, do not discuss, ponder, or even directly attack, but turn the position, if I may so speak, by work and a determination to be of some use.
cfrz10{_}3137{_}If you were tempted by what you call wicked thoughts you would not nurse them.
cfrz10{_}3138{_}It is a great pity that people are so narrow in their notions of what wicked thoughts are.
cfrz10{_}3139{_}Every thought which maims you is wicked, horribly wicked, I call it.
cfrz10{_}3140{_}By the way, going to another subject, that poor girl, Phoebe Crowhurst, who lived at your house, is very ill again.
cfrz10{_}3141{_}She would like to see you.
cfrz10{_}3142{_}Catharine left, and Mrs. Furze came in.
cfrz10{_}3143{_}"Has anything unsettled your daughter lately?"
cfrz10{_}3144{_}"No, nothing particular."
cfrz10{_}3145{_}She thought of Tom, but to save Catharine's life she would not have acknowledged that it was possible for a Catchpole to have power to disturb a Furze.
cfrz10{_}3146{_}Had it been Mr. Colston now, the case would have been different.
cfrz10{_}3147{_}"She needs care, but there is nothing serious the matter with her.
cfrz10{_}3148{_}She ought to go away, but I understand she has no friends at a distance with whom she can stay.
cfrz10{_}3149{_}Give her a little wine."
cfrz10{_}3150{_}"Any medicine?"
cfrz10{_}3151{_}"No, none; I should like to see her again soon; good morning."
cfrz10{_}3152{_}Phoebe's home was near Abchurch, and Catharine went over to Abchurch to see her, not without remonstrance on the part of Mrs. Furze, Phoebe having been discharged in disgrace.
cfrz10{_}3153{_}Her father was an agricultural labourer, and lived in a little four-roomed, whitewashed cottage about a mile and a half out of the village.
cfrz10{_}3154{_}The living-room faced the north-east, the door opening direct on the little patch of garden, so that in winter, when the wind howled across the level fields, it was scarcely warmer indoors than outside, and rags and dish-clouts had to be laid on the door-sill to prevent the entrance of the snow and rain.
cfrz10{_}3155{_}At the back was a place, half outhouse, half kitchen, which had once had a brick floor, but the bricks had disappeared.
cfrz10{_}3156{_}Upstairs, over the living-room, was a bedroom, with no fireplace, and a very small casement window, where the mother and three children slept, the oldest a girl of about fourteen, the second a boy of twelve, and the third a girl of three or four, for the back bedroom over the outhouse had been given up to Phoebe since she was ill.
cfrz10{_}3157{_}The father slept below on the floor.
cfrz10{_}3158{_}Phoebe's room also had no fireplace, and great patches of plaster had been brought down by the rain on the south-west side.
cfrz10{_}3159{_}Just underneath the window was the pigstye.
cfrz10{_}3160{_}Outside nothing had been done to the house for years.
cfrz10{_}3161{_}It was not brick built, and here and there the laths and timber were bare, and the thatch had almost gone.
cfrz10{_}3162{_}Houses were very scarce on the farms in that part, and landlords would not build.
cfrz10{_}3163{_}The labourers consequently were driven into Abchurch, and had to walk, many of them, a couple of miles each way daily.
cfrz10{_}3164{_}Miss Diana Eaton, eldest daughter of the Honourable Mr. Eaton, had made a little sketch in water-colour of the cottage.
cfrz10{_}3165{_}It hung in the great drawing-room, and was considered most picturesque.
cfrz10{_}3166{_}"Lovely!
cfrz10{_}3167{_}What a dear old place!"
cfrz10{_}3168{_}said the guests.
cfrz10{_}3169{_}"It makes one quite enamoured of the country," exclaimed Lady Fanshawe, one of the most determined diners-out in Mayfair.
cfrz10{_}3170{_}"I never look at a scene like that without wishing I could give up London altogether.
cfrz10{_}3171{_}I am sure I could be content.
cfrz10{_}3172{_}It would be so charming to get rid of conventionality and be perfectly natural.
cfrz10{_}3173{_}You really ought to send that drawing to the Academy, Miss Eaton."
cfrz10{_}3174{_}That we should take pleasure in pictures of filthy, ruined hovels, in which health and even virtue are impossible, is a strange sign of the times.
cfrz10{_}3175{_}It is more than strange; it is an omen and a prophecy that people will go into sham ecstasies over one of these pigstyes so long as it is in a gilt frame; that they will give a thousand guineas for its light and shade--light, forsooth!--or for its Prout- like quality, or for its quality of this, that, and the other, while inside the real stye, at the very moment when the auctioneer knocks down the drawing amidst applause, lies the mother dying from dirt fever; the mother of six children starving and sleeping there-- starving, save for the parish allowance, for the snow is on the ground and the father is out of work.
cfrz10{_}3176{_}Crowhurst's wages were ten shillings a week, and the boy earned half a crown, but in the winter there was nothing to do for weeks together.
cfrz10{_}3177{_}All this, however, was accepted as the established order of things.
cfrz10{_}3178{_}It never entered into the heads of the Crowhursts to revolt.
cfrz10{_}3179{_}They did not revolt against the moon because she was sometimes full and lit everybody comfortably, and at other times was new and compelled the use of rushlights.
cfrz10{_}3180{_}It was so ordained.
cfrz10{_}3181{_}Half a mile beyond the cottage was a chapel.
cfrz10{_}3182{_}It stood at a cross- road, and no houses were near it.
cfrz10{_}3183{_}It had stood there for 150 years, gabled, red brick, and why it was put there nobody knew.
cfrz10{_}3184{_}Round it were tombstones, many totally disfigured, and most of them awry.
cfrz10{_}3185{_}The grass was always long and rank, full of dandelions, sorrel, and docks, excepting once a year in June when it was cut, and then it looked raw and yellow.
cfrz10{_}3186{_}Here and there was an unturfed, bare hillock, marking a new grave, and that was the only mark it would have, for people who could afford anything more did not attend the chapel now.
cfrz10{_}3187{_}The last "respectable family" was a farmer's hard by, but he and his wife had died, and his sons and daughters went to church.
cfrz10{_}3188{_}The congregation, such as it was, consisted nominally of about a dozen labourers and their wives and children, but no more than half of them came at any one time.
cfrz10{_}3189{_}The windows had painted wooden shutters, which were closed during the week to protect the glass from stone-throwing, and the rusty iron gate was always locked, save on Sundays.
cfrz10{_}3190{_}The gate, the door, and the shutters were unfastened just before the preacher came, and the horrible chapel smell and chapel damp hung about the place during the whole service.
cfrz10{_}3191{_}When there was a funeral of any one belonging to the congregation the Abchurch minister had to conduct it, and it was necessarily on Sunday, to his great annoyance.
cfrz10{_}3192{_}Nobody could be buried on any other day, because work could not be intermitted; no labourer could stay at home when wife or child was dying; he would have lost his wages, and perhaps his occupation.
cfrz10{_}3193{_}He thought himself lucky if they died in the night.
cfrz10{_}3194{_}The chapel was "supplied," as it was called, by an Abchurch deacon or Sunday-school teacher, who came over, prayed, preached, gave out hymns, and went away.
cfrz10{_}3195{_}That was nearly all that Cross Lanes knew of the "parent cause."
cfrz10{_}3196{_}The supplies were constantly being changed, and if it was very bad weather they stayed at home.
cfrz10{_}3197{_}On very rare occasions the Abchurch minister appeared on Sunday evenings in summer, but that was only when he wanted rest, and could deliver the Abchurch sermon of the morning, and could obtain a substitute at home.
cfrz10{_}3198{_}Crowhursts had been buried at Cross Lanes ever since it existed, but the present Crowhursts knew nothing of their ancestors beyond the generation immediately preceding.
cfrz10{_}3199{_}What was there to remember, or if there was anything worth remembering, why should they remember it?
cfrz10{_}3200{_}Life was blank, blind, dull as the brown clay in the sodden fields in November; nevertheless, the Light which lighteth every man that cometh into the world shone into the Crowhurst cottage--that Light greater than all lights which can be lit by priest or philosopher, as the sun is greater than all our oil-lamps, gas, and candles.
cfrz10{_}3201{_}When Phoebe first had congestion of the lungs, not a single note of murmuring at the trouble caused escaped a soul in the household.
cfrz10{_}3202{_}The mother sat up with her at night, and a poor woman half a mile off came in during the day and saw that things went all straight.
cfrz10{_}3203{_}To be sure, there was Dr. Turnbull.
cfrz10{_}3204{_}It was a long way out of his rounds, but he knew the Crowhursts well, and, as we have said, he watched over Phoebe as carefully as if she had been the daughter of a duke.
cfrz10{_}3205{_}Now Phoebe was ill again, but Dr. Turnbull was again there, and although her cough was incessant, the care of father, mother, brother, and sister was perfect in its tenderness, and their self- forgetfulness was complete.
cfrz10{_}3206{_}It was not with them as with a man known to the writer of this history.
cfrz10{_}3207{_}His wife, whom he professed to love, was dying of consumption.
cfrz10{_}3208{_}"I do not deny she suffers," he said "but nobody thinks of ME."
cfrz10{_}3209{_}The sympathy of the agricultural poor with one another is hardly credible to fine people who live in towns.
cfrz10{_}3210{_}If we could have a record of the devotion of those women who lie forgotten under the turf round country churches throughout England, it would be better worth preserving than nine-tenths of our literature and histories.
cfrz10{_}3211{_}Surely in some sense they still ARE, and their love cannot have been altogether a thing of no moment to the Power that made them!
cfrz10{_}3212{_}Catharine had never been to Phoebe's home before.
cfrz10{_}3213{_}At the Terrace she was smart, attractive, and as particular as her mistress about her clothes.
cfrz10{_}3214{_}Nobody ever saw Phoebe with untidy shoes or stockings, and even in the morning, before she was supposed to be dressed, her little feet were as neat as if she had nothing to do but to sit in a drawing-room.
cfrz10{_}3215{_}She was now lying on a stump bedstead with a patchwork coverlet over her, and to protect her from the draughts an old piece of carpet had been nailed on a kind of rough frame and placed between her and the door.
cfrz10{_}3216{_}Catharine's first emotion when she entered was astonishment and indignation.
cfrz10{_}3217{_}Therein she showed her ignorance and stupidity.
cfrz10{_}3218{_}The owner of the cottage did not force the Crowhursts to live in it.
cfrz10{_}3219{_}It was not he who directed that a girl dying of consumption should lie close to a damp wall in a room eight feet square with no ventilation.
cfrz10{_}3220{_}He had the cottage, the Crowhursts, presumably, were glad to get it, and he conferred a favour on them.
cfrz10{_}3221{_}"Oh, Miss Catharine," said Phoebe, "this is kind of you!
cfrz10{_}3222{_}To think of your coming over from Eastthorpe to see me, and after what happened between me and Mrs. Furze!
cfrz10{_}3223{_}Miss Catharine, I didn't mean to be rude, but that Orkid Jim is a liar, and it's my belief that he's at the bottom of the mischief with Tom.
cfrz10{_}3224{_}You haven't heard of Tom, I suppose, Miss?"
cfrz10{_}3225{_}"Yes, he is in London.
cfrz10{_}3226{_}He is doing very well."
cfrz10{_}3227{_}"Oh, I am very thankful.
cfrz10{_}3228{_}I am afraid you will find the room very close, Miss.
cfrz10{_}3229{_}Don't stay if you are uncomfortable."
cfrz10{_}3230{_}Catharine replied by taking a chair and sitting by the bedside.
cfrz10{_}3231{_}There was somewhat in Phoebe's countenance, Catharine knew not what, but it went to her heart, and she bent down and kissed her upon the forehead.
cfrz10{_}3232{_}They had always been half-friends when Phoebe was at the Terrace.
cfrz10{_}3233{_}The poor girl's eyes filled with tears, and a smile came over her face like the sunshine following the shadow of a cloud sweeping over the hillside.
cfrz10{_}3234{_}Mrs. Crowhurst came into the room.
cfrz10{_}3235{_}"Why, mother, what are you doing here?
cfrz10{_}3236{_}You ought to be abed.
cfrz10{_}3237{_}Where is Mrs.
cfrz10{_}3238{_}Dunsfold?"
cfrz10{_}3239{_}"Mrs. Dunsfold is laid up with the rheumatics, my dear.
cfrz10{_}3240{_}But don't you bother; we can manage very well.
cfrz10{_}3241{_}I will stay with you at night, and just have a bit of sleep in the mornings.
cfrz10{_}3242{_}Your sister can manage after I've seen to father's breakfast and while I'm a-lying down, and if she wants me, she's only got to call."
cfrz10{_}3243{_}The mother looked worn and anxious, as though, even with Mrs. Dunsfold's assistance, her rest had been insufficient.

cfrz10{_}3249{_}I'm sure you shan't," replied Mrs. Crowhurst; but Catharine persisted, and a message was sent by Phoebe's brother, who, although so young, knew the way perfectly well, and could be trusted.
cfrz10{_}3250{_}The evening and the darkness drew on, and everything gradually became silent.
cfrz10{_}3251{_}Excepting Phoebe's cough, not a sound could be heard save the distant bark of some farmyard dog.
cfrz10{_}3252{_}As the air outside was soft and warm, Catharine opened the window, after carefully protecting her patient.
cfrz10{_}3253{_}Phoebe was restless.
cfrz10{_}3254{_}"Shall I read to you?"
cfrz10{_}3255{_}"Oh, please, Miss; but there is nothing here for you to read but the Bible and a hymn-book."
cfrz10{_}3256{_}"Well, I will read the Bible.
cfrz10{_}3257{_}What would you like?"
cfrz10{_}3258{_}Phoebe chose neither prophecy, psalm, nor epistle, but the last three chapters of St. Matthew.
cfrz10{_}3259{_}She, perhaps, hardly knew the reason why, but she could not have made a better choice.
cfrz10{_}3260{_}When we come near death, or near something which may be worse, all exhortation, theory, promise, advice, dogma fail.
cfrz10{_}3261{_}The one staff which, perhaps, may not break under us, is the victory achieved in the like situation by one who has preceded us; and the most desperate private experience cannot go beyond the garden of Gethsemane.
cfrz10{_}3262{_}The hero is a young man filled with dreams and an ideal of a heavenly kingdom which he was to establish on earth.
cfrz10{_}3263{_}He is disappointed by the time he is thirty.
cfrz10{_}3264{_}He has not a friend who understands him, save in so far as the love of two or three poor women is understanding.
cfrz10{_}3265{_}One of his disciples denies him, another betrays him, and in the presence of the hard Roman tribunal all his visions are nothing, and his life is a failure.
cfrz10{_}3266{_}He is to die a cruel death; but the bitterness of the cup must have been the thought that in a few days--or at least in a few months or years--everything would be as if he had never been.
cfrz10{_}3267{_}This is the pang of death, even to the meanest.
cfrz10{_}3268{_}"He that goeth down to the grave," says Job, "shall return no more to his house, neither shall his place know him any more."
cfrz10{_}3269{_}A higher philosophy would doubtless set no store on our poor personality, and would even rejoice in the thought of its obliteration or absorption, but we cannot always lift ourselves to that level, and the human sentiment remains.
cfrz10{_}3270{_}Catharine read through the story of the conflict, and when she came to the resurrection she felt, and Phoebe felt, after her fashion, as millions have felt before, that this was the truth of death.
cfrz10{_}3271{_}It may be a legend, but the belief in it has carried with it other beliefs which are vital.
cfrz10{_}3272{_}The reading ceased, and Phoebe fell asleep for a little.
cfrz10{_}3273{_}She presently waked and called Catharine.
cfrz10{_}3274{_}"Miss Catharine," she whispered, drawing Catharine's hand between both her own thin hands, "I have something to say to you.
cfrz10{_}3275{_}Do you know I loved Tom a little; but I don't think he loved me.
cfrz10{_}3276{_}His mind was elsewhere; I--saw where it was, and I don't wonder.
cfrz10{_}3277{_}I makes no difference, and never has, in my thoughts,--either of him or of you.
cfrz10{_}3278{_}It will be better for him in every way, and I am glad for his sake.
cfrz10{_}3279{_}But when I am gone and I shan't feel ashamed at his knowing it-- please give him my Bible; and you may, if you like, put a piece of my hair in that last chapter you have been reading to-night."
cfrz10{_}3280{_}"Phoebe, my Phoebe, listen," said Catharine:  "I shall never be Tom's wife."
cfrz10{_}3281{_}"Are you sure?"
cfrz10{_}3282{_}"As sure as that I am here with my head on your pillow."
cfrz10{_}3283{_}"I am sorry."
cfrz10{_}3284{_}She then became silent, and so continued for two hours.
cfrz10{_}3285{_}Catharine thought she was asleep, but a little after dawn her mother came into the room.
cfrz10{_}3286{_}She knew better, and saw that the silence was not sleep, but the insensibility of death.
cfrz10{_}3287{_}In a few minutes she hurried Catharine downstairs, and when she was again admitted Phoebe lay dead, and her pale face, unutterably peaceful and serious, was bound up with a white neckerchief.
cfrz10{_}3288{_}The soul of the poor servant girl had passed away--only a servant girl--and yet there was something in that soul equal to the sun whose morning rays were pouring through the window.
cfrz10{_}3289{_}She lies at the back of the meeting-house amongst her kindred, and a little mound was raised over her.
cfrz10{_}3290{_}Her father borrowed the key of the gate every now and then, and, after his work was over, cut the grass where his child lay, and prevented the weeds from encroaching; but when he died, not long after, his wife had to go into the workhouse, and in one season the sorrel and dandelions took possession, and Phoebe's grave became like all the others--a scarcely distinguishable undulation in the tall, rank herbage.
cfrz10{_}3291{_}CHAPTER XIX    Catharine left the cottage that afternoon, and began to walk home to Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}3292{_}She thought, as she went along, of Phoebe's confession.
cfrz10{_}3293{_}She had loved Tom, but had reached the point of perfect acquiescence in any award of destiny, provided only he could be happy.
cfrz10{_}3294{_}She had faced sickness and death without a murmur; she had no theory of duty, no philosophy, no religion, as it is usually called, save a few dim traditional beliefs, and she was the daughter of common peasants; but she had attained just the one thing essential which religion and philosophy ought to help us to obtain, and, if they do not help us to obtain it, they are nothing.
cfrz10{_}3295{_}She lived not for herself, nor in herself, and it was not even justice to herself which she demanded.
cfrz10{_}3296{_}She had not become what she was because death was before her.
cfrz10{_}3297{_}Death and the prospect of death do not work any change.
cfrz10{_}3298{_}Catharine called to mind Phoebe's past life; it was all of a piece, and countless little incidents unnoticed at the time obtained a significance and were interpreted.
cfrz10{_}3299{_}She knew herself to be Phoebe's superior intellectually, and that much had been presented to her which was altogether over Phoebe's horizon.
cfrz10{_}3300{_}But in all her purposes, and in all her activity, she seemed to have had self for a centre, and she felt that she would gladly give up every single advantage she possessed if she could but depose that self and enthrone some other divinity in its place.
cfrz10{_}3301{_}Oh the bliss of waking up in the morning with the thoughts turned outwards instead of inwards!
cfrz10{_}3302{_}Her misery which so weighed upon her might perhaps depart if she could achieve that conquest.
cfrz10{_}3303{_}She remembered one of Mr. Cardew's first sermons, when she was at Miss Ponsonby's, the sermon of which we have heard something, and she cried to herself, "Who shall deliver me from the body of this death!"
cfrz10{_}3304{_}Strange, but true, precisely at that moment the passion for Mr. Cardew revived with more than its old intensity.
cfrz10{_}3305{_}Fresh from a deathbed, pondering over what she had learned or thought she had learned there--the very lesson which ought to have taught her to give up Mr. Cardew--she loved him more than ever, and was less than ever able to banish his image from her.
cfrz10{_}3306{_}She turned out of her direct road and took that which led past his house--swept that way as irresistibly as a mastless hull is swept by the tide.
cfrz10{_}3307{_}She knew that Mr. Cardew was in the habit of walking out in the afternoon, and she knew the path he usually took.
cfrz10{_}3308{_}She had not gone far before she met him.
cfrz10{_}3309{_}She explained what her errand had been, and added that she preferred the bypath because she was able to avoid the dusty Eastthorpe lane.
cfrz10{_}3310{_}"I do not know these Crowhursts," said Mr. Cardew; "they are Dissenters, I believe."
cfrz10{_}3311{_}The subject dropped, and Catharine had not another word to say about Phoebe.
cfrz10{_}3312{_}"You look fatigued and as if you were not very well."
cfrz10{_}3313{_}"Nothing particular; a little cough at times, but the doctor says it is of no consequence, if I only take care."
cfrz10{_}3314{_}"You have been up all night, and you are now going to walk back to Eastthorpe?"
cfrz10{_}3315{_}"Yes, the walk will refresh me."
cfrz10{_}3316{_}He did not ask her to go to his house.
cfrz10{_}3317{_}Catharine noticed the omission; hoped he would not--knew he would not.
cfrz10{_}3318{_}"Have you heard anything of your father's assistant, Mr.
cfrz10{_}3319{_}Catchpole?"

cfrz10{_}3321{_}"Is he quite happy?"
cfrz10{_}3322{_}"Yes, I believe so."
cfrz10{_}3323{_}"I encountered Mr. Colston, junior, a few minutes ago.
cfrz10{_}3324{_}He was on his way to Eastthorpe.
cfrz10{_}3325{_}I am afraid I was rather rude to him, for, to tell you the truth, I did not want his society.

cfrz10{_}3333{_}"Unattainable!
cfrz10{_}3334{_}Yes, just what I imagined:  you paint something unattainable to ordinary mortality.
cfrz10{_}3335{_}It is strange that most men and women, even those who more or less in all they do strive after perfection, seem to be satisfied with so little when it comes to love and marriage.
cfrz10{_}3336{_}The same sculptor, who unweariedly refines day after day to put in marble the image which haunts him, forms no such image of a woman whom he seeks unceasingly, or, if he does, he descends on one of the first twenty he meets and thinks he adores her.
cfrz10{_}3337{_}There is some strong thwarting power which prevents his search after the best, and it is as if nature had said that we should not pick and choose.
cfrz10{_}3338{_}But the consequences are tremendous.
cfrz10{_}3339{_}I honour you for your aspirations."

cfrz10{_}3358{_}"I believe I saw Mr. Cardew in the meadow:  I have just called on his wife."

cfrz10{_}3360{_}"Yes, not seriously, I hope.
cfrz10{_}3361{_}You know Mr.
cfrz10{_}3362{_}Cardew?"
cfrz10{_}3363{_}"Yes, a little.
cfrz10{_}3364{_}I have heard him preach, and have been to his house when I was living at Abchurch."
cfrz10{_}3365{_}"A remarkable man in many ways, and yet not a man whom I much admire.
cfrz10{_}3366{_}He thinks a good deal, and when I am in company with him I am unaccountably stimulated, but his thinking is not directed upon life.
cfrz10{_}3367{_}My notion is that our intellect is intended to solve real difficulties which confront us, and that all intellectual exercise upon what does not concern us is worse than foolish.
cfrz10{_}3368{_}My brain finds quite enough to do in contriving how to remove actual hard obstacles which lie in the way of other people's happiness and my own."

cfrz10{_}3372{_}He is not like any one of those persons, and he never will be in any one of those situations.
cfrz10{_}3373{_}The situation in which he found himself that morning at home, or that in which a poor neighbour found himself, is that which to him is important.
cfrz10{_}3374{_}It is a pernicious consequence of the sole study of extraordinary people that the customary standards of human action are deposed, and other standards peculiar to peculiar creatures under peculiar circumstances are set up.
cfrz10{_}3375{_}I have known Cardew do very curious things at times.
cfrz10{_}3376{_}I do not believe for one moment he thought he was doing wrong, but nevertheless, if any other man had done them, I should have had nothing more to say to him."

cfrz10{_}3378{_}He may not be constituted as we are."

cfrz10{_}3380{_}Nothing is more dangerous, physically and mentally, than to imagine we are not as other people.
cfrz10{_}3381{_}Strive to consider yourself, not as Catharine Furze, a young woman apart, but as a piece of common humanity and bound by its laws.
cfrz10{_}3382{_}It is infinitely healthier for you.
cfrz10{_}3383{_}Never, under any pretext whatever, allow yourself to do what is exceptional.
cfrz10{_}3384{_}If you have any originality, it will better come out in an improved performance of what everybody ought to do, than in the indulgence in singularity.
cfrz10{_}3385{_}For one person who, being a person of genius, has been injured by what is called conventionality--I do not, of course, mean foolish conformity to what is absurd--thousands have been saved by it, and self-separation means mischief.
cfrz10{_}3386{_}It has been the beginning even of insanity in many cases which have come under my notice."
cfrz10{_}3387{_}The doctor paused a little.
cfrz10{_}3388{_}"I am glad Mrs. Cardew is better," said Catharine.
cfrz10{_}3389{_}"I did not know she had been ill."  "There is a woman for you--a really wonderful woman, unobtrusive, devoted to her husband, almost annihilating herself for him, and, what is very noteworthy, she denies herself in studies to which she is much attached, and for which she has a remarkable capacity, merely in order that she may the better sympathise with him.
cfrz10{_}3390{_}Then her care of the poor in his parish makes her almost a divinity to them.

cfrz10{_}3397{_}"Ought?
cfrz10{_}3398{_}Do you think, Dr. Turnbull, a man ought to love what he cannot love?"
cfrz10{_}3399{_}"Yes, but I must explain myself.
cfrz10{_}3400{_}I have no patience with people who seem to consider that they may yield themselves to something they know not what, and allow themselves to be swayed by it.
cfrz10{_}3401{_}A man marries a woman whom he loves.
cfrz10{_}3402{_}Is it possible that she, of all women in the world, is the one he would love best if he were to know all of them?
cfrz10{_}3403{_}Is it likely that he would have selected this one woman if he had seen, say, fifty more before he had married her?
cfrz10{_}3404{_}Certainly not; and when he sees other women afterwards, better than the one he has chosen, he naturally admires them.
cfrz10{_}3405{_}If he does not-- he is a fool, but he is bound to check himself.
cfrz10{_}3406{_}He puts them aside and is obliged to be satisfied with his wife.
cfrz10{_}3407{_}If it were permissible in him in such a case to abandon her, a pretty chaos we should be in.
cfrz10{_}3408{_}It is clearly his duty, and quite as clearly in his power, to be thus contented--at least, in nine cases out of ten.
cfrz10{_}3409{_}He MAY--and this is my point--he MAY wilfully turn away from what is admirable in his own house, or he may turn towards it.
cfrz10{_}3410{_}He is as responsible for turning away from it, or turning towards it, as he is for any of his actions.
cfrz10{_}3411{_}If he says he cannot love a wife who is virtuous and good, I call him not only stupid, but wicked--yes, wicked:  people in Eastthorpe will tell you I do not know what that word means, because I do not go to church, and do not believe in what they do not believe themselves, but still I say wicked--wicked because he CAN love his wife, just as he can refrain from robbing his neighbour, and wicked because there is a bit of excellence stuck down before him for HIM to value.
cfrz10{_}3412{_}It is not intended for others, but for HIM, and he deserts the place appointed him by Nature if he neglects it."
cfrz10{_}3413{_}"You have wonderful self-control, Dr. Turnbull.

cfrz10{_}3418{_}You seem to be fond of an argument.
cfrz10{_}3419{_}Some day we will debate the point how far the proper appreciation even of a picture or a melody is within our own power.

cfrz10{_}3422{_}"Better."
cfrz10{_}3423{_}"That is good, but you must be careful, especially in the evening.

cfrz10{_}3426{_}He drove her home, and she went upstairs to lie down.
cfrz10{_}3427{_}"Commonplace rubbish!"
cfrz10{_}3428{_}she said to herself; "what I used to hear at Miss Ponsonby's, but dressed up a little better, the moral prosing of an old man of sixty who never knew what it was to have his pulse stirred; utterly incapable of understanding Mr. Cardew, one of whose ideas moves me more than volumes of Turnbull copybook."
cfrz10{_}3429{_}Pulse stirred!
cfrz10{_}3430{_}The young are often unjust to the old in the matter of pulsation, and the world in general is unjust to those who prefer to be silent, or to whom silence is a duty.
cfrz10{_}3431{_}Dr. Turnbull's pulse was unmistakably stirred on a certain morning thirty years ago, when he crept past a certain door in Bloomsbury Square very early.
cfrz10{_}3432{_}The blinds were still all drawn down, but he lingered and walked past the house two or three times.

cfrz10{_}3435{_}How he watched for that birth!

cfrz10{_}3439{_}With that dreadful capacity which some people possess, for the realisation of that which is not present, the parting with Mr. Cardew came before Catharine as she shut her eyes on her pillow: the arm was behind her--she actually felt it; his eyes were on hers; she was on fire, and once more, as she had done before, she cursed herself for what she almost called her cowardice in leaving him.

cfrz10{_}3444{_}She was young.

cfrz10{_}3446{_}WE MUST LEARN TO BEAR OUR TROUBLES PATIENTLY, says the copper-plate line for small text, and the revolving years bring nothing more.

cfrz10{_}3448{_}She opened it, and found Alice, her retriever.
cfrz10{_}3449{_}Alice came in, sat down by the chair, and put her head on her mistress's lap, looking up to her with large, brown, affectionate eyes which spoke almost.
cfrz10{_}3450{_}There is something very touching in the love of a dog.
cfrz10{_}3451{_}It is independent of all our misfortunes, mistakes, and sins.
cfrz10{_}3452{_}It may not be of much account, but it is constant, and it is a love for ME, and does not desert me for anything accidental, not even if I am a criminal.

cfrz10{_}3482{_}CHAPTER XX    Mrs. Cardew recovered, but Dr. Turnbull recommended that as soon as she could be moved she should have an entire change, and at the end of the autumn she and her husband went abroad.
cfrz10{_}3483{_}That winter was a bad winter for Mr. Furze.
cfrz10{_}3484{_}The harvest had been the worst known for years:  farmers had no money; his expenses had increased; many of his customers had left him, and Catharine's cough had become so much worse that, except on fine days, she was not allowed to go out of doors.
cfrz10{_}3485{_}For the first time in his life he was obliged to overdraw his account at the bank, and when his wife questioned him about his troubles he became angry and vicious.
cfrz10{_}3486{_}One afternoon he had a visit from one of the partners in the bank, who politely informed him that no further advances could be made.
cfrz10{_}3487{_}It was near Christmas, and it was Mr. Furze's practice at Christmas to take stock.
cfrz10{_}3488{_}He set to work, and his balance-sheet showed that he was a poorer man by three hundred pounds than he was a twelvemonth before.
cfrz10{_}3489{_}Catharine did not see him on the night on which he made this discovery.
cfrz10{_}3490{_}He came home very late, and she had gone to bed.

cfrz10{_}3492{_}It was not so much the actual loss which upset him as the old incapacity of dealing with the unusual.
cfrz10{_}3493{_}Oh, for one hour with Tom!
cfrz10{_}3494{_}What should he do?
cfrz10{_}3495{_}Should he retrench?
cfrz10{_}3496{_}Should he leave the Terrace?
cfrz10{_}3497{_}Should he try and borrow money?
cfrz10{_}3498{_}A dizzy whirl of a dozen projects swung round and round in his brain, and he could resolve on nothing.
cfrz10{_}3499{_}He pictured most vividly and imagined most vividly the consequences of bankruptcy.
cfrz10{_}3500{_}His intellectual activity in that direction was amazing, and if one-tenth part of it could have been expended on the consideration of the next best thing to be done, not only would he have discovered what the next best thing was, but the dreadful energy of his imagination would have been enfeebled.
cfrz10{_}3501{_}He was sitting at his desk at the back of the shop with his head propped on his elbows, when he heard a soft footstep behind him.
cfrz10{_}3502{_}He turned round: it was Catharine.

cfrz10{_}3507{_}Catharine was not greatly surprised.
